THE RED PAGE.
Olive Schreiner’s New Book.
‘ Trooper Peter Halket of Mashonaland is a
Tract—a South African Tract. A Tract is a
thing which, like the moon, shows you only one
side : which places natural law on a Procrustean
bed of artificial theories—lopping it here, stretch-
ing it there, suppressing, and adding, and em-
bellishing until the Thing That Is is made to look
like the Thing That Never Was, and earth and
sea, sun and stars, gods and men, the whole con-
ceivable universe, are demeaned and distorted in
the reductio ad' dbsurdurn of one puny intelli-
gence. Taking him by and large, Man is a slow
old clock, whose morality-hands move very tardily
indeed round the vast dial of Time. So the Tract
just anticipates a little, and when it is ten o’clock
in the morality morning it shoves the hands round
to half-past four o’clock in the afternoon—or what
it imagines is half-past four o’clock in the after-
noon—which doesn’t help things any, and is apt
to spoil the temper of the clock. In a dozen
words, the Tract is admeasurement of the infi-
nitely great by the standard of the infinitely little.
In one word, the Tract is a Lie.
* » * *
Follows that, considered as Literature or Art,
Olive Sehreiner’s tract is a failure. It fails
because, however true in fact or intention, it is
false' in method and results. And the author’s
dominant sex, which has been balancing for the
last fifteen years, now asserts itself as female. Her
literary vitality is waning, her literary hysteria in-
creasing. The intellectual plane of her new book is
but little above that of ‘ Marie Corelli ’ —to whose
envenomed jealousy Olive Schreiner has hitherto
risen easily superior. But one machine-god is no
better than another, and all are fatal to truth—
that is, to Art. Olive Schreiner uses a god to
attack men and ideas she does not like, and puts
Words into his mouth to suit herself. ‘Marie
Corelli ’ uses a devil to attack men and ideas she
does not like, and puts words into his mouth to
suit herself. The method is the same ; only Olive
Schreiner’s ideas are better, and, under the cir-
cumstances, there is more sense in denouncing
Cecil Rhodes than in denouncing black-hearted
critics. But with just as much reason and just as
much artistic effect gods and devils could be used
to attack Olive Schreiner and ‘ Marie Corelli.'
* * * *
: tAs an ad captandum argument, as a shriek to
the Nonconformist Conscience of Great Britain,
Olive Schreiner’s tract may have a striking suc-
cess. One ponders with interest how the Rhodes-
cajoled Stead will like being hoist with his own
petard, and proved by Scripture (not to mention
facts) to be the accomplice of robbers and mur-
derers. For ‘ Peter Halket ’ is a very skilful
appeal to the low religious order of mind which
swamps the British community. And it has right
on its side, and justice, and much actual truth—
and a god of the most approved pattern to ex-
pound them in simple language which can hardly
fail to drive conviction home. Really, the Non-
conformist Conscience ought to squirm to some
purpose. But such an appeal was unworthy of
the Olive Schreiner who wrote ‘ The Story of an
African Farm.’
4
When the story opens Peter is sitting all alone
beside his fire, and not feeling very well. Facb is,
Peter is lost, and has had no tucker since morn-
ing. He has a flask of brandy, but it is only a
small flask, and bad brandj r . And the longer he
sits the less well Peter feels.
* » » »
Peter is a young man—under 21; with pale
blue eyes, slanting forehead, a weedy carcase, and
no intellectuals worth mentioning—a good poten-
tial Salvation Army captain, in fact. His busi-
ness is to hunt niggers in Mashonaland, and his
employers are the Chartered Company, alias
Rhodes and Co. The niggers have been
hunted for a considerable area around lonely
Peter, and just at present he is engaged with a
dcWen others carrying provisions to one of the
troopers’ camps on ahead. The Mashonas’ kraals
have been burnt, the male occupants more or less
slaughtered, female ditto ‘dealt with,’ and there
is not much fear of attack. Nevertheless Peter
was sent out this morning to act as scout —and
Peter got lost, as weedy young fellows with slant-
ing foreheads occasionally will. But he knows
his mates will come and look for him in the
morning, so he has made a fire, and sits over it in
a sad, hollow fashion, thinking of home- and
mother, and taking a nip of brandy at intervals.
* * * *
Now, what follows seems meant as the natural
sort of bad dream resulting from inferior brandy
on an empty stomach. Peter is a visionary kind
of cuss, and between the brandy and the empty
stomach and the thoughts of home and mother
and the weak intellectuals he evolves a beautiful
trance, and his mates have a job to wake
him when they find him in the morning.
And here is where the author’s art fails : for the
incidents of the trance are represented with all
the detail and definition of reality—not only to
Peter, but to the reader ; and, moreover, incidents,
thoughts and language are often beyond the range
of Peter’s knowledge, and such, therefore, as he
could not possibly have dreamt, no matter how
bad the brandy. For dreams cannot transcend
the dreamer: the unconscious brain cannot
originate ideas of which it has not previously
acquired the material. Thus, many 12th century
Europeans dreamt about the Crusades, but none
ever dreamt about kangaroos. And even the
* Published by Fisher Unwin, London ; advance copy
from Angus and Robertson, Sydney. Forwarded from
Bullbun office on arrival in a few weeks; price, 3s.
paper; 4s. cloth ; post-free.
hypnotist must build on a basis of previous know-
ledge. He can say to his subject: ‘ At ten o'clock
to-morrow morning you will write a letter to
So-and-so, asking him to call here ’ —and a good
subject will do it. But not if se has never learnt
to write.
* • * m
Peter Halket—his second name is Simon —is
apparently modelled on the lines of the old
original Simon Peter who Jives in the Acts of
the Apostles. This Peter also ‘ fell into a
trance ’ on an empty stomach. . He used to board
at a place in Joppa, and one day when
his soul abhorred hash rather more than
usual he got up from the table and went on
top of the house and fell asleep in disgust—while
the landlady remarked to the servant-girl on * the
airs these fifteen-drachma boarders do give them-
selves—and him with only one decent gaberdine
to his name, and if he doesn’t pay this week I’ll
pack him off as sure as my name’s Mary
Moses.’ But Peter on the housetop was having
a delicious dream. He thought he saw a big sheet
coming down from the sky, full of all kinds of
toothsome beasts and birds just jumping to be
cut up and thrust on a gridiron. And Peter
thought he heard a voice telling him to get up
slick and have quail on toast; and Peter didn’t,
so the big sheet was hauled up again. But pre-
sently down it came anew, just as full and just as
tempting, and the voice recommended Peter to
awake and arise and try some haunch of venison
and black currant jelly—and Peter wouldn’t. So
the sheet was hauled up a second time. But Peter
dreamt he looked so hungry and miserable that
down the sheet catne once more, and the voice
urged Peter to be a man and give his un-
biassed opinion about the lobster curry, any-
way— and Peter wasn’t game. So it was
‘Going, going, gone,’ and ‘the third time i.s
final,’ and Peter was given best as a bad
job, and the sheet was drawn up and stayed
up. And then plates rattled in the kitchen down
below, and Peter woke with his mouth water-
ing and his nose smelling the last odors of the
hash wafted from the washing-up.
* * * *
That is the old .original Simon Peter’s story of
his vision, and there is truth in every line of it.
The first thing a hungry man dreams of is a good
dinner; and the sheet, and the venison, and the
lobster—these were all within Peter’s previous
knowledge. But the new and spurious Simon
Peter [Olive Schreiner’s Peter Simon, with the
name just a little altered so as to avoid an action
for colorable imitation of trade-marks] doesn’t
think, in his trance, of the things he would like
to eat, or the things he knows all about. He
thinks of but we shall see as we go along.
* * * *
Mind, there is no direct statement that it is a
trance. The reality of the hollow Peter slides
imperceptibly into the dream.
From the darkness at the edge of the koppje a figure
stepped out into the full blaze of the fire-light.
Trooper Peter Halket looked up at it.
It was the tall figure of a man, olad in one loose linen
garment, reaching lower than his knees, and which clung
close about him. His head, arms, and feet were bare.
He carried no weapon of any kind ; and on his shoulders
hung heavy locks of dark hair.
Peter Halket looked up at him with astonishment.
“ Are you alone? ” he asked.
“ Yes, I am alone.”
Peter Halket lowered his gun and knelt up.
“Lost your way, I suppose?” be said, still holding his
weapon loosely.
“ No; I have come to ask whether I may sit beside
your fire for a while.”
“Certainly, certainly!” said Peter, eyeing the
stranger’s dress carefully, still holding his gun, but with
the hand off the lock. “I’m confoundedly glad of any
company. It’s a beastly night for anybody to be out
alone. Wonder you find your way. Sit down! sit
down 1 ” Peter looked intently at the stranger ; then he
put his gun down at his side.
The stranger sat down on the opposite side of the fire.
His complexion was dark ; his arms and feet were
bronzed; but his aquiline features and the domed fore-
head were not of any South African race.
* * * *
The hospitable Peter asks the stranger to have
a drink—
He took the small flask from his pocket and held it out.
“ I’m sorry there’s so little, but a drop will keep the cold
out.”
The stranger bowed his head ; but thanked and declined.
(Seems ‘ Cape smoke ’ isn’t ‘ class enough ’ for
celestial palates.)
Peter raised the flask to his lips and took a small
draught; then returned it to his pocket. The stranger
folded bis arms about his knees, and looked into the fire.
“ Are you a Jew ? ” asked Peter, suddenly ; as the fire
fell full on the stranger's face.
“ Yes ; I am a Jew.”
“Ah,” said Peter, “ that’s why I wasn’t able to make
out at first what nation you could be of ; your dress, you
know ” Then he stopped and said, “ Trading here, I
suppose ? Which country do you come from ; are you a
Spanish Jew ? ”
“ I am a Jew of Palestine.”
“Ah!” said Peter; “ I haven’t seen many from that
part yet. I came out with a lot on board ship ; and I’ve
seen Barnato and Beit; but they’re not very much like
you. I suppose it’s coming from Palestine makes the
difference.”
» * * •
Then the hospitable Peter regrets the absence
of tucker—
“ I’m sorry I haven’t anything to eat to offer you ; but
I haven’t had anything myself since last night. It’s
beastly sickening, being out like this with nothing to eat.
Wouldn’t have thought a fellow’d feel so bad after only
a day of it. Have you ever been out without grubf”
said Peter cheerfully, warming his hands at the blaze.
“Forty days and nights,” said the stranger.
“ Forty days 1 Phe—e—w ? ” said Peter. “ You must
have had a lot to drink, or you wouldn’t have stood it.”
* » * *
Then the hospitable Peter makes conversation—
about women, of course —and recounts some
African experiences in the wake of the Chartered
Co.
“ It wasn’t such a bad life, though. I only wish I was
back there again. I had two huts to myself, and a couple
of nigger girls. It’s better fun,” said Peter, after a
while, “ having these black women than whites. The
whites you’ve got to support, but the niggers support
you! And when you’ve done with them you can just
get rid of them. I’m all for the nigger gals.” Peter
laughed. But the stranger sat motionless with his arms
about his knees.
“You got any girls?” said Peter. “ Care for niggers ? ”
.“I love all women,” said the stranger, refolding his
arms about his knees.
“Oh, you do, do you?” said Peter. “Well, I’m pretty
sick of them. I had bother enough with mine,” he said
genially, warming his hands by the fire, and then inter-
locking the fingers and turning the palms towards the
blaze as one who prepares to enjoy a good talk. “One
girl was only fifteen ; I got her cheap from a policeman
who was living with her, and she wasn’t much. But the
other, by Gad ! I never saw another nigger like her;
well set up, I tell you, and as straight as that— " said
Peter, holding up his finger in the firelight. “She was
thirty if she was a day. Fellows don’t generally fancy
women that age ; they like slips of girls. But I set my
heart on her the day I saw her.”
* • * *
And Peter relates how one day he came home
and found his best girl talking to a nigger; and
how a little time afterwards, when there was more
‘ war ’ in Matabeleland, both girls basely deserted
him, taking all his ammunition and his old rifle;
“ And do you know,” said Peter, striking his knee, and
looking impressively across the fire at the stranger ;
“ what I’m sure of as that I’m sitting here? It’s that
that nigger I, caught at my hut, that day, was her nigger
husband! He’d come to fetch her that time ; and when
she saw she oouldn’t get away without me catching her, she
got the cartridges for him ! " Peter paused impressively
between the words. “ And now she’s gone back to him.
It's for him she’s taken that ammunition! ”
Peter looked across the fire at the stranger, to see what
impression his story was making.
“ I tell you what,” said Peter, “if I'd had any idea
who that bloody nigger was, the day I saw him standing
at my door, I’d have given him one cartridge in the back
of his head more than ever he reckoned for 1” . . ..
“You’ve been in the wars, too, I see,” said Peter
bending forward a little, and looking at the stranger’s
feet. “ By God ! Both of them!—And right through!
You must had a bad time of it?”
“ It was very long ago,” said the stranger.
* * * *
[Olive Schreiner used to have a savage wit—a
sort of saturnine appreciation of the incongruous
—but little humor at any time. Now, her sense
of humor seems gone altogether. At several
points already in this narrative the contrast
between Peter and the stranger has been pushed
over the edge of climax into an anti-climax of
smiles ; and now ! ‘ It was very long ago,’
said the stranger. . . . ‘ It’s not the first time
he’s done that r Pat,’ said a joking friend when
Q. Warden Mowbray kissed a back-blocks
bride. ‘Ah, begorrah, thin,’ said bridegroom
Pat with a heave of his shoulders, ‘ an’ divil a bit
oi care—if he hasn’t done it racently.’ . . . Or—
‘Pray, young woman, what is your name?’
‘Sarah, if you please, ma’am.’ ‘How long have
you been married?’ ‘ Married, ma’am ? ’ ‘Yes,
married.’ ‘lf you please, ma’am, I had a mis-
fortune, ma’am,” replied the girl, casting down
her eyes. ‘ What, have you not been married ? ’
‘No, ma’am, not yet.’ ‘Good heavens! Dr.
Middleton, what can you mean by bringing this
person here ? ’ exclaimed Mrs. Easy. ‘ Not a
married woman, and she has a child ! ’ ‘lf you
please, ma’am,’ interrupted the young woman,
dropping a courtesy, ‘ it was a very little one.’—
Mr. Midshipman Easy.~\
• * * *
They sat quiet in the blaze for a while.
Then Peter said, “ Did you see any niggers about yes-
terday? I haven’t come across any in this part.”
“There is,” said the stranger, raising himself, “an old
woman in a cave over yonder, and there is one man in the
bush, ten miles from this spot. He has lived there six
weeks, since you destroyed the kraal, living on roots and
herbs. He was wounded in the thigh, and left for dead.
He is waiting till you have all left this part of the country
that he may set out to follow his own people. His leg is
not yet so strong that he may walk fast.”
“ Did you speak to him ? ” said Peter.
“ I took him down to the water where a large pool was.
The bank was too high for the man to descend alone."
“ It’s a lucky thing for you our fellows didn’t catch
you,” said Peter. “Our captain’s a regular little mar-
tinet. He’d shoot you as soon as look at you, if he saw
you fooling round with a wounded nigger. It’s lucky you
kept out of his way.”
“The young ravens have meat given to them,” said
the stranger, lifting himself up ; “and the lions go down
to the streams to drink.”
Ah—yes said Peter ; “ but that’s because weean’t
help it! ”
* • • *
Then the stranger takes up the tongumg and
enquires who gave Peter the piece of land which
is his reward for trooper-service. ‘ Why, the
Chartered Company,’ says Peter. ‘And’who
pave it to the Chartered Company?’ ‘Why
England, of course.’ ‘And who gave it to
land ? ’ ‘ Oh, the English said it was theirs and
of course it was.’ And Peter affirms that the
niggers went with the land, and that they are
rebels, and the stranger wants to knows whether
Armenians are rebels, and Peter says ‘no, they
are on our side,’ and ‘you see, if we don’t help
the Armenians the Russians would ; and we’ve
got to prevent that: they’d get the land ; and it’s
on the road to India.’ And the stranger, having
stressed the contrast between Britain weeping
over the murdered and outraged Armenians, and
Britain murdering and outraging the Mashonas
and Matabeles, leaves the moral lying nice and
loose for hypocrites to pick up if they dare.
* * * •
Then the stranger goes on to tell Peter stories—
about an old woman and a young one who
escaped the English slaughter, and how the old
woman starved herself to death so that the young
woman might bring forth her baby, and Peter
happens to have met that identical young woman
with her baby—he had a mate with him . . . and
she was young and pretty . . . and, well . . ,
they didn’t shoot her ; and about a Cape minister
who denounced Cecil Rhodes from the pulpit;
and about many other things—dragged in regard-
less of proportion or probability. And gradually
a great change is worked in Peter Simon. Before
the stranger came he has been considering how he
would float a big mining company and become a
multi-millionaire like Rhodes : now he offers to
join the stranger’s company, which seems to con-
sist principally of poor and afflicted persons who
die off before the regular insurance-table age.
And then—
A curious warmth and gladness stole over Peter Halket
as he knelt; it was like when, as a little child, his mother
folded him to her: he saw nothing more about him but a
soft bright light. Yet in it he heard a voice cry, “ Be-
cause thou hast loved mercy—and hated oppression "
When Trooper Peter Halket raised himself, he saw the
figure of the stranger passing from him. He cried, “My
Master, let me go with you.” But the figure did not
turn; And, as it passed into the darkness, it seemed to
Peter Halket that the form grew larger and larger: and
as it descended the further side of the koppje it seemed
that for one instant he still saw the head with a pale,
white light upon it: then it vanished.
And Trooper Peter Halket sat alone upon the koppje.
* • * •
Here endeth the first part of the story, com-
prising about seven-tenths of the whole. Its
artistic falsity should be apparent. Th 6 characters
and scenes have to be real and natural, or there is
no force in them—they uselessly beat the air. And
they do actually pretend to be real and natural,
and the author strives hard by many clever little
touches to make them seem so. But the trance
kills the unity. Even conceding the point whether
Peter Halket would dream in such fashion, he
could not possibly dream such things—things
alien to him, above his capacity, which he has
never seen or heard of. So" the reader falls be-
tween two .stools. Halket’s hands pull him this
way, and Schreiner’s voice that way; he drops
the substance, yet cannot reach the shadow ; the
wolf’s eyes persist in glaring under grandmam-
ma’s nightcap. The result is a target missed, un
coup manque.
The second part dates a few days further on.
Under the river bank, Halket’s companions have
caught the identical nigger the stranger men-
tioned—the precise one, as Peter believes, who ran
away with his best girl, and into his head he has
vowed to put a bullet. But Peter is not the Peter
of yore. He has been a changed man since that
night on the veld. And when the martinet captain
announces that the nigger is to be hanged in
the morning Peter is suddenly moved to protest,
and preaches his captain a sermon about the duty
of loving one’s black brethren, winding up with
an appeal to let this particular black brother go
free. When the captain gets all his oaths out he
announces a slight change of programme—Peter
will shoot the black brother in the morning. But
in the night Peter gets up and lets him loose ; off
he bounds ; out comes the captain with his re-
volver—Pop ! —and Peter goes into a trance for
good. He is quietly buried ; and the Chartered
Company asks no questions. End of Trooper
Peter Halket of Mashonaland.
4— _
And so passes away Olive’s glory—unless
‘ Peter Halket ’ has been written recently, and is
not identical with the big book (‘From Man to
Man,’ the projected title) on which she was said
to have been at work for meditative years. Olive
is 34 now, and a woman, and married, and her
golden days are over. Much she may write that
is strong and interesting as compared with the
work of others : little, surely, that can favorably
compare with her own. For the flash and fervor
of divine youth have left her, and she is a woman,
and married, and 34. How sad that any clever
person should ever be married and 34, when there
are so many dull persons who are unmarried and
merely 21 or so ! But, after all, to have written
Ihe Story of an African Farm ’ is glory enough
for any single individual. What millions of un-
distinguished embers glow and fade before a clear
jet of flame lightens humanity like that magni-
ficent book !

king among men in body, mind arid heart.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
FREEDOM.
Thus said the Gods : “Let us bind the soul of man in
chains lest he become a God even as we.” So forged they
the fetters of love and fear and hope and hate and faith
that no man might be free.
How shall a man unravel
The skein of >things aright,
Untouched of joy or travail,
Undazed of any light?
How shall he outline surely
From all illusion free
The bare Truth veiled obscurely
In all that seems to be ?
When Faith hath ceased to cumber,
When Fear hath failed to quell,
When Death is one with Slumber,
When Heaven is one with Hell.
When Love’s last link is broken,
When Hope’s last lure is slain,
Shall wake the word unspoken
That trembles in his brain.
Unspurred of fierce ambition,
Un vexed of vain desire,
The wail of weak contrition,
The wave of fruitless ire ;
Through the vast Cosmic Temple
His thought shall wander free,
Till Truth has laid the kernel bare
In all that seems to be.
Queensland.
GEO. ESSEX EVANS.
TWO LENSES.
Those big brown eyes
With fervors flashing bright
From fires behind, that to my own each night
Responsive rise! —
Dearer to me by far
Are they than gold and flawless rubies are,
ijf Those big brown eyes.
To purblind eyes
A blur of blending blue
Appears the loveliest scene man ever knew —
Seas smudged with skies —
Until the lens before
Them rises to cast bursts of beauty o’er
The purblind eyes.
So big brown eyes
Have shown to me the truth
Of love, of life, of passion and of youth ;
The mantling lies
Have flown the world since I have seen it through
Those big brown eyes.
Melb.
M‘G.
THE POWER OF THE CIRCUMSTANCE.
“The strong God Circumstance.” — Vedas.
A raindrop fell from the asphodel
Deeps of the summer sky ;
In the scoriae bed on Vesuvius side,
In the lambent lap of the lava tide
It sank to die.
A raindrop fell in the snowy bell
Of a fairy flower ;
It glistened and gleamed in its beauty there
Like a diamond decking a forehead fair
In the bridal hour.
A raindrop fell in a sheeny shell
By Oman’s wave ;
It softly slept ’neath the watery whirl
Of the dreamy deep and woke—a pearl
In Ocean cave.
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.
LAST NIGHT!
Last night, when the moon rose—round and
white—
Over the crest of the distant hill,
You sang your song to us there last night
When the sleeping world lay hushed and stiM.
Oh, why did the scented breezes shake
The oak-trees’ foliage then ? I vow
It was that the envious birds should wake
And hear your song ’neath the leafy bough !
They say, in this sunlit Southern land
The birds thereof have been silent long ;
Why they sing no more I understand—
For you have stolen the wild birds’ song !
Ah, I saw the bright stars wane—and swoon,
And heard the murmurous night-wind sigh !
You sang your song—and the placid moon
Trembled and paled in the soft grey sky.
You sang last night, and the saddened bird
Away to her leaf-hid nestlings flew,
Knowing their music would ne’er be heard
Since Heaven had given their songs—to you !
N.S.W.
THE BREAKER.
Maoriland has finally got level with
Stephen Bosher, or thinks it has, and he is to be
duly hanged. Bosher is supposed to have broken
into the house of old Jones at Petone, and mur-
dered him and his wife, after first blinding them
with pepper. Almost the only direct evidence
against him was that of his wife, and to make her
an available witness the police patiently dug out
the fact that Bosher had a previous wife living,
got him two years for bigamy, and nullified his
second marriage. Then the alleged Mrs.
Bosher No. 2 proceeded to tell things,
with the above result. The first suspicion
about the bigamy arose through Bosher, while
drunk, making ill-regulated observations to
his second wife ; if he had been a sober, teetotal
man the murder of old Jones, of Petone, might
have remained unavenged for ever. Bosher, by
the way, was quite sober when he killed Jones.
Apparently it wasn’t drink that caused the
murder, whereas it ' certainly was drink that
brought it to light, so the usual moral lesson
doesn’t come in here at all.

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confident ial.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 10, 1897.
The Provincial Standpoint.
Edmund Barton, the leader of the Federal
Contention and probably the most influential
Federationist in Australia, has definitely
thrown in his lot with the semi-provincial
crowd which desires to keep the railways of
the mainland under five conflicting manage-
ments. This pronouncement may not un-
likely turn the scale in favor of provin-
cialism ; things were otherwise so evenly
balanced that it is just possible Mr. Bar-
ton’s voice might have turned it either way.
The reasons given by the Federal leader for
his decision are singularly unsatisfactory ; in
fact, they could serve rather better as reasons
for federating the railway systems than for
keeping them in their present state of chaos.
They suggest that the great advocate of
Federation has studied the legal and con-
stitutional aspect of his subject a great deal,
and the financial aspect of it very little
indeed. According to the Sydney Daily
Telegraph’s report, Mr. Barton’s theory is
as follows :
It was a pleasure to find that such a large number of
members of the Convention thought that the railways
should remain the property of the States. There was one
argument in regard to railways which could scarcely be
resisted. It was conceded that the lands were to remain
the property of the provinces, because it was conceded
that the Federation had more to do with the external
outlook, whilst the provinces would have more to do with
local and provincial affairs, and amongst these latter
necessarily and inevitably were the lands of the provinces.
If the lands were not to pass they must consider what
the railways were created for. Were they not the very
instruments for the development of those lands, just as
the roads were, and nobody thought for one instant of
handing over the roads and bridges to the Federation.
The railways were instituted for just the same purposes
as the roads and bridges. The railways were inherently
provincial and not Federal properties. ... It would
be with a considerable lack of that local knowledge
necessary for their proper management. If ever the rail-
ways were taken over, it would be when the people were
accustomed to look on them from a standpoint which had
not yet been reached. In the Draft Bill of 1891 there was
a clause that seemed to be particularly applicable for
that purpose. That was, in effect, that at any time a
majority of both Houses in each State might refer to the
Federal authority any matter which they thought could
be better dealt with by the Federal authority than by the
States. Let them wait until that time came.
The Bulletin submits that this reasoning
is absurd, inconclusive and irrelevant. The
railways are certainly “intended for the
development of the lands,” but they are no
more “inherently provincial” than are the
posts and telegraphs, which are to be handed
over to the Federal Government. These were
also intended for the development of the
province which created them. The iron
roads of any province are just as strictly
provincial as its customs revenue, its excise,
its defences, and everything belonging to it,
and no more so. There is nothing “ in-
herently provincial ” about any of them, nor
about the roads and bridges, nor even about
the public lands. In The Bulletin’s
opinion there is nothing inherently provincial
about anything, in fact; everything that is
done or left undone in any province affects
the prosperity and happiness, present or
future, of every other province and every
inhabitant in every other province. There-
fore, everything is inherently Federal to
begin with, right down to the culvert at
Cow Flat, and the bad state of the dirt road
at Brown’s Plains, and the lamentable
absence of a log bridge at Borrolloola.
But as the Federal Government can’t keep
its personal eye on all these things, it pro-
poses to delegate some of them, such as
roads, bridges, and control of State lands, to
the Provincial Governments, and the Pro-
vincial Governments, being also unable to
personally supervise all their responsibilities,
delegate some of their functions to municipal
councils, shire councils, divisional boards,
&c. But there is nothing inherently pro-
vincial about anything ; the division of duties
is a mere matter of convenience and policy.
» * * »
The term “ inherently provincial ” applies
to the railways less fittingly than to almost
anything else. The idea of the great means
of communication the permanent link
which binds the States together—being
purely provincial, sounds almost ludicrous.
The railways were created, in part, at least,
as Mr. Barton alleges, “ for the develop-
ment of the lands.” And, thanks to provin-
cial management, they have developed them
about as badly as could possibly be imagined,
and will probably continue to do so. Pro-
vincial management develops the lands
about Jerilderie by loading them with
freights for 412 miles to Sydney, whereas
Federal management, which took no notice
of provincial boundaries, would make a con-
nection about 160 miles long with Melbourne.
It develops the lands about Hay by bur-
dening the produce with 454 miles of freight
when about 200 miles is all that is neces-
sary. It develops the lands west of
Hay by refusing them any railways
at all, because a connection with Syd-
ney is too expensive, and because the
great maxim of provincial management is to
see these districts and all other districts
darned before it will supply a connection
with the easily accessible ports of South
Australia, or any other place outside the
province. All the north-west of Victoria
remains undeveloped, because a railway to
Melbourne would be long and expensive and
probably unprofitable, and the general tenor
of provincial management is to see the north-
west or any other place in Hades rather than
build a few cheap branch lines to afford
access to the adjacent South Australian
ports. It would be interesting to know how
much more the lands of south-western New
South Wales would be worth if they were
only 150 to 200 miles by rail from a seaport,
as they would be under a Federal railway
system that was not bound by provincial
interests and provincial boundaries, instead
of over 400 miles, as necessitated by the
present mode of “development.” Also,
how much more the lands of western New
South Wales and western Victoria and south-
western Queensland would be worth if they
had railway communication —which could be
easily and cheaply provided under a Fedei’al
railway policy—instead of having none at all,
which is, and seems likely to be for many
years, their fate under the provincial method.
Then it would be interesting to further esti-
mate how much this increase would add to
the total wealth of the country, to the land
revenue and land and income tax returns of
the provinces, to their population, to their
capacity for supplying employment, and
many other things. Provincial management
develops the country well enough in the
districts for which the provincial ports are
the natural outlet. Beyond that limit it
either does it very badly or not at all.
* * * *
It is not only Federal control of existing
lines that is wanted but—what is much more
important—Federal control of construction.
Failing that the new lines in the western
Riverina will all be built pointing north-
eastward to Sydney, instead of south-east-
ward towards Melbourne ; and those of
north-western Victoria will be built Mel-
bourne-wards, when their natural direction
is west towards Adelaide, and those of
western New South Wales will point east-
wards over the howling immensity of the
plains instead of taking the short road which
Nature has marked out for them towards
Spencer’s Gulf. The whole of three im-
portant railway systems will be slewed
round the wrong way ; many needless
millions will be wasted in building super-
fluous miles of line; many more millions or
scores of millions will be wasted, for scores
of years thereafter, in carrying goods need-
less distances ; the burden of these extra
charges will keep the inland districts poor,
retard development, permanently reduce
land-values, hamper industries, diminish
the State revenues, and keep the
country hungry and unprogressive. And
by forcibly dragging the trade of each
province to its own provincial city regardless
of natural tendencies, the fusion of the Aus-
tralian people will be retarded, and the
separation into distinct and semi-hostile com-
munities will be accentuated and made moie
permanent. The abolition of border tariffs
is intended to obliterate boundaries and let
trade, within Australia, go where it will.
The great object of the railway system is to
make trade go where it doesn’t want to, by
opening lines in that direction, and to pre-
vent it taking its natural course by refusing
to build railways to connect with anywhere
save the local capital.
* * • *
The only way to develop the lands is to
afford means for carrying the produce of the
lands to a market as cheaply as possible, and
the essential of cheapness is economy of
distance. Railways alone don’t necessarily
develop the lands. If the wool - growing
regions of the Riverina were “ developed ”
by a railway which had one end at J erilderie
and the other at Perth, it would be a very
great and long and wonderful railway, but,
as the freights upon it would probably absorb
more than the value of the wool carried, it
wouldn’t develop the country worth a straw.
This is an extreme illustration, but it is
just what the provincial railway manage-
ments are doing, though they do it on a
smaller scale, every day, and what they pro-
pose to keep on doing indefinitely.
* * * *
Mr. Barton opposes railway federation for
the additional reason that a central manage-
ment would lack the “local knowledge”
necessary to control so vast an undertaking.
If this argument were applied all round it
would lead to interesting results. The
Federal post and telegraph boss, for instance,
will not have a complete local knowledge of
every place in Australia, so presumably he
will depend on trusted subordinates who,
being in charge of different districts, can
supply the required knowledge. Similarly,
a Federal railway manager would presumably
have the continent divided into districts,
with men something like Eddy and Mathie-
son in charge, and they would have their
capable subordinates in charge of sub-dis-
tricts, and the “ local knowledge ” would be
supplied very much as it is at present. The
Federal Public Works Committee, or some
analogous body, could easily be constituted
to include members from each province, and
with a local knowledge of each province. It
couldn’t be any more absurd than it is in
New South Wales at present, when purely
city men have to decide re the construction
of lines in districts which some of them
never saw, and gather their informa-
tion at the best by flying visits, in
the course of which they look at en-
gineering problems of which they know
nothing, and decide on sight whether soil of
which they are quite ignorant will grow
vegetables that they are wholly unacquainted
with.
* * * *
There are any number of ways by which,
if it is so desired, provincial interests
can be preserved, and the construction ex-
penditure allotted on a reasonably equitable
scale. But the three essentials are that
fares and freights shall be made uniform ;
that there shall be one railway gauge and
one only throughout Australia ; and that all
future lines shall be built to give the pro-
ducer the best and cheapest and shortest
outlet for his produce, and that the back
country shall no longer be sacrificed to the
interests of the cities and the city traders.
If these things can be done otherwise than
by Federal control, The Bulletin would
much like to know how. Mr. Barton’s
theory is that Australia should wait until the
people are accustomed to look at the matter
from a very different standpoint. The
answer to this can be put in four proposi-
tions : (1) There isn’t likely to be any time
within the next 500 years when Sydney and
Melbourne, with their adjacent districts,
won’t have a controlling vote in their
respective provinces ; (2) There isn’t likely
to be any time inside 1000 years or more
when these cities won’t want to drag all the
trade of their provinces to Sydney and Mel-
bourne, no matter who suffers ; (3) if it
would cost £5,000,000 to make a uniform
gauge now, by waiting for Federal control
till people look at the matter from a new
standpoint the time will come when it will
cost £10,000,000; if the standpoint is waited
for long enough the cost will be £100,000,000
or more ; (4) while the new standpoint is
being waited for the railway system is going
further and further askew, and becoming
more costly and useless and circuitous ; and
the districts whose natural outlet is by
lines running north and south are being sup-
lied with political railways running east and
west, and vice versa; and the development
of the interior is being shamefully retarded.
Also, it may be remarked that the way to
make the people look at the matter from a
national standpoint, which Mr. Barton
seems to vaguely admit as being desirable,
is to teach them to do so, and Mr. Barton’s
advocacy of the narrow provincial standpoint
doesn’t help much in this direction.
A Hard Knot
Ihe situation with regard to Premier Reid s
visit to England to join in the Record Reign
celebration grows more weirdly complicated as
time goes on. There are about a dozen different
aspects of it, and each one, when carefully con-
sidered, looks more tangled than the others.
Ihe Bulletin hereby proceeds to sort them
out—or to attempt doing so—and to show as
exactly as possible how New South Wales’
troubled little fat man really stands, or, rather,
how he wobbles, as he isn’t standing just now
in any definite sense.
* * * *
The other Premiers propose—if their present
intentions are not altered by circumstances— to
each appoint an understudy who will do duty
in his chief’s absence. Fysii, Isaacs and
Holder will probably fill the vacant places in
the three Southern provinces. The respective
Parliaments will, therefore, sit and go on with
public business as usual. If the Convention
has finished drafting the Federal Constitution
before the Premiers depart, it will be put
through its Parliamentary stages in their
absence, so that when the various bosses return
it will be possible, so far as their provinces are
concerned, to call the Convention together
almost immediately for its second session, and
go on with the work as expeditiously
as if there had been no Record Reign
grovel at all. Premier Reid is, for the
present at least, the one obstacle in the way
of this very simple and common-sense arrange-
ment. His purpose is to adjourn the N.S.W.
Legislature until his return ; therefore, when he
comes back it will only begin to discuss the
Federal Constitution ; and the other provinces
and the Convention and the whole business of
Federation will have to wait for six or eight
weeks until N.S.W. is ready to go on. Under
these circumstances the other Premiers may
possibly abandon the plan of allowing their
Legislatures to discuss the Constitution Bill in
their absence ; if they have to wait till the one
backward province of N.S.W. catches up there
is little or nothing to be gained by it. But the
fact will remain that they signified their will-
ingness to act on this basis, and Premier Reid
blocked the way. Therefore, Reid will be held
up to obloquy in the other provinces, and by
the Opposition in his own province, as the one
utterly selfish groveller who suspended the
most urgent business that Australia ever had
on hand, and put great national interests at
hazard, solely that he might have his own pri-
vate and individual and selfish grovel in peace.
Possibly the other Premiers only made their
offer by way of striking an attitude, and for
purposes of advt., and will be glad of an excuse
to go back from it. Still the fact remains that
they did make it, and that G. Reid was the sole
dissentient.
* * * *
Premier Reid’s great reason for going to
England, according to his own statement, was
that the rest of the Premiers were going ; that
Federation would necessarily be hung up in
their absence ; and that even if he remained and
tugged alone at the weary oar of politics, and
pushed the Constitution Bill through his own
Legislature, he would have to wait for the other
Parliaments, and nothing would be gained by his
haste. Now it appears that he would not
have to wait for the other Parliaments, as
they are willing to go straight on just
as if there was nothing unusual in the
way, and this extinguishes one of Reid’s
great reasons why he should go. Also, it
appears that the other Parliaments will have to
wait for New South Wales, which, by his own
logic, is a most powerful reason why Reid
should not go. Yet apparently he is going all
the same, though he has now no excuse worth
mentioning save that he wants to go, after his
repeated assurances that he didn’t really want
to go at all.
* * * *
G. H. Reid is at a further disadvantage as
compared with his fellow-grovellers that are to
be. He is the one Premier of the seven who
laid it down as an immutable axiom (in Dibbs’s
case) that no head of a Government should go
away bent on loyal gush and holiday-making,
taking the Premiership with him. Yet that is
just what Reid himself proposes to do. More-
over, Dibbs did not keep public business wait-
ing his convenience as Reid intends doing—the
Legislature met as usual with Barton in
charge, and went on with its ordinary work.
And even if Dibbs had suspended all legislative
business till his return, his offence, about which
Reid made so much complaint, would have
been very trifling by comparison, for there was
no public business then on hand of one-tenth
the importance of that which Reid desires to
hang up while he is away doing his little piece
of abasement. Moreover, any business that
was on hand then was Netv South Wales busi-
ness only—not that of all Australasia. If G. H.
Reid takes the Premiership to England, and
hangs up Federation indefinitely to do it, and
thereby forces the other provinces to hang it up
indefinitely also, especially after the view he
took of Dibbs’s conduct, he will furnish bis
opponents with a fair excuse to heave mud and
scorn and verbal dead cat at him, and to call him
a time-server and an opportunist and a hypo-
crite, for the rest of his political career. And
if he changes his course of action at this late
hour, it will be so obviously done through fear
of consequences that he will lose the holiday
and the grovel, while the dead-cat will be very
little alleviated.
* * * *
The apparently obvious way out of one, at
least, of the difficulties of the case, would be
for Premier Reid to allow Parliament to sit
and business to proceed while he is away, and
thus keep New South \Vale3 in line with the
other States, and avoid any serious delay in
Federal matters. Unfortunately, his position
is so very different from that of the other
Premiers that he can’t well do this. Turner
will presumably leave Isaacs in charge, and
Isaacs is strong, brilliant, resourceful and
moderately safe. Fysh is a reasonably strong
and eminently safe man, who will keep Brad-
don’s seat about as secure for him as Brad DON
could himself. Holder, Kingston’s under-
study, is also reasonably strong and eminently
reliable, and both he and Fysh are veteran ex-
Premiers, who know thoroughly what they
have to do. Reid, on the other hand, would
presumably have to depute his authority to old,
weak, fuzzy Brunker, who is neither strong
nor experienced, and whose knowledge of
politics consists mostly of a harmless gift for
dealing in cows and auctioning produce. It i s
one thing to leave Holder to keep an eye on
“Turncoat Bill” Copley, or the brilliant Isaacs
to suppress that broken reed, John MTntyre,
and quite another to leave the well-meaning
but dismally commonplace Brunker to run
the campaign against that astute, scheming,


ever-wakeful, and not too scrupulous veteran,
W J. Lyne. Again, each of the other
Premiers leaves his locum tenens with a solid
majority behind him; Reid doesn’t—he is
largely dependent on the support of the Third
Party, and there is absolutely no knowing what
Lyne might do, or Brunkbr might not do with
it in his absence. The Third Party avowedly
gives support in return for democratic conces-
sions, and Lyne might offer it the earth while
Reid was away, as the price of its vote, and the
spectacle of Brunker conducting a delicate
counter-negotiation would be something for the
gods to weep over.
* * • •
Still another trouble. The leader of the
Opposition in each of the other provinces
approves, more or less reluctantly, of the Pre-
mier’s departure, and promises not to harass
the Government in his absence. Lyne doesn’t
approve and won’t promise anything. His atti-
tude in this matter is quite natural and remark-
ably human. M‘lntyre, Lewis, and Copley
have no specially large and effective brick to
throw in the absence of their three Premiers,
for neither Turner, Braddon, or Kingston
has given his enemies such a handle as
Reid did in re the case of his predeces-
sor, Dibbs. Having no brick worth speak-
ing of to throw, they make no sacrifice in
promising not to throw it. Lyne, having
the largest and most effective brick to
throw that he ever handled, naturally re-
fuses to promise anything about not throwing
it. To approve of Reid’s visit to England
would mean giving up all right to jeer at his
enemy about his change of front, and to say the
same things to him that he said to Lyne’s late
chief a few years ago, and Lyne, naturally
enough, doesn’t feel like surrendering so beauti-
ful an opportunity without any compensation.
Therefore, if Reid shuts up Parliament and
goes he will be gibed at as the man who sacri-
ficed the interests of five provinces for the sake
of his own private grovel at the foot of the
British throne, and who went back on all the
beautiful principles that he laid down so
forcibly for Dibbs’s guidance. And if he goes
without shutting up Parliament, Brunker
will probably let the Ministry go to pieces
in his absence, and, in addition to the
obloquy, he may lose his billet. And if he
doesn’t go, after all, he will lose his share of
the great holiday outbreak, and will appear as
a man who hadn’t principle enough to do what
he said was right, or courage enough to do
what he knew was wrong. Altogether there
are more different aspects about Premier
Reid’s present position than it is easy to count.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Clroumlocution in the Commonwealth.
Item from report of the Convention debate on
the best means of remedying deadlocks between
the two Houses of the Federal Legislature : — 1
Mr. O’Connor’s method of getting over a deadlock
between the two Houses was also unique. He would
allow the two Chambers to meet in conference with equal
powers of voting, and let the majority decide. Mr.
Isaacs and other Victorian representatives at once
pointed out that that would enable a minority in the
Assembly to defeat the Government. Mr. O’Connor
admitted that his plan would be open to that objection,
and in reply to an interjection by Sir Graham Berry, he
admitted that a new Government would have to be
formed, which would go to the country on the point in
dispute. “Then you must go to the people, after all,”
observed the Victorian Speaker, and seeing how he was
unconsciously advocating the referendum, Mr. O’Connor
changed the subject.
Query : why would a new Government have to
be formed ? If a small majority in the Assem-
bly was in favor of something, and a large
majority in the Senate was against it, and when
the two Houses sat and voted together the
majority in the Senate and the minority in the
Assembly combined came out on top, why
should not the Government go on with business
as if nothing had happened, and trust to the
next election to put a new complexion on
things ? The Convention, however, practically
agreed that the simple plan of both Houses
sitting together to vote on disputed points was
unworkable, and the general opinion favored
instead an elaborate scheme put forward by
Premier Turner, as follows :
To have a dissolution of both Houses in cases of irre-
concileable differences; and in the event of both Cham-
bers returning from their respective constituents with the
mandate still to differ, the matter in dispute is then to be
submitted to a popular Referendum, both of the States
and of the whole populace. This would supply absolute
finality. For example, we may take female suffrage as a
case in point. Suppose the National Assembly had
passed the Bill, and the States Council had thrown
it out. Neither would give way after confer-
ence. Then the Governor-General, in terms of
the constitution, would dissolve both Chambers.
Members would go for re-election, making this question
the sole battle ground. Popular opinion would be
focussed on it. But it might happen that when the new
Houses were constituted they would be still in antagon-
ism. In that case the question “Yes” or “No” would
be put to the individual voters of all the States separately
and of all the States combined. If out of five colonies
three States gave a majority for the Bill in the aggregate,
as well as a separate majority in each colony, then
Woman’s Suffrage would become law ; but if a majority
of the whole body of electors resident in two colonies only
were to vote for the Bill, and the majority in three colo-
nies, though a minority of the whole, were to vote against
it, then the Bill would be lost.
The brilliant feature of this scheme, as shown
in the italicised sentence, is that, after all this
trouble and circumlocution, the minority in the
Assembly could defeat the Government just the
same. If the Assembly favored anything it
would mean, in a general way, that the majority
of the people were for it; if the Senate opposed
it, that would mean that a majority of the
States were against it. If the two Houses
then voted together the majority of States
might override the majority of men. Turner’s
elaborate and costly referendum provides that
there must be a majority both of people and of
States to carry the measure by a popular vote,
therefore the majority of States may vote dow’n
the majority of people again. It is exactly
the same result arrived at differently, with
much waste of money and profanity, and with
an expenditure of two months’ time instead of
two hours.
A Great Waste of Figures.
One financial aspect of Federation which calls
for more anathema than the average is the
making of elaborate calculations, and the build-
ing up of elaborate tables, to show how much
each province will contribute to the Customs
revenue when a uniform tariff is enforced. The
beginning of these masses of confusion is the
following set of propositions : Each province
imports goods to such and such a value ; it has
a certain tariff; the tariff yields so much per
annum. If the W.A. tariff is applied all round
N.S.W. will pay more than it now does and
Victoria will pay less. If the S. A. tariff is ap-
plied all round N.S.W. and Westralia will both
pay more than they do. If the Tasmanian
tariff is applied all round, all the mainland
provinces will pay more than thej r now do, and
the amount they will pay works out to some-
thing incredible. These theories are all based
on the assumption that, if 10 per cent, duties
yield so much, 20 per cent, duties will yield
twice as much, which is just as logical as
arguing that, if a man is bitten by one dog on
the first day of the month, he will necessarily
be bitten by three dogs on the third. Tariffs
don’t work out that way, and all the calcula-
tions based upon the assumption that they do
are waste-paper. No one knows with any
accuracy what a tariff will yield until
it is tried. When the Dibbs Government
came into power it doubled and trebled
the duties, and, in two or three years, when the
country adapted itself to its new conditions, the
new tariff yielded just about 10 per cent, more
than the old one—not twice as much, or any-
thing like it. The Victorian tariff is several
times as heavy as that of N.S. Wales, and last
financial year it yielded less in the bulk than
the N.S. Wales one, and only per cent, more
in proportion to population. As a matter of
figures it should have yielded 100 or 200 per
cent, more, but it didn’t. The Westralian tariff
is lighter than that of Victoria, and it produces
four times as much, in proportion to population.
No one knows, until it is tried, what any scale
of duties will yield, or what any province will
pay under it, or will lose or gain by it; but the
general result seems to be that, if a uniform
scale is applied throughout Australia, after a
few years the country will adapt itself to its
new conditions, and the burden will be much
the same all round. The rest is mostly wasted
figures.
The Case of God!
Dear Bulletin, — God’s all right, now 1 —
“ the churches ” have taken up His case.
“ We’ll pull You through,” they are telling
Him. “ You are the Almighty Ruler of the
Universe—holding all things in the hollow of
your hand—but we'll see to it that You are not
snubbed by being dropped out of the Federal
Constitution. Infinite, Eternal and Omniscient
—only keep up Your courage, and we’ll take
care that You’re not left out in the cold ! ” All
which appears to be playing it pretty low down
on the God of the Old Testament; the ferocious
God that revelled in the massacre of women
and children ; the peevish God that was
always on the prowl for affronts to His
dignity ; the paltry God that was so much exer-
cised over priestly pots and pans and petti-
coats. For this is the God the churches want
“ recognised ” —less, apparently, upon the
assumption that, if He isn’t allowed to figure
in the bill, He may get mad and burst-up the
show, than upon the ground that, as He has so
long played a leading part in the Great Farce,
it would hardly be civil to leave Him out of the
cast at this time of day. But it is mere moss-
grown medisevalism to attempt to force recogni-
tion of the Pentateuchal monstrosity upon men
to whom He would be simply a devil, and who,
regarding the Supreme Being as absolutely
irrelative and conditionless, can’t help smiling
at the notion of His being angry or pleased, in-
jured or benefited, ignored or “recognised.”
To men of this stamp the amenities orthodoxy
is certain to lavish upon them will bring
no surprise. They will fully expect to have
their motives misrepresented in the name of
Gawd, their characters blackened for the
Deity’s sake—to be promptly relegated to the
“zealot’s ready hell,” and denounced as
“ enemies of religion ” for being rejectors of a
barbarous and brutal theology which is itself the
worst enemy of religion. But, since it is by
deeds, not by beliefs, men have to be judged,
and, since the actions of the man who can’t
swallow the Fiend-GOD compare favorably for
the most part with those of the man who still
rejoices in the hope of hell-fire for the majority,
it would be a mere useless impertinence to
attempt to impose officially upon the former
any endorsement of the crass theological
conceptions of his intellectual inferiors.
You can’t squeeze the greater into the less ; and
it is an unmistakeable step backwards towards
intellectual darkness to attempt to “ recognise ”
nationally any God that could be so recognised,
or that could want to be so recognised, or that
could be conceived of as wanting to be so recog-
nised by the inconceivably unimportant life-
atoms on one of the pettiest units of, probably,
one of the most insignificant sun - systems
amongst an incalculable multitude of such sys-
tems in an unthinkably infinite universe. Man
is a circumstance—cursed with the conceit that
he is the circumstance ! And when he shall
have completely outgrown that delusion he will
have found God —not got rid of Him I—Sar-
donyx.
“ Agitator ” Fleming is making a strong at-
tempt to shift Labor-member Trenwith from
his pedestal. In the first place, Fleming,
“ speaking from personal experience as a work-
ing bootmaker,” contradicted Trenwith’s very
questionable statement that high wages ruled
in the Melbourne boot - trade. Fleming
since then has prepared a list of aspersions on
Trenwith’s democratic character, and, with
the assistance of the Freetraders, it is quite
possible that the aggrieved agitator may get
his knife fairly into Trenwith’s ribs by next
election-day. TheMelb. working-man is, mean-
while, told (by Fleming) that Trenwith was a
violent anti-Homeßule spouter at the time of the
Redmond Bros.’ visit to Australia, when sym-
pathy with Irish tenants happened to be
unfashionable, owing to the recent murder of a
landlord or two. If the utterance, at any time,
of an Orangemanic oration can be proved
against Trenwith, he will seem a decidedly
unsuitable leader for a Labor-party. As for
the suggestion that he “saves himself from
exposure by crying or pretending to cry ” (on
the subject of his eyesight), it is a paltry and
cowardly-looking charge to put into print, even
though the afflicted man’s enemies may almost
persuade themselves of its truth. Touching
which matter The Bulletin hears that Tren-
with’s eyes were not greatly improved by the
cataract operation performed in London and
declared by cable to have been a complete suc-
cess. Since his return to Melbourne he has
made up his mind to undergo another opera-
tion, without travelling further than Collins-
street for the ordeal.

Melbourne Champion reckons that Gillies
will be leader of the Tory party in next Yic.
Parliament, vice MTntyre, gone out on his ear,
and that the old Parliamentary hand will be a
dangerous opponent for the Turner Ministry.
This paper ventures to bet a small sum that, if
Turner keeps his health and physical vigor,
he will tramp right through the fine-spun webs
of the artful Gillies and leave nothing of them
but fragments. Gillies knows how to out-
manoeuvre another web-spinner like himself,
therefore when he ran against inferior schemers
such as Munro he looked something above the
average. But a man like Turner, who doesn’t
diplomatise and scheme, and fish for votes, and
buy support with jobs, and angle with smellful
bait in troubled waters, but goes straight
through everything, will utterly upset Dun-
can’s apple-cart. What’s the good of Gillies
being an expert at the three-card trick if the
other man won’t play ?
The Federal Convention, according to reports
in Monday’s S.M. Herald, has struck the
exact difficulty which this paper foretold when
it lately wrote about “ The Tasmanian Fly-
speck.” Tasmania, it finds, is the great diffi-
culty in the way of fixing up a workable con-
stitution. The less populous provinces want
equal representation in the Federal Senate,
and no one objects to it in the case of South
Australia, Westralia and Queensland, because
they will inevitably grow to be at least as
populous as N.S. Wales and Yictoria, and
any inequality will adjust itself by-and-bye.
Everybody objects to it, however, more or
less in Tasmania’s case, because Tasmania is so
small that it can never hold half as much popu-
lation as even the smallest of the mainland
provinces, and under the equal-representation
system it will be a permanent anomaly. If
Tasmania were combined with Victoria the
two would, in area, make a respectable pro-
vince ; at present the situation is rather ridicu-
lous. The sleepy isle makes the third small
province in the Convention, and it thus enables
the small provinces to outvote the large ones. Its
presence seems to have burst up all hope of rail-
way federation ; its own railways are cut off from
those of the mainland, so most of the advan-
tages of Federal management would not directly
affect it; therefore its attitude on the subject
is one of blank indifference or hostility. If
Tasmania would amalgamate with Victoria all
these difficulties would be settled and a lot of
money saved in unnecessary government as
well. The united province, by the way, might
be called Tasmania, that being a sensible sort
of name with some eternal fitness about it. And
the island itself might be specifically named
Dobson, which would also have a lot of eternal
fitness about it. It is already Dobson to all
intents and purposes.
At time of going to press it was reported that
the financial committee of the Convention had
decided to throw up all idea of railway federa-
tion, and was also fast tending towards a
decision to throw up the idea of consolidating
the public debts. If it carries out the latter
idea it will have got over about two-thirds of
the questions it was appointed to solve by the
simple and effective process of throwing them
up, and it will be remembered hereafter as the
great unanimous chucker-up of Australia. The
futility of it was partly owing, no doubt, to
the policy of putting the mediocrities who knew
nothing of finance on the monetary branch of the
business, and putting the most promising finan-
cier of the crowd on the judicial committee; also,
Barton’s luckless pronouncement in favor of
provincial railway management probably had
something to do with it. The reason ascribed
for giving up the railway question is that
“ there are various serious difficulties in the
way.” Everybody knew that, but the Conven-
tion was created for the express purpose of
tackling the difficulties and getting over them.
If there had been no serious difficulties in the
way there would have been no need for a Con-
vention at all.
“ Gambler ” to The Bulletin
Despite the rejection of the Bill introduced into Vio.
Leg. Assembly last session by Hancock and Sangstkr for
legalising certain so-called “ art-unions,” these gambles
go merrily on. The A.N.A. lottery was lately drawn, and
those of the Eight Hour Committee and Druids shortly
will be. Re these affairs, there is much shuffling and
hypocrisy on the part of the Turner Govt. When Han-
cock and Sangster’s Bill for putting the Friendly
Societies’ lotteries on a legal basis came before the House,
Peacock was put up to oppose it; and when a goody-
goody deputation interviewed Turner to ask him to pro-
hibit Friendly Societies holding “ art-unions,” he said
that the prizes must not be in money, or the
authorities would interfere. Yet, after the draw-
ing of the A.N.A. “ art-union,” it was calmly
announced in the Age that the winner of the first prize
of £lOOO would receive a cheque for the amount, and the
Government has not taken the least notice. Of course,
most people can see little harm in a lottery fairly and
squarely conducted ; but there is a good deal of “confi-
dence trick ” about the management of those run by the
A.N.A., Druids, and Eight-Hour committee. No one but
a “mug” would invest in a lottery if he knew that only
about one-fifth of the money paid for tickets went
towards prizes, and that the other four-fifths was annexed
by the promoters for expenses, &c. Yet that is what is
done. A few figures from the report of the Druids’ Gala
for 1896 Tickets sold, 141,448, valued at £7072; prize-
money, £l333—leaving a balance of £5739, of which only
£646 was devoted to charities, the remainder being swal-
lowed up in “ expenses ” and commission. I have seen the
balance-sheets of the other “ art-unions ” referred to, and
they are very similar. This sort of thing is most unfair
to the investing public. Just imagine “ Tattersall ”
Adams running his sweeps on similar lines, and deducting
£BO,OOO out of a £lOO,OOO sweep! He could well afford to
placate Govt, by handing over a few thou, to charities.
The query naturally suggests itself—What becomes of the
money 1 The answer is, that these “ art-unions ” are run
for the benefit of a few favored individuals, who
get nice pickings in the shape of secretaryships,
&c., their remuneration being altogether out of pro-
portion to the value of their services. To give an
idea of the extravagant manner in which the sale of
tickets is conducted, I will again quote from the report
of the Druids’ Gala for’96 : —“ln N.S.W. 25,163 tickets
(value £1258) were sold, and a draft for £503 forwarded.”
In other words, it cost £755 to sell £1258 worth of tickets.
I ask is this sort of thing fair to the investing public ?
Would it not be better to prohibit these miscalled art-
unions altogether, and for Government to hold State lot-
teries ?
Victoria has a 2Jjd. “ National League ” con-
sisting of Brother Walter Madden, a stray
secretary with the unfortunate name of Bald,
and a few sundries. Here is one of Brother
Walter’s remarks (Argus report) to the annual
meeting held recently, at which 25 people
attended: —
Probably the most dangerous plank in the radical plat-
form was “ equal electorates,” and he was astonished to
meet many intelligent men who did not realise what that
meant. At the present time it took approximately about
4000 votes to elect a member of the Assembly in the
larger cities and towns, and about 2000 to elect a member
in the country districts, the reason being that it was con-
sidered twice as easy for men in the cities and towns to
go to the poll as for men in the country districts. If we
had equal electorates, 2000 voters in the cities and towns
would elect a member the same as in the country dis-
tricts, and the latter would be snuffed out. This was an
important point to impress on our country friends.
Since the first lunatic put straw in his hair
there has never been anything much more
childish than Madden’s reason for the ex-
istence of unequal electorates. He might
as well have put it down to the fact
that townspeople drink their milk fresh
from the can. That country constituencies
have smaller voters’ rolls is due to a tra-
dition in favor of the representation of
territory rather than of population. The
observance of such a tradition now would be
charmingly archaic—but the tradition doesn’t
count worth a cent. Nevertheless, dispro-
portionate representation of country districts
will continue awhile with the full approval of
many radicals. It costs now at least J>2oo to
contest a country electorate. Double the
electorate and you double the cost—thus re-
ducing greatly the number of men who are
available for candidates. The question was
discussed during the last session of Vic. Parlia-
ment, and there was no sign of an important
advocacy of equal electorates. Nevertheless,
Madden’s reason for unequal electorates should
be framed as the worst piece of sheer silliness
that has emanated even from the Madden
family.
“The two bye-elections of Romford and
Walthamstow aroused considerable comment and
spread dismay and jubilation alternately in the
Conservative and Liberal camps,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent. “ A pre-
vious 2000 - odd Conservative majority was
pulled down to 123 at Romford, and wiped out at
Walthamstow altogether. Sinclair, or Schlesinger,
a German Jew, formerly of Melbourne and the
Abgus, saved one seat for the Conservatives and
squeezed home by the short tip of a long nose
with his day-old naturalisation - papers in his
pocket. Mountain Dewar, of whisky fame, ran
against a sturdy, sensible Radical, and came a
cropper over the passive feet of a local Conserva-
tive boss—a sulky teetotaller. The Radical, Wood,
is a capable man, apparently worth his place in
any Parliamentary boat. His was a glorious
victory. ”
My Lord the Cardinal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Lord the Cardinal, let me re-echo your
famous sentence,
Couched in the choicest words, breathing the
spirit of charity,
And yet withal, to us, the keynote of your
Federal Address :
“ The man who urges separation from the great
Mother land
Is worse than criminal, aye, even with the
beast, bereft of reason.”
So, so. We’ll paste that gem of wisdom in our
Imperial hats
To keep our aspiring souls from bubbling with
the sentiment
Of Faith in our bright future, Hope for its near
approach,
And Charity, each with the other, when
Nationhood arrives.
You are right, my Lord the Cardinal; your
mundane wisdom
Transcends your saintliness. The man who
has the talent
To show the danger of the present move, and
has not worldly tact
To refrain from doing so, moves us to more
than pity.
What use to us are unborn sons that we should
leave them free ?
Better to pluck the honor that awaits the abdi-
cation of our trust,
The tinsel and the gewgaw show that fills our
soul with pride,
Our fellows with the envy of our indicated
merit, even though
Our land is sacrificed—a nascent Nation for a
bauble.
And yet, my Lord the Cardinal, it was, when
the world was young,
A virtue to revere that shrine where tangents
of the past and present met
And left us smiling. And so on through the-
ages up to—when ?
“ Let all the ends thou aimest at be thy Coun-
try’s, God’s, and Truth’s ”
Was princely counsel for the ardent youth and
chivalry of France.
But we ? We’re nothing—mere outcasts. Our
land a drift of island
In a lonely sea that needs the magic influence
of the North
To vitalise it. And are we, sons of a sterile
land, impotent, too?
If so, effacement is our doom. Let us together
stoop to fate.
And now, My Lord the Cardinal, when once
God came on earth
To shape the destiny of his chosen men, did he
decree
What we in these days call democracy, and its
resultant rule ?
Did this erection flourish till the Jews in flush
of pride
Cried out aloud, “Make us a King as other
Nations” ? for the Jews
Knew more than the Lord of Hosts. They
knew a little more than you
When, fired with Loyalty, you yielded to the
voice of men who sold you
As we would be, did we not know our path is
onward, though great men fall.
Yours is another milestone on our way this
decade.
N.S.W.
D. HEALEY.

The Corbett - Fitzsimmons battle will
shortly be reproduced with faithful details at
the Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-st., Sydney.
It was cabled that the great fight was recorded
all through by the Kinetoscope, and the colonial
branch of the Edison Laboratory before the fight
took place made its arrangements for securing the
record. The Edison Vitascope, Kinetoscope, and
Kinetophone, and the wonderful X-rays are still
on show in the Parlor, and are attracting great
public interest.*

SUNDRY SHOWS.
