“Sweet Lavender,” staged at Sydney
Criterion for a very short season, enables the
publio to draw comparisons between the Thornton
that is and the one that used to be. The present
Thornton comes out of the scrutiny very well ;
his Dick Phenyl is as fresh and original and in-
teresting and as affably tight as ever he was.
Phenyl is a refreshing change after Joseph Trent,
bookmaker, inasmuch as he is a human drunk
with a great soul, whereas the bookmaker was
more like an automaton with a great soul. There is
a fragrance of stale beer and of old piouldy hat mis-
laid in the ash-barrel about Phenyl, and his soul
is deteriorated somewhat by being left out in the
gutter at night when it was too far gone to get
home on all-fours, but this paper, not being a tee-
total organ, raises no special objection to these
small matters. Dick Phenyl is a solemn warning
against benevolence, inasmuch as that old bar-
rister with vine leaves in his hair is ready to do a
good turn to everybody, and obviously makes
nothing by it. Also, he is a fine example of what
the Prodigal Son looks like when the old original
swine whose company he enjoyed when he was
young are all dead, and he dpesn’t get on with the
new swine, who are fresh, and arrogant about the
tail, and life generally has lost its savour. His
career shows that virtue is not rewarded, inasmuch
as his one piece of good luck comes to him when
he is very drunk and departs before he has time to
get sober. The only people in the play who seem
to prosper much are the old banker who betrayed
the woman who trusted him, and the jumping
American who looks as if his money had been
made out of salt-pork at Chicago. The story has
no particular ending. Nothing special happens to
gladden the life of the. woman who was thrown
out when she was young. The wicked old banker
is left in a state of renewed prosperity. The
character of the jumpy American is not cleared of
the accusation of salt-pork which hangs over him,
and nothing particularly good happens to barrister
Phenyl. Nevertheless, “Sweet Lavender” is
a play that nobody can help loving, and
it is true to Nature, inasmuch as no
story has any definite ending. There always
remains after it the soulless necessity for
all the characters to get up and wash and go to
bed again for forty years or so, just as if there
had never been any story.
* » * *
“Lavender” is full of good parts, and
they are all filled remarkably well. Barrister
Phenyl, comedy cynic, philanthropist, drunk,
philosopher, and many other things, is about the
best character on Thornton’s list, which is saying
a great deal. Miss Noney Seabrooke plays Minnie
as well as she was ever played in Australia—
possibly better; and Miss Bessie Rignold, as
Lavender, though this paper is hardly so badly
mashed on her as it was when she played the
brown maid from Demerara, is excellent as a
whole, and the only specific complaint that
can be laid against her is her hair. Herbert
Terry, who has a tendency to be rather a
colorless young man at times, makes Clement
Hale go quite as well as that rather ordinary
good person can be made to go. If the Amurrikin
of George Rowlands could keep his nose a little
more under control, and didn’t wear quite so
aggressively shiny an aspect, suggestive of a
hurried wash at the kitchen sink, and the ancient
banker looked a trifle more like a person that any
sane depositor would leave his money with, there
would be no fault to find with “ Sweet Lavender.”
Anyhow, both the play and the co. are far above
the average. Even the clever Pinero never wrote
anything prettier than this conventional little tale
of a bad banker, and a noble-hearted drunk, and
a woman who went astray in the old, old, tired
way that women have been straying in since the
days when our remote ancestor, the original seed-
ape of the female persuasion, went wrong during
the Miocene Period, and was scorned by the mega-
therium and other decent society for ever after-
wards.
** * *
Westmacott bade good-bye to the stony
paths of theatrical management at Sydney Royal
on Monday afternoon. The farewell took the
shape of a benefit, the show consisting of “ Pene-
lope,” operetta in one act, the comedietta, “A
Show of Hands,” interludes by Charles Godfrey,
Alice Leamar, Coleman, Edgar Granville, the
Allisons, Little Gulliver, Knowles, and Ernest
Fitts, and one act of the “Private Secretary.”
Said act was a beautiful and affecting monument
erected prematurely to the memory of the original
Uncle Cattermole —good old Harwood—whose place
in the comedy it is very difficult to fill. No one ever
cattermoled like him in this country. Westma-
cott’s benefit was a respectable success on the
whole, and that manager departs out of the busi-
ness (for the present at least) with the good wishes
of everybody who knows him. He is remembered
as a man who put on some of the worst plays ever
seen in Sydney, and some that weren’t quite so
bad, and some that were even better, and who
kept the ghost walking honorably to the last.
The latter is a matter to be especially proud of.
The Australian managers whose ghost is always
reliable are getting to be very few now.
** * *
The Nile will rise at Her Majestyls for
positively the last time this (Saturday) evening,
and then there will be a week’s silence till
“ Prisoner of Zenda ” commences on the 17th.
The last night of “Matsa” is also the last stage
appearance of Miss Florence Young, who has put
on the gilded chains of matrimony and departs
for England with her husband to expose her ador-
able chest to the rigors of an inhospitable clime.
The Firm has resolved to fittingly express its
sorrow at the loss of this clever little lady at a
Saturday matinee, the programme consisting of
two acts of “Matsa” and one of “In Town.”
Fifteen acts wouldn’t have been too many to ex-
press what the public feel at Miss Young’s depar-
ture, but want of time makes it only possible to
give utterance to a small fraction of the general
woe.
** * *
The Palace having shared in the general
tendency to close up, Rickards’ company is now
concentrated at Sydney Tivoli. As the show this
week includes Edgar Granville ? Coleman, Alice
Leamar, Alma Grey, the Allisons, and those
especially clever artists, the three Delevines, it is
a more powerful combination than Rickards has
been represented by for some time, and, as the
city of the great slump will be actually down to
two theatres next week, it ought to coin money.
** * *
A new “ Silver King ” co., which proposes
to carry the softening and refining influences of
Dan’l Jaikes to the smaller towns, commences at
Newcastle on Easter Monday under the manage-
ment of Charles M‘Mahon, brother of the Mighty
Atom, and goes thence to Bendigo, Ballarat, and
other centres. It includes Scot Inglis as the King,
Inman, Sweeney, Misses Ida Hamilton and Maud
Appleton, and others. With this strong combina-
tion, Dan’l will be seen getting the shilling from
Bodgers in a more artistic fashion than he has yet
got it outside the capital cities.
#* # *
“Lelamine,” described as a French serio-
comic military opera, will get a whole week’s run
at Melb. Princess’s, commencing on May 8, unless
something happens to alter the present arrange-
ment. Alfred Moulton, who conducts the orches-
tra, is responsible for the music, and the libretto is
by Edouard Krusard, lately deceased in Jersey.
Less fortunate than Gov. Wobinson’s “Preda-
toros,” which had the assistance of Nellie Stewart
and the pick of the Firm’s forces, “ Lelamine ” is
to be played in the ominous name of Charity, by a
company of amateurs, and mostly sassiety ama-
teurs at that—Lord Shaftesbury, Col. Bingham,
C. S. Cunningham, F. E. Holroyd, and others.
His Lordship’s decent little voice has lost much of
its magnetism for the snobocracy by repeated
warblings on the concert platform, otherwise
“ Shaftesbury ” might have filled the house for at
least a couple of nights. Under the circumstances,
George Musgrove’s daughter, Rose, will probably
be the most interesting name on the bill, for it has
often been paragraphed in connection with per-
sonal attractions, and an unsatisfied ambition to
appear in public.
** * *
The half-crown musical entertainment at
Melb. Anglican Cathedral was not quite so im-
pressive as some of the free shows have been. An
orchestra and a number of female voices in the
choir brought a worldly feeling into the matter,
anyway. Also, the recently-published photo, of
murderer Phillips in a cassock and surplice has
weakened the seraphic effect of choir-boys. One
“ worshipper ” was observed to gaze earnestly at
the youngsters through opera-glasses, as though he
were looking for bloodstains on their hands.
** * *
“Mrs. Tanqueray,” at Melb. Bijou, last
week, must have disappointed everybody who
remembered the original B. and B. rendering of
Pinero’s great play. Mrs. Brough’s married Paula
is now a coarse, melodramatic person, artificial
alike in her explosions of wrath or attempts at
tenderness—an unlovable termagant suffering
from chronic dyspepsia. Possibly the audience
was to blame for the utter soullessness of Mrs.
Brough’s acting last Thursday night. A thick-
headed multitude in the amphitheatre treated
Paula’s tantrums as a joke, and laughed gleefully
on sundry occasions when she wrung her hands or
took the name of God in vain. A fine perform-
ance of such a play before such people as these
was not to be expected anyway, and furthermore
the leading lady had weaker support than of yore
from Cayley (Brough) and Ellean (Miss Hardy),
the step-daughter, in particular, being insuffi-
ciently cold and saintly. But O ! the difference
between this Mrs. Tanqueray, and the woman
whom Miss Olliffe depicted on her farewell night
at the Princess’s. Previously Miss Olliffe had
been less satisfactory than Mrs. Brough at many
points, but her one reappearance in the character
was an inspiration.
At Poverty Point,
A southern Guvment-’Ouse family, noted for economy
and kindred virtues, has just added another item to its
long list of “ thrifty ” deals. Vice-rovalty in that pro-
vince does not always do the local theatre on the nod, and
the other day the Governoress drove round to the box-
office to engage seats for the evening’s performance. The
usual tariff is two guineas every time the Governor’s box
is used, number of persons immaterial. As this was the
second occasion the show had been patronised by vice-
royalty, the management naturally expected Her Ex. to
hand in a cheque for four guineas. But she just asked
for five tickets and dumped down two sovereigns,
smilingly remarking that, as three persons had occupied
the box the other evening and five now made eight, at 5s
each, two pounds “will be exactly right, will it not?”
Of course, when the Firm’s polite voung man recovered
his breath he loyally replied in the affirmative.
What language are we amused in ? Some English words
picked from last week’s Sydney play-bills “ Nemesis,”
“Matsa,” “ Tivoli Matin6e,” ‘ Cinematographe,” “ Ront-
gen rays.”
The departure of Will Barnes for New York will give
this journal a few weeks’ rest from the drip, drip, drip of
laudatory paragraphs concerning that genius. Mr. Barnes,
who figures as chief costume-designer for the Firm, seems
to enlist the admiration of every penwoman in Australia,
and the combined female effort to make “Matsa” (for
which Barnes designed the ballet dresses) a means of
soaking Barnes into The Bulletin’s soul really deserves
credit for its pertinacity. It has been about the best sus-
tained effort in the history of unpublished contributions.
Haresfoot: “ I would like to see Titheradge as the
Vicar, Mrs. Brough as Olivia.” Why not do it at Mrs.
Brough’s farewell benefit?
One reason why Brough disbands is that Williamson
has Frohman’s co., in the same line, on the way from
America.
Melb. : “ The Alexandra seems to be gone for ever.
This week an auction has cleared out all the managerial
fittings, and left the place naught but a barn. The sym
posium affectionately fitted up by Dampier, since the
cave of Dan Barry and other monarchs, is as ugly as a
newspaper-office.”
Titheradge hangs pertinaciously to his cardinal error
as the abb(s in “ The Priest’s Secret.” He accents his
answer to the police-officer hunting Jean Torquenie, “I
give you my word no criminal is here,” in such a manner
that even the most dunder-headed “ cop ” could not fail
to detect the white lie in it.
Chirgwin is the most domesticated of music-hall stars,
and haunts St. Kilda sands or the park in company with
his family, a portion of which is usually contained in a
perambulator at which the white-hide Kaffir often takes a
hand. Chirgwin has modest tastes in everything barring
hats. By the way, isn’t the Kaffir’s “patter” just about
the worst ever heard, and the whistle with which he punc-
tuates it a gratuitous torture ? The man must be good
who survives the little prejudice created by such bald and
awful talk.
Alma Stanley, who accompanies Paulton to Australia,
was a professional beauty, rather than an actress, when
she first took to the stage.
Grey-headed Bellew and the galvanic Cora Potter
occupied the lower left stage-box at the Bijou on the first
night of the last revival of “Paula Tanqueray,” and at
each interval, when the lights had been turned high up,
the pair of beauties retired ostentatiously out of sight,
leaving their unconsequential companions to enjoy the
adoration of the gods. These removals suggested the
idea that Bellew and Cora had failed to induce Brough to
part a percentage of the takings, and consequently' de-
clined to officiate as a spectacular effect ’tween acts. Of
course they can quote distinguished precedents : the fat
girl, and the dwarf, and the living skeleton, from the
museums, are always careful to avoid the vulgar eye
when the said eye has not qualified at the pay-box.
Re, the general idea that Knowles is always the same
sort of breathless funny-man, a correspondent testifies to
the intense seriousness of Knowles, behind the scenes, at
the time when the Sydney Palace Theatre suspended
business. On that occasion he was every inch a Hamlet,
looking for the “ ghost.”
Scot Inglis and Harry Roberts lead the way for the
coming crowd of Australian young men actors.
It would gladden “ Matsa” Gulliver to hear some of the
juvenile remarks concerning him. A very small girl
amused those near her at a recent matinee by a running-
fire of exclamations: “Oh, there’s that dear little boy
again!” “Oh, mamma, I do love to watch that dear
little fellow. Isn’t he clever?”—and “Oh, isn’t he a
sweet little pet ? ”
Bernhardt hits Paris hard in Sardou’s “ Spiritisme.” A
husband imagines his wife dead. A medium brings her
ghost before him, but, of course, it is herself, and this
hollow-looking situation is managed with consummate
art.
Haresfoot : “ Williamson rises from the table in all but
disgust over the Melb. play season, and is inclined to
throw up the cards, as regards drama, with the success
of ‘Chinatown’ and ‘Matsa’ in his eye. ‘They want
variety,’ says he, and he is the potentate who can say by
Jingo they shall have it.”
Theorbo : “ Signor Cecchi’s shoes are languidly
struggled for in Melb. The teaching profession, voice and
piano, is at a low ebb, nothing comparable to the standard
of ten years ago. This is from the current genteel
poverty, which naturally affects first of all the purveyors
of luxury.”
The first Cinenratographe which ever struck Maoriland
was taken around there recently by Brother Joe M‘Mahon
on a six months’ tour, and did record business. All the
same a very much disgusted M'Mahon has now brought
the machine back, and is opening his show —a first-
rate one, by the way —in Bendigo on Easter Monday.
Maoriland is a pleasant enough place so far as the large
towns are concerned. In the small ones there are gene-
rally one or, at most, t o hotels, and as the Cold Tea
crowd has managed to make it pretty certain that no new
licenses will be granted, the monopolistic pub-keeper is
often haughty, and the girl who hands round the hash is
unbearable. Her general attitude is that “ that ’ere thing
on the plate is mutton, an’ if you asked for roast beef I
didn’t hear you, an’ if you don’t like the mutton you’d,
better go an’board somewhere else.” Then she guffaws
and goes away.
The new Theatre Royal, Perth (W.A.), opens with
“ Silver King,” for which several members of the dis-
banded Westmacott company have been engaged. Pre-
sently the Firm sends a comic opera co. along, so it is
probable that the six reigning families of the Westraliau
capital will have to take a chorus girl or two into their
little plutocratic circle ere the rainy season sets in.
Francie Adler, a grand-daughter of Madame Simonsem
who will take Flora Graupner’s part in “Matsa,” is no
taller than her petite predecessor. Australia would seem
to be producing a race of singing midgets. Even Gulliver
has a good voice for his inches.
Purves, Q.C., plays Sergeant Buzfuz without any ap-
parent sense of humor, but the performance he gave last
Friday was less seriously matter-of-fact than his first
attempt, which happened at Melb. Royal some years ago.
As the trial scene from “ Pickwick ” is likely to be an
annual feature of Dramatic-Fund benefits, Purves stands
a chance to grow into a low-comedian by degrees.
A recently imported actor supplies Melbourne with a
feast of color on fine afternoons. Bright brown coat and
pants, and fancy waistcoat to match ; light grey felt hat,
pink and white shirt, blue and white tie, and a bunch of
violets in his buttonhole. Harmonious.
Coleman is to play the part of the Irish spinster (vice
Mrs. Lauri) when “Matsa’’opens in Adelaide, at Easter.
This change in the cast will tend to strengthen the
family resemblance between “Matsa” and “Djin-Djin”
of yore.
When a sassiety lady takes to the stage there is no more
hope for the unprofessional johnnies who used to dance
attendance on her. Any gaudy mummer of the co., with
a mind to do a “ mash,” can be trusted to take the shine
out of them p.d.q., which, being interpreted, means
pretty dam quick. It has happened in a recent notable
case, where the ill-dissembled agony of the forsaken
cavalier should serve as a warning to all young men of the
bouquet-bestowing class. What’s the good in sendirm- a
floral tribute to your escaped goddess, if the prettiest
blossom in the bunch is fated to adorn the coat of a grease-
painted rival ?
One of the many Cine, shows has been taken on tour
among the gold and sugar fields of the North, and is
heralded by a glowing programme stating that the
machine is making its first appearance in Queensland
after delighting thousands in Sydney and Brisbane and
enjoying the patronage of “ H.R.H. the Governor of
N.S.W.”
According to a London paper, Blondin—who was 74 at
the time of his death—began his public career when only
four years of age. His real name was Francois de
Gravelet, and his father was one of the great Napoleon’s
most trusted soldiers.
Olga Nethersole, who got married in New York the
other day, will not be surfeited with domestic bliss, accord-
ing to all accounts. Her husband, Stewart Oliver is
Surgeon-Superintendent of the Indian Emigration De-
partment (whatever that may be), and bis professional
duties keep him in India for at least half the vear : the
other six months or so he will pass in the fair Olga’s com-
pany, if both parties are agreeable. The happy pair took
a homoeopathic dose of honeymoon to begin with, after
which the doctor rushed away to superintend emigration
in the East, whilst his bride bustled off to fulfil an engage-
ment in the West. Perhaps the gentleman suffered least
from this cruel separation. Judging from various
criticisms of Miss Nethersole as Carmen, her famous
clingsome kiss will give the recipient quite enough joy to
go on with.
Dear B.,—Your correspondent, “ 5.,” questions the fair-
ness of a writer who “cannot condemn” the hospital
nurse’s performance of Mrs. Maesmore Morris in “Two
Little Vagabonds,” but who “ frigidly discounts its merits
by assuming all to be due to arctic impudence.” Surely
the writer complained of paid the lady a fair compliment
in saying that, “up to a certain point, and under the
neoessarity calm conditions, there is no visible difference
between cold, natural ‘ cheek ’ and the art which conceals
art.” “ S.” asserts that Mrs. M. M. “ acts in a manner
that would not discredit an actress of talent and long ex-
perience.” That’s his opinion. I would rather maintain
that the fair novice isn’t called upon to act a little bit in
the professional sense of the term. Again, “S.” says,
“ As for the assertion that she will only do well * as long
as there is nothing particular to be done,’ it is easier to
win honor in one’s own country by acting than by pro-
phesying.” Which amounts to a prophecy by “ S.” that
the other writer’s prophecy will prove incorrect. Leave
time to decide the question.-
PATIENCE.
The Fiscal Ghost.
The inevitable fiscal question has turned up in the
Federal Convention. It is agreed that Customs
and Excise shall be the chief sources of Federal
revenue, and the amount to be raised by these
means will depend mostly on the amount of ex-
penditure to be provided for. The Protectionists
desire to make the Federal Government respon-
sible for a large proportion of the general expendi-
ture, which means that it must raise a large
revenue out of the Customs to make ends meet.
The Freetrade contingent, or rather a small part of
it—principally Premier Reid —desires to make the
Provincial Governments responsible for almost all
the expenditure, and to charge so very little to the
Federal Government that it must needs abolish
nearly all the duties, or else have a huge surplus.
As the Federal Government won’t, according to
present ideas, have charge of railway construction,
it can’t use its surplus in building new lines ; and
no provision is made for using it to reduce the
public debt, and the Federal Government can’t
legally drink the surplus, so it is reckoned it must
abolish nearly all the Customs duties to get rid of it.
If the Freetrade crowd sticks to this idea, a dead-
lock is absolutely certain, because N.S.W., by
reason of its huge land revenue, is the only
one of the older provinces which could keep out
of insolvency by the proposed method. Under
the system advocated by Reid and a few others
Victoria, for instance, would give up a little over
.£2,000,000 of customs and excise revenue, but
instead of being relieved of £2,000,000 of expendi-
ture it would be relieved of only about £1,000,000
or possibly less. Therefore, it would have to raise
an extra £1,000,000 by direct taxation to square
the provincial accounts. In Victoria there is
already a heavy income-tax, a limited State land-
tax, a heavy general land-tax levied for local
government purposes and heavy probate duties; so,
taking things all round, the raising of that extra
million looks a very awful problem indeed. New
South Wales would also probably have to raise an
extra million per annum by direct taxation
to squaife its provincial accounts, and by extend-
ing the local government system throughout the
province and thus making the local government
land - tax universal, it could provide probably
£600,000 of it in one act. N.S.W., having
£2,000,000 a year of land revenue against only
£400,000 in Vic. and £200,000 in S.A., has been
able to get on hitherto without a general land-
tax for local government purposes—over 99 per
cent, of the country pays no such tax ; and it has
this resource still to fall back upon, while Victoria
in particular has not. The Foreign-trade party’s
proposals would, therefore, make Federation in
the case of Victoria, and to some extent
in that of S. A. and Tasmania, equivalent
to provincial insolvency. The two parties
don’t talk about Freetrade and Protection, but
the one crowd wants to make the burdens of the
Federal Treasury so light that to all intents and
purposes it must abolish the duties on almost
everything, and the other crowd tries to make
them heavy partly to keep up the duties, and
partly because it is apparently the only way to
keep Vic., S.A. and Tasmania out of bank-
ruptcy. And Premier Reid’s advocacy of a policy
which will make union practically impos-
sible for two, if not three, of the provinces looks
like another attempt to wreck Federation on a
side issue.
A smart system of letter-indexing (inrto-
ducedby Mr Horace Grayson, of Mammatt’s electro-
plated goods agency) which was lately adopted in
the N.S.W. income-tax department, has now been
taken up by the slowgoing Vic. Titles Office.
Grayson’s ingenious index book is said to effect a
saving of 19| minutes in each hunt for particulars
of a land registration, as compared with previous
devices, therefore the clerks in the Titles Office
enjoy largely-increased opportunities for picking
their teeth.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N.S.W. revenue for the first nine months
of the financial year (to 31st March) is £109,417 less than
for the corresponding period of last year. At the end of
December, however, the six months’ revenue was £228,074
behind that for the corresponding six months of ’95, so
things have improved greatly since then, and if the re-
venue keeps on increasing at the present rate it will fully
equal last year’s total. The deficit is the interesting
feature ; the nine months’ revenue is £6,717,394 ; expen-
diture, £7,135,724; deficit, £418,330. Under these cir-
cumstances it will take a lot of economy, and hard faking,
and postponement of liabilities till next year, or else a
wholly unexpected outbreak of prosperity, to produce a
surplus, or even to make ends meet.
During the nine months N.S.W. has spent
£1,074,755 of borrowed money, most of it on small, casual,
everyday jobs which yield no revenue whatever, or next
to none. The one tradition to which the N.S.W. Govern-
ment sticks with deadly earnestness is that it is neces-
sary to get from £1,000,000 to £1,500,000 further in debt
every year, and that the debt must always keep increas-
ing a trifle faster than the population. And in view of
this steady, pottering process of piling up liabilities, and
of charging posterity with the bill for wood-blocking
streets, and repairing old roofs, and replacing decayed
wharves, and for roads, bridges, statues, gunpowder
and cartridges and all manner of non-productive
and perishable sundries, Tub Bulletin respectfully
asks the Government of N.S.W., and several
other Governments, if they have any distinct idea
where they are going to? Also, it rises as a voice
crying in the wilderness to make the following sugges-
tions —for the billionth time or so : (1) That N.S.W.,
being by its own statistics and its own assertions the
richest country in the world, should pay its own way, and
stop borrowing from countries said to be much poorer
than itself, and be independent and self-reliant, and cease
to be a grovelling beggar at the door of the British
capitalist ; (2) that as its foreign loans fall due they
should be renewed locally—if they must be renewed ;
(3) that a sinking fund, however small, should be
started for the gradual extinction of the public debt.
Or if the country has got the borrowing complaint so
badly that it must borrow, this paper proposes the
following three innovations : (1) That all borrowing shall
be done locally ; (2) That borrowing shall only be per-
missible for works yielding a direct and constant revenue
—not for gaols, asylums, statues, museums, bridges,
roads, repairs to old structures, forest-thinning, scrub-
cutting, sarid-shifting, wood-blocking streets, and such
like rubbish ; (3) That the amount of debt per inhabitant
must decrease at the rate of (say) 2s. per annum—that is
to say, if the debt at the end of '97 is £5O 10s. per inhabi-
tant the amount borrowed during the next year must be
so proportioned to the increase of population that the
debt at the beginning of ’9B shall be only £5O Bs. At the
ordinary rate of increase this would still allow a very
respectable margin for loans, and it would at least be a
guarantee that the burden per inhabitant was growing
smaller instead of larger.
* * * •
Comparing the situation of five provinces : The
N.S.W. revenue for the nine months just completed is, as
above-mentioned, £109,417 less than for the correspond-
ing nine months of previous year. The Vic. revenue is
£130,505 better than for nine months of previous year ;
S.A. revenue is £77,190 better ; Queensland revenue is
£1879 better. Westralia’s revenue for the last three
months is £276,927 more than for the corresponding
quarter of ’96, and the increase for the nine months is
something like £700,000. It is possible now that Westra-
lia, with not much more than one-third the population,
will show a bigger revenue for the year than Queensland.
» * * •
The Vic. State Land Bank floated its second
loan of £20,000 at 3 per cent, with great difficulty. The
public subscribed £3200 only, and the Savings Bank put
up the other £16,800. The moral is that, when the
Savings Bank gets tired and won’t underwrite the loans
any longer, either the Land Bank will have to raise its
interest to 3J, or else it will have to make the loans a fix-
ture for at least 20 years—probably 30. At present the
Bank has the option of repayment at any time, and
people who take up 3 per cent, securities at par want to
know that their money is safely invested for a long period.
The fact that it can be handed back to them in a month
or two, and they may have to scout round for new in-
vestments, ruins the business. There is plenty of 3 per
cent, money to be got, but not on these precarious con-
ditions.
• * * *
At the beginning of this year there were 68 fire
and marine insurance companies doing business in Vic-
toria, and only 22 of them were Australasian affairs. The
underwriting absentee is a great institution.
• * * *
More alleged horrors concerning the Ward
Farmers’ Association (Maoriland) keep coining to light.
One strong point made in the preliminary inquiry was
that ex-Treasurer Ward had fixed matters in so foolishly
honest a way that profits made went to the Associa-
tion, while losses were often debited to Ward per-
sonally. The liquidator says that instead the Associa-
tion bore all its own losses, and some of Ward’s as well.
It is averred that during its brief and stormy career the
Association discounted 174 more or less bogus drafts
for £llB,OOO altogether, apparently to make its account
look right on balancing-day. In other words, if the Asso-
ciation owed the bank £25,000 it would, just before the
bank’s half-yearly balance, discount a draft upon John
Smith or somebody for £24,500, and the proceeds would
be put to its credit. Then the Colonial Bank officials
could tell their shareholders, if necessary, that Ward’s
busted show was only overdrawn £5OO, which was techni-
cally true; and a day or two later the useless draft would
be cancelled, and the overdraft would be £25,000 as be-
fore. Ward’s own huge debt to the Association seems to
have been faked the same way ; the idea was simply,
when balancing time came, to charge £50,000 or so of
Ward’s debt to anybody whose account could stand it,
and then, if any shareholder had asked how much Mr.
Ward owed the Association, it would have been a per-
fectly square thing for the chairman to say that Mr.
Ward’s account in the books was £l6 in credit, and that
the low-minded insinuator who asked the question was
doubtless a political opponent or a base impostor of some
kind.
Afterwards the debits and credits would be
shuffled round, and Ward would owe the same old
£50,000 once more. -The liquidators’ yarn is practically
to the effect that the Ward Farmers’ Association and its
pal, the Colonial Bank, were a long continuation of lies,
suppressions, evasions, underhand dodges, false balance-
sheets, Barabbas and gas-meter. After reporting about
40 different shady transactions of one kind and another
he remarks significantly that the Association started with
£lBB3 7s. 6d. available cash, and its first act was to issue
a cheque for £13,941 in payment for goods of various
kinds bought from Ward. Its second act, apparently,
was to lend Ward £3OOO with which to speculate in wool.
The capital was then £1245, so Ward seemingly had no
sooner started the Association, and sold his business and
stock to it at a good figure, than he borrowed all the
money it had and nearly all it could raise as well. And
he also, it would appear, kept on borrowing as long as the
miserable institution lasted, till it and he and the Colonial
Bank of Maoriland went down together.
• * • •
The Bank of Victoria announces that it will
pay off another instalment of its reconstructed deposits
on 12th July. This will make about 60 per cent, of the
total amount set free.
* • * *
The London Bank of Australia’s reconstruction
scheme has been further modified in the House of Lords,
and it now reads that the minimum rate of interest pay-
able on the old deposits shall be reduced to 3 per cent.,
but that the depositors shall have a first lien on the profits
until they get their full 4J per cent. Under these circum-
stances there are only two courses open to the London :
to get ahead of the reconstructed depositors in the mean-
time oy writing off as much of the profits (over and above
the 3 per cent.) as possible to cover depreciation of securi-
ties—the securities can be written up again later on, but
it is advisable to consider them from a low-spirited and
pessimistic standpoint in the meantime ; and to pay off
the said depositors with all possible rapidity, as it is evi-
dent the shareholders will have no show worth mention-
ing until they are paid off. A very depressed manager
who could contrive to feel utterly hopeless about the
assets might save the London Bank £30,000 a year under
the latest new arrangement.
• • * •
The Farmers’ Co-operative Fire and Marine In-
surance Co., of Canterbury (M.L.), shows a net premium
income of £4077 against £3376 for ’95, and a profit on in-
surance of £496 against £1406 in ’95. Interest on invest-
ments brings in £2981, so the year’s profit is £3477, and
the proportion of this which comes out of the actual in-
surance business is so small as hardly to be worth men-
tioning. A 5 per cent, dividend and 10 per cent, bonus
to policyholders absorb £3156. As the profits of the
insurance business are only £496, and the co. pays £714
bonus to policyholders, it would really be £2lB better off
this year if it had done no business at all but had simply
lain low and kept a hawk-like eye on its investments.
* * * *
The Mutual Life Insurance Co. of New York
finished 1896 in a blaze of glory. The year’s income was
£10,499,059, being £520,000 more than the figures for ’95,
and £2,778,321 was added to the assets, which now total
£48,202,083. The surplus of assets over liabilities is
£588,696 more than it was a year ago, and now stands at
£6,105,444 ; over 5)4 million pounds were paid to policy-
holders during the year, and the total insurance in force
amounts to nearly £189,000,000. These are the sort of
figures which are not run against every day, and people
begin to wonder where an institution of this kind will
stop. Suppose it goes on till all the earth belongs to the
Mutual Life of New York—what will happen afterwards ?
* * * *
A Sydney paper reports that in the two suburbs
of Redfern and Erskineville (Sydney) there are now 616
empty buildings out of 5212 in all. Wanted, a complete
official report of the empty tenements of Sydney, Mel-
bourne, and a few other towns.
* • * *
The Cheque Bank, Limited, shows a net profit
for 1896 of £6904, compared with £14,172 for 1895. A 6 per
cent dividend on ordinary and 7£ per cent, on preference
shares absorb £5759, and the balance raises the amount at
credit of profit and loss to £3583. The Cheque Bank is a
small British concern with £86,050 paid-up shares and
only £5OOO reserve fund. Out of its assets (£451,664),
£131,320 consists of a guarantee fund, which seems to be
simply unpaid capital: and £48,859 is “balance of pur-
chase of Cheque Banks of 1873 and 1876,” which also
doesn’t look like a realisable asset, and seems to be some-
thing in the nature of a goodwill that is undergoing a
very gradual process of writing off. Leaving out/these
two items, the visible assets of the bank are about
£271,000.
* * * *
Harrold Bros., the big Adelaide firm which re-
cently went insolvent, mainly owed its failure to the
much-boomed Norseman field. It practically owned
six mines in that district. Harrold, M.P., the head of the
firm, will lose his Assembly seat as a result of the failure.
He was, until recently, President of the local Ass.

papers were sent to gaol. I don’t mean all in a batch,

The present state of the drapery department of
a very big Melb. establishment will probably provide a
first-class tumult when the shareholders get together
again. Some £3OOO “ worth ” of unsaleable stuff is piled
around in miniature “ Mount Despairs,” and share-
holders, primed with alleged facts, will warit to know,
firstly, where the colossal “parcel” came from; and
secondly, whether it was in any degree forced upon the

A new company dodge in marvellous Melbourne
is to turn your cronk concern into a proprietary co. and
thus get the benefit of the limited liability system without
the necessity of publishing balance-sheets; and to take
deposits as usual, although forbidden to do so, by the
naive device of giving a bill of exchange payable on de-
mand instead of a deposit-receipt. Will it wash ?

Several insurance policies on N.S.W. publio
buildings lapsed the otherdayowingto premiums not being
renewed. The State its own insurer, was accepted by

* * * *
The latest thing among advertisers who engage
office-boys at a salary of a few shillings a week, is to keep
in hand a week’s wages—forfeited if they leave without
notice. When the boys hear of a better situation they
generally have to start at once, hence the few shillings in
hand are a sure thing for the skinflint who employs them.

April opened with silver at 2s. 4 5-16 d. Two
years ago it went a fraction below this ; but apart from
that the present is the lowest quotation on record.

B.H. Junction purposes erecting a 3000-ton
concentrating plant—a wise move, remembering the dis-
proportionate extraction of shipping ore hitherto.
800 ft, developments in the South are again
disappointing, poor and hard faces showing. Latest

* * * *
Junction Norths were knocked out by the
announcement that there were smaller quantities of
shipping ore than had been supposed to exist, milling
ore displacing the shipping quality. Both the Junction
and Junction North occupy a bend in the lode, and
regularity of ore deposits cannot reasonably be expected
there.

Texas Silver Spur (near Stanthorpe) is looking
up. Last year 3186 tons gave 122,4210 z. silver, 59 tons
copper, and 1750 z. gold. It was decided lately to erect a
plant to treat sulphide ore occurring in lower lodes.
* * * *
After two months’ silence, it transpired at the
shareholders’ meeting that the last crushing of the Great
Western, Cobar, N.S. W., was only 2dwt. per ton, and the
excuse given for non-publication was that it was “so
poor” they were ashamed to let it be known. However,
it is now known that the pay-streak in the ore-body was
gutted out before the co.’s own plant was erected, that
the mine has been left in a dilapidated condition, and the
manager has his work cut out to find ore worth putting
through the mill. Altogether it is a fair average sample
of N.S.W. mining.
* » * *
Stated on good authority that the Cobar copper-
mine was worked last year at a profit of £97,000 ; also,
that £90,000, cash against scrip, was offered for the share-
interest of the tributors in the mine, which interest is
not quite one-half. Though the offer was subject to the
tribute running the full term, it was declined. Also
rumored that the tributors are so satisfied with the result
of smelting at Lithgow that they contemplate the erection
of much larger works, at a cost of £40,000, with the object
of dealing with most of the copper ores of the western
districts.
* * * *
Rev. J. Campbell, at one time incumbent of
Glen Innes, has, it would appear, practically formed in
London a syndicate of half-a-ntillion to work his much-
talked-of new process for treating ores. He intimates by
private letter that the scene of his first operations will
be somewhere in the New England district.
* * * *
Water-jacket copper-smelting has begun at
Nymagee, N.S.W. Looks as if the Oobar tributors are
going to follow up one big success with another.
* * * *
Melbourne Age : “It is very remarkable how
‘ distance lends enchantment to the view.’ No one would
proceed to Daylesford or Omeo on the strength of finds
equal to those in New Guinea, although there are here no
dangers of pestilence or ferocious natives. There has
not been even a single sensational discovery made in New
Guinea. At present there are four parties in the interior,
including about a dozen men on the Upper Mambare, ap-
parently at work chiefly on the M‘Laughlin Creek. They
were understood by latest reports to be getting but little
gold. No results had come from the other parties,
though they had been out for some time. Sir William
Macgregor says it will be a surprise to him if all of them
come back alive. It is only a sort of mania which w’ould
induce a rush to such a countrjq especially from Victoria,
where valuable deposits are discovered every day and will
continue to be discovered for many years by those who
intelligently and diligently seek them.”
* « * *
There is a wild rush of “ mining experts ” for
the billet of Vic. Govt, mining representative in England.
Each auriferous centre in the province is running its own
man, but Sebastopol Town Council, which backs one
Stapleton, barracks for its candidate in the freshest and
most ingenuous style. In a circular addressed to other
councils it says : “ He (Mr. S.) has ability and aptitude
in giving forcible and pleasing expression to his opinions.”
A first-class mining drummer, in fact. Unfortunately,
persons of this~sort are no longer a novelty in London.
The inhabitants of that township have seen them before.

At Queenstown diggings, only 27 miles from
Melbourne, three men have cleared each £l2OO in 19
months past.
* * » *
A couple of miners prospecting at Avoca, Vic.,
struck human bones at 17ft., and leisurely w’ashed a
prospect from amongst the remains. Result, about sdwt.
to the dish —that is, one sovereign dated 1850. Strangely
enough, no attempt is to be made to float the mine in
Melbourne on the strength of this yield.
• * * •
From a letter written by a London director after
a tour through Westralian goldfields “ The rubbish
that one has seen placed on the English market, arrd the
amount of English capital ruthlessly wasted to eni-ich a
few unprincipled promoters, is enough to disgust English
investors with West Australia. If responsible English
directors were to travel in the country wherein they have
interests, things would be done in a much more satis-
factory way. The truth is they will not take the trouble,
and are entirely led by the reports of men in whom they
repose misplaced trust. I was the first English director
seen in the backblocks, and of course heard a great deal.
Anything and everything is considered right in W.A."
whereby money can be made in the shortest possible time.
If English capitalists had subscribed some of the money
to assist in working the mines in the other provinces they
would have had much better return. . . . Good money,
and plenty of it, is often put into ‘ salt bush ’ flats, and
the genuinely good properties are ruined for want of
capital. In Bendigo and Ballarat the mines are run
by local men and by local capital. They never thought
of appealing to the mother country for assistance
to develop their mines, nor to English investors to send
them money and capital. The very machinery used is
made in the district, and so constructed as to be specially
adapted to that particular part of the country. In W.A.,
on the other hand, all the capital is English, and it made
my blood boil to hear the remarks about the idiocy of
‘ John Bull ’ to find so much money for useless work, and
expressions were made use of on every side of ‘ serve the
Englishmen right.’ . . . It is not the promoter, nor
the syndicate, nor the original prospector who suffers.
They all get their share, but it is the poor unfortunate
shareholder who suffers, and it is this dishonesty, so ap-
parent throughout the length and breadth of W.A.,
which is doing so much to ruin a country in which there
are undoubtedly some excellent properties. In Bendigo
4dwt. stone, which has to be hauled over 2000 ft., can be
put through at a profit. Compare this with W.A.,
where there are very few mines indeed which can be
worked very much under an ounce and make a profit.
People will tell you that 8 and 10 dwt. can be made to
pay in W.A., but it is very, very rarely true.” This sort
of thing is much too true to receive publication in the
Liars’ Country.
• * * *
From London to The Bulletin:—“You have
no idea how utterly demoralised the mining-market is
here. There is next to no business doing in shares, and I
am sure it would be easier to raise capital in Australia
than London just now. Most of the W.A. financial co’s
are hard-up for money, and find it impossible to realise
on their paper. Every settlement sees a depreciation of
an eighth to a quarter in most things. Some say there
will be a revival in the spring, though I do not count
much on it.”

What has become of the famous General Gordon
mine, supposed to be in Westralia? Silence too often
occurs when the battery is about to speak in all
languages. A meek attempt was made a few days ago to
revive the same old game —a director had been up to the
mine and was satisfied (we never hear anything else of
any director) the battery would start in a few days.
Allison Smith, the handsome and dignified ex-loco-
engineer of Vic., now consulting engineer of the G.G.,
said at a recent shivoo on the mine that the battery
would soon tell the tale. How does it come that, in the
face of all the puff, its shares are down to 65., or about £1
less than when the battery was a long way off ?
♦ * * *
Richard Speight, the Victorian ex-Commis-
sioner of Vic. railways, has joined the lists of Wes-
tralian company-promoters. At present he is busy re a
big two-million tramway to be run from Newcastle, a
hamlet 60 or 70 miles from Perth, to the north of the
Menzies fields, running through the Mt. Ida and Mouline
country, if only Bull can be persuaded that there is any-
thing in it. Speight is consulting-engineer, and draws
screw at the rate of £lOOO a year. The business is only
in syndicate stage at present, and it is said the Forrests
have been roped in. One of the syndicate, with that
lively “scion of a noble race,” young Dilke, under his
wing, left for London some time ago. The idea Is to bring
ore to the water, and in this way it is just the opposite of
the Government’s theory of taking water to'the ore.
When both schemes get going there will be enough ore-
treating appliances in Westralia to cope with all the
ore raised in Australia and South Africa.
* * * *
W.A. wire: “The manager of the Ivanhoe
mine has pegged out 70 acres in the vicinity of the co.’s
water-right, an action supposed to have been prompted
by developments not yet made public.” Pegged for the
co., no doubt. There was a time in Westralia when
managers, of a different stamp to Hewitson, under such
circs, pegged for themselves.
'*» * *
Most of the mining registrars and wardens on the
W.A. fields,although expressly prohibited from doingso, not
only accept, but lay themselves out for, “ insults” in the
way of paid-up interests in good shows. One well-known
bacchanalian Warden has been heard to declare in his
cups that, although he only gets somewhere about £4OO
per annum, he made close on £lOOO by “ diplomacy.” A
large proportion of officials in W.A., from the highest to
the lowest grade, are cashiered civil servants from the
east.

That “ splendid meeting,” in Melbourne, of the
Queen Margaret barrackers doesn’t seem to have excited
a wild rush for shares in the forthcoming shower of gold.

A mining engineer came to Westralia to supen-
intend the ereetion of plant. His careful examination of
pieces of ore prompted “What’s the caper?” from the
boss. “I believe,” said the engioeah, “there are two
kinds of reefs in W.A.—the male and the female —and I
want, if possible, to get at the sex of this one.” “ I think
you will find that nine out of ten are bucks,” replied the
boss.
* * * •
Kauffman being out of the country, and the
glory and gorgeousness of George Gray being turned
down, if not put right out, George Brookman is boss
of Westralia. He is now organising a £200,000 co. to
build smelting works at Fremantle, has secured some
copper and lead mines, and is building a fine pub. at
Albany a’nd lots of other things too trifling to name.
Brookman, landing at Albany a few weeks ago, was not
satisfied with the rooms available at the best hotel, so
walked down to the station, ordered a special train, and
left for Perth —cost, £l5O. At the same time Premier
Forrest, after expressing his willingness to take a shake-
down in the crowded hotel, shared for several nights
a small room with a N.S.W. practical miner, who
availed himself of the magnificent chance to curse Cat-
land.
* * * •
Thus the Rialto (London), on W.A. finance
companies:—“Promotion being dead, the finance co.’s
have nothing to look forward to in the shape of profits
but the development of the mines they have floated. It
is within the mark to say that two-thirds of the W.A.
mines floated on the London market are absolutely worth-
less, and that the public have paid pounds instead of
pence for them ; also, that there has been more robbery
going on at the mines themselves than ever occurred even
in South Africa. Many of the mining managers would,
by their sayings and doings, put poor old Ananias to the
blush. The directors in Londhn have purchased proper-
ties on the report of a mining Ananias ; they have
remitted money for development work which may or may
not have been spent in that direction by Barabbas, their
manager ; they have wasted money in useless machinery
on the joint advice of Ananias and Barabbas ; and when
their bank-account became dry, they discovered at the
same time that the property they purchased, and on
which they have spent so much money, is not worth—
vulgarly speaking— 1 twopenn’orth-o’-gin.’ There are, of
course, exceptions, but you can count them on your
fingers. This year, which we have been looking forward
to with so much hope, and in which we were all going to
make our fortunes, will be, as far as W.A. is concerned,
nothing more nor less than a year of liquidations. We
must not shut our eyes to it. It is as sure to come as
indigestion follows pork chops.” The pork-chop boom !

According to Q. mining wardens the gross local
product in ’96 was 638,2120 z. The actual exports pan
out like this:—
Gold in bars and dust, 626,9300 z. ... £2,114,257
Gold ore, 66 tons ... ... ... 1,078
Gold slag, 2 tons ... ... ... 350
£2,115,685
The valuation per ounce is under £3 10s. The Queensland
output ought, therefore, to be reduced one-eighth, before
a proper comparison can be made with, say, Vic. gold-
yield. But even the Vic. gold-yield is not authoritatively
valued by anyone. In olden days, the valuation used to
average well over £4 —but it has lately been reduced by
the low quality of much of the Gippsland gold.

Managing Engineer Badger, of Brisbane tram-
ways, who comes from the States, and says all he wants to
by eloquent grimaces and a judicious use of “ wal,” seems
to know enough about electric tramway systems to be
able to juggle with the subject. He wants someone to
find the money to erect a huge turbine on the upper
Brisbane which will generate sufficient electricity to light
Brisbane and Ipswich, work all the factories and tram-
ways—in fact, shunt steam and gas altogether.

Pearler Clark, of Thursday Island, tr,ying to
find a market for opal, took £BOO worth of the gem to
’Frisco the other day, and declared the value to the
Customs officer at 4000dol. Customs expert valued the
stones at 8500d01., they were seized, but on reap-
praisement by - outside experts, reduced to 4400d01.
Another IOOdoI. and Clark would have been fined and
would have lost his opals. The market for opals is poor
(says Clark), and he sold 901 b. for £725. Clark asserts,
further, that most Queensland alleged emeralds now
offering are really sapp hires.

to London. MTlwraith, M‘Eacharn & Co. lately reported

nounced good. As the current value of rubber is front
2s. 2d. to 2s. 4d. a Ip., there is a big thing looming ahead
for somebody.

Society
The characteristic Australian opinion about
that record reign is expressed in the report of
proceedings of Mount Morgan Town Council—
A letter was read from Secretary, Associated bocal
Authorities Association, to the effect that the association
intended to signify their loyalty and attachment to the
throne and person by presenting an address to the Queen
on the occasion of her having reigned 60 years, and asking
the Council to join in. The cost would be about lCs. 6d.,
but not more than £1 Is. Aid. Whitehead thought it
was cheap at the price, and Mount Morgan ought to keep
up with the times. Seconded by Aid. Flint, and sup-
ported by Aid. Matthews, who said he was a loyal subject,
and carried.
Exactly. We are loyal subjects at “about 10s. 6d.;
but not more than £1 Is.”—by Emu !

One morning last week Melb. Argus burst
forth with large-typed announcements that the
local jubilee memorial would take the form of a
fever hospital, and that Lady Brassey was appeal-
ing for subscriptions, and that the Mayor held
“strong views ”on the subject. For the next 24
AT THE SALE YARDS.
“Look here, old man, that cow you sold me was no good; she won’t give any milk. ”
DEALER: “ When did you try to milk her?”
“ Last Sunday; she kicked me in the ribs besides.”
DEALER: “No wonder she didn’t give any milk; didn’t I tell you when you bought her not
to milk her on the Sabbath? Why, the cow was a blessed Ranter! ”
hours the Argusian hysteria remained unaccount-
able. People not “in the know ” were asking one
another why this unexciting charitable scheme —
which had been put forward by baronet Clarke’s
wife, long before Lady Brassey smiled upon it, or
the Mayor’s “ views ” were advertised—should be
suddenly magnified into a gigantic event. Then
came the explanation. The proprietors of the
Argus had decided to give 1000 guineas to the
new hospital fund.
In the contest of munificence between Mel-
bourne’s rival newspaper-proprietors, David Syme
may consider himself beaten. He devotes ,£2OOO
to the encouragement of military sport by sending
a team of volunteer shooters to England. The
undemocratic nature of David’s gift is emphasised
by the fact that squatters Pursy Chirnside and
Staughtqn are likewise providing large and ex-
pensive picnics for men-in-buckram. The oracle
of Liberalism and the two hard-shell Tories figure
as three souls with but a single'thought, three
hearts that beat as one. But the Argus manage-
ment presents itself as a practical pal to the
working-classes by handsomely aiding the endow-
ment of a norspital.

Gov, Buxton’s pretty and English-looking
daughter appears to take a .lively interest in
Federation. She has attended the Convention
sittings with her father every time the old gentle-
man has been there, and, notebook on knee, has
jotted down gems of eloquence from the lips of
the talking men. Waxcellency Madden’s plump
and pretty daughters also spent a couple of days
at the Convention while in Adelaide on their way
’Ome.
* * * *
Governor Brassey leaves not a detail of
Govt. House management to anybody but him-
self. The mere domestic details of the place are
superintended by H.E., and scarcely anything at
all is left to the management of his spouse. The
old man is as great an authority as to the amount
of suet that goes to a boiled pudding as concern-
ing the armament of battle-ships, and he knows
as much of soups and sausages as of statistics and
blue-books.
* * *
A Sydney palmist shows a letter alleged to
be in the handwriting of the lady-Governor of an
Australian province, complimenting the anaemic
vein-reader on the accuracy of her diagnosis and
assuring her that her revelations have been “help-
ful to me in the highest degree.” “Helpful”—
that is somehow a very English-fashionable word.
* * * *
Two warrants for the arrest of Assistant-
Paymaster Craddock, of H.M.S. Katoomba, were
issued—one of them for desertion. For years past
the British navy has declined to follow deserters.
The motto is, that the man who “ jumps ship ” is
not worth keeping. Before that any policeman or
civilian who captured a deserter received =£l,
known as “blood money.” If Craddock’s only
offence had been desertion his name would simply
have been taken off the roll. What, then, was the
use of warrant No. 2 ?
* * * *
The policeman’s idea of the fitness of
things (Sydney daily paper paragraph last week):
Any person having lost an English lever watch with an
open face, and bearing the number 166888, is requested to
call on Constable or Constable , at No. 1 Police
Station, for the purpose of identifying the watch, for the
alleged theft of which a young man is at present in
custody.
Evidently the polioe have no evidence that the
young man in question stole the watch, and no
one informed them that such a watch was missing.
Therefore, the young man is apparently arrested
on the ground that he looks like the sort of young
man who might steal a watch, and the watch is
detained because it looks like the sort of watch
that would naturally be stolen by that sort of
man. Then the watch is offered as a reward to
the person who will assist the force in getting that
young man comfortably gaoled for the winter.

Costs of court, unless incurred in some
criminal action, should bet treated as an ordinary
debt. Justice A. H. Simpson declined last week
to interfere with an “ attached ” man detained in
Darlinghurst Gaol since January last. Parlia-
ment should leave as little as possible to judicial
discretion.

A Melb. Argus report of an attempted
murder case suppressed the fact that the victim, on

lady-doctor. The conservative organ hates lady-
doctors. They are intolerable to the sentiment of
50 years ago.

The part of Sydney metropolitan news-
paper rivalry which cuts the old-established
Herald most keenly is that people will persist in
sending their letters to both morning papers;
and sometimes—O sacrilege !—the Telegraph
gets them first. Then Editor Curnow grinds his
teeth. The Herald notifies that it will not
guarantee insertion of letters sent to another
paper ; but it has to insert them—or go without.

In the Isis Scrub (Q.) there appeared the
other day an old man propelling a heavily-laden
barrow containing picks, shovels, dish, tent—in
fact, a whole miners’ kit. The hardy veteran had
wheeled the whole from Clermont goldfield—47o
miles—and had been three months on the journey.
There’s grit in this land yet—among the old men,
anyway.
Item from the land of “ Jolly and Fat ” :
WILCANNIA, Tuesday.—A wotran who was lost on the
Seventy-mile Track turned up at the Twenty-six-mile
Tank on Saturday in a very exhausted condition, having'
been three days without food or water. The woman
whose name is Anderson, is 70 years of age, and was
travelling with a swag in search of work. She started to
walk from Hay to Wilcannia, and the endurance and
pluck shown by her are remarkable considering her age.
Poor old grandmother on the wallaby-track'
What a picture !

X.z. : “The recent death of diver Mad-
sen, in Sydney Harbor, was practically suicide.
TAKING NO CHANCES.
NURSE (to moribund patient): “ Why
won’t you take the brandy the
doctor ordered? Surely you can
lay aside your teetotal scruples
at such a time as this.”
PATIENT (to parson—faintly ): “Do
you think the angels ’ll smell it in
my breath?”
In the new-invention dress he wore there were
iron rings to resist the pressure of the water,
which, at the depth he went to, would be about
five atmospheres. In previous trials he had found
that the rings in the sleeves greatly hampered his
arm-action, and ere going down on his final trip
he removed the arm rings, despite the warnings of
a well-known Sydney doctor of science (who hiin-
se“ has tramped the harbor bottom often), who
carefully explained that to descend in the altered
dress would mean asphyxia by drowning. Madsen
went down; the immense pressure stopped circu-
lation m the arms the upper-parts of the sleeves
were driven in through the shoulder-rings, thus
producing a five-atmosphere compression of the
« hes b ar ‘ d respiration and circulation were vio-
lently stopped. The man was drowned. The
doctor was offered a big fee to attend the expen-
ment, but declined. Ihe Bulletin prints this
[ or what it is worth. Medical evidence at the
inquest alleged that Madsen was not drowned,
bnt di . ed of s P inal injury, and the jury verdicted
accordingly.

A clergyman writes to a Melb. paper that
200 self-denying persons are engaged in teaching
Chinese at the local mission churches. In round
numbers 150 will be ladies—loo of them young
and ready to fall in love, even with a Chinaman ;
for, in the matter of love, Propinquity is the one
essential. The rev. gentleman “anticipates re-
suits.” The results will come—just as they do
after church-picnics and choir-practice.

In Albury (N.S.W.) district, a tramp has
been travelling under the burden of two swags
weighing unitedly somewhere about 1801 b. The
weight was a bit too much for him, but he got
over the difficulty by humping one swag along for
about 100yds., planking it down, and returning
for the other. In this way he progressed at the
rate of nine miles or so a day. That man was
scarcely “looking for work.”

* * * *
Editor Bulletin, —When a poor woman,
deserted and otherwise maltreated by her husband,
has to institute divorce proceedings “ on the nod,”
is it necessary and humane to publish the fact (as
is done by Sydney dailies), that she is suing in
formd pauperis ? Truly, poverty is the greatest
crime ! Instead of the farce of assigning counsel
and solicitor (presumably paid by the cheap advt.)
to the poor petitioner, the Judge could easily him-
self bring out the simple facts—marriage and mis-
conduct-provable in ordinary undefended cases by
the marriage certificate and the woman herself.—
Lex.
* » • *
As the dear girls who have taken to the
bike find it necessary to expose their tempting
ankles, they naturally want to hang something
about them that will catch the manly eye, and
already the gold and jewelled anklet has made its
appearance on cycladies in London. Its arri al
in Australia is hourly expected, and presently it
will be the delirious privilege of Reginald
Augustus to measure his best girl for a sort of
lower-down subsidiary garter worth a month’s
wages.
* * * *
One Arrowsmith writes to Melbourne Age
advising its readers to succour poverty in their
midst, “ instead of sending their money to help a
lot of Kaffirs, who would think nothing of scalping
you if you came in their way.” Mr. Arrowsmith
evidently imagines that the Indian Famine Relief
Fund is being got up to assist distressed Apaches
or Sioux, and that Apaches and Kaffirs are the
same thing. His ideas are mixed, but his senti-
ments are not altogether unsatisfactory.
* * * *
A W.A. policeman (in plain clothes) was
taking a lunatic by rail to the Perth Asylum, and
had absent-mindedly placed the “ admittance
papers ” on the seat of the carriage beside him.
While the bobby was looking out of the window
the lunatic quickly picked up and pocketed the
documents. At the asylum door the officer felt
for the papers—they were gone. The lunatic im-
mediately produced them, and, handing them to
the warder, said: “His chief trouble is that he
thinks he is a policeman, otherwise he is very
quiet.” The bobby was forced inside, protesting
all the time that he was the policeman. Ulti-
mately the pair changed places. But that Robert
will travel in uniform henceforth.

carriage-way, or foreclosing on a time-payment

don’t deserve to flourish. An institution which
BUTLER-HARWOOD-ASHE (from San Francisco prison portraits).
can’t be democratic without fooling with tin
swords, infantile passwords and billy-goats, de-
serves to die young.
* * * *
The other day a swagman on the Percy, a
drought-stricken country, was forced to climb a
tree in order to get a few green branches to strew
on the ground as a couch. (The cattle had cropped
off all the lower twigs.) Immediately a branch
fell the cattle rushed from all directions and tried
to seize it, and the swaggie would have to drop
down and beat them off with fire-sticks. So the
duel kept up for some time—victory ultimately
lying with the man. Just think of the condition
of those cattle !

Choice evidence from a recent Melb. bad-
meat case —good reading for Lent: —
When the hide was taken off the disease was on the
other side also; it had, in fact, gone through the ribs.
Part of the pleura, which was diseased, had been stripped
away. The lymphatic glands were a mass of tuberculosis,
which was also very bad about the kidneys, and when an
incision was made the pus ran out.
People turn impulsively to vegetarianism, and
read nexUday of a Chinaman slopping unspeak
able abomination about his “ cabbagee,” and then
they all want to join a museum and fast for a
living.

In the early summer mornings
When we’re sound asleep in bed,
Oh, we hear the old man tramping
Loud enough to wake the dead,
And we search for skirts and jackets
By a fat-lamp’s flickering glow,
Drink a cup of tea, and hasten
To the milking-yard below.
And the heated, dusty stock-yard
Its load of labor brings—
When a hundred cows are finished
Then the separator sings.
When the separator sings,
With its burr-ur-ur;
When the separator sings,
With its whirr-ir-ir,
If life’s slavery you’d be learning,
And the lowest wages earning,
Just try the handle turning
When the separator sings.
... R. : In English law (writes an authority) no action
will lie for a libel on a corpse, the main reason being that
there can be no proper plaintiff. At Common Law, how-
ever, it is a misdemeanor to write and publish libellous
words concerning a dead man, provided that it be alleged
and proved that the words were published with the intent
of bringing the family and relations of the deceased into
hatred and contempt. As early as 1734 a criminal infor-
mation was granted against a person for libelling one Sir
C. Nicoll, then dead ; there it was alleged that the
libel was published with intent to vilify the memory of
Sir C., and to stir up hatred against his family and pos-
terity. Later, an indictment alleging a libel on Earl
Cowper with intent to disgrace his family and character
was held bad after a verdict of guilty, and judgment was
arrested because no intent to create ill-feeling or to
throw scandal on his faf .ily was alleged. It will thus be
seen that the criminal remedy, which is the earlier,
is also the more extensive one, and that a libel
may be indictable though not actionable D.8.C.:

BRISBANE.
The Federation Convention delegates are
provided with free meals at Adelaide Parliament
House during the days of sitting, but few avail
themselves of the privilege, except the local men.
Mr. Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., desires to
inform intending patentees that on account of his
satisfactory arrangements with leading patent
agents in all the Australasian provinces, the prin-
cipal countries of Europe, America, and South
Africa, he is in a position to take out patents,
register trade-marks, companies and copyrights,
at rates very much below those hitherto charged by
expert patent agents, and which will be within the
reach of everyone. For the sum of =£2o, exclusive
of cost of drawings, he will obtain a Certificate of
Provisional Protection (in the first instance) for
an .V invention in the seven Australian provinces
and Great Britain and full patents in any other
countries in the world at proportionately reason-
able rates. Mr. Griffith’s office is 41 Castlereagh-
street, City, next Hotel Australia.*
