A WOMAN'S LETTER.
A/r r , Sydney, April 6,1897.
My Dear Myee—
Eight hundred blue tickets are now scattered
around the Nicest of the city, admitting them to
the Cathedral for the wedding of Hon. Margaret
Brand and the opulent Capt. Ferguson, on 21st
April. Much fluttering anxiety was caused
among the invited by the words “full dress ” on
u 6 °P ri ) er the white end. Some women
thought it referred to ladies’ costumes and decreed
a display of floured collar-bones and arms to the
searching sunlight; so, to settle the point, a tall
politician was formed by his female friends into a
deputation to an over-worked aide among whose
duties it is to settle little matters of that sort,
and brought back official information that “ full
dress ” meant that any guest having any kind of
uniform must furbish up same for the ceremony.
Whereat the long legislator was much pleased, as
now he can arrange to beautify the show by wear-
ing his National Guard duds inside the sacred
edifice.
The circulars issued some time ago to 300 likely
persons among the toney, inviting subscriptions
for a present for the forthcoming Govt. House
wedding, were responded to by a gathering last
week. Subscribers appeared, but “ not—not the
300.” It was decided to give the bride a silver
toilet ornament. The girls’ gift was undertaken
byasassiety damsel at the head of a clique at
variance with other cliques, and so coin has not
been flowing freely in for the object. The
numerous dearest friends and bosom enemies of
the promoters of joint-ptock wedding-gifts would
rather each hand in her own modest little butter-
dish or photo.-frame with donor’s card attached
than help unadvertised to buy a gigantic glittering
tea-and-coffee service with other unnamed “ ladies
of Sydney.”
An above-the-average attendance graced Rose-
hill racecourse on Saturday afternoon. The trains
ran to time, and the sec. ’s gallant endeavor to
popularise his meetings with lady race-goers was
rewarded by the presence of some smart sporting
girls who generally turn every gee-gee con-
test around Sydney. As for dressing, it is a lost
art just now, with all one’s summer things worn
out and shop windows untemptingly displaying
furs and velvets in this steaming weather. At
last week’s outdoor gatherings the woman with
the longest-lived muslin was the queen of the
situation. Two or three cloth “tailor-mades”
and tweed gowns, ante-dated winter, seemed to
smother their wearers.
As the record April fool—ror his folly was made
known to the world on April Ist—the arrested
naval assistant-paymaster really must take first
prize. To endeavour to pull off such a double
event as he did was wondrous. Marriage under a
false name and getting away safely, and for
good, to Melbourne with his bride from a place
where every fifth or sixth person in the street
must have known him by sight, was more than
enough for one man to undertake. But he is
alleged to have got away with ,£2400 of ship’s
gold, and under such circumstances to take up
quarters at a leading city hotel argues that this
enterprising young man of 24 has more valor than
discretion. It is said that he had taken passage
for himself and bride in six different steamers, in
none of which has the lady now any chance of
wearing her ,£2OO worth of new frocks packed in
eight trunks.
The lass that loved sailor in this astounding
case loved him unbeknownst. It wasn’t the sup-
posedly irresistible blue-and-brass that did the
business. The fascinations of the sea-going per-
son must be mope than uniform-deep. Another
alleged naval love-affair has been lately interesting
Melb. sassiety-par. writers, who affiance a Sydney
“ naval officer ” to a young woman journalist and
solemnly lecture her in social columns on the mis-
take of plunging into matrimony, on the strength
of a sky-rocketty announcement which surprised
nobody more than the alleged fiancee herself.
A deadlock occurred recently between New-
castle Municipal Council and Govt. House in con-
sequence of the aldermen’s refusal to give His Ex.
an official welcome to their town after it had been
arranged that he was to open a local Benevolent
Asylum, not situated in the city itself but in an
exclusive suburb. The trouble has, I hear, a
woman at the bottom of it. She is a metropolitan
philanthropist who engineers the charity. Hav-
ing obtained Gov. Hampden’s promise" through
the Chief Sec. that he would launch it on its new
career, she moved the asylum authorities to point
out to the aldermen,that it was their duty to give
the vice-regal procession a public welcome, but the
aldermen intimated that as neither the Chief Sec.
nor the lady philanthropist were citizens it was
beyond their jurisdiction to invite the Governor
to the town at all. Then His Ex. heard of the
fuss and promptly cancelled the engagement. A
meeting of citizens was held afterwards and a cor-
dial invitation given to the Governor to come
along casually at any time and bring the “ Gover-
ness ” with him.
An orange-blossom scented atmosphere is ex-
pected to be wafted about between Easter and the
beginning of May, so there will be a week or two
after Lent wherein loving couples may be united
before the unlucky month throws its blight on
superstitious wooers. So enterprising drapers are
making ready for the boom in weddings by adorn-
ing their windows with filmy veils—chiffon, not
net, is the latest material for the bride to blush
under and other vanities. In one of the windows,
by the way, is shown a pair of plush curtains, so
brilliant that they made me wink, worked by the
nuns in one of the convents with flannel-flowers,
waratahs, and other indigenous vegetables, also
emus, parrots, and assorted natives, striped with
polychrome lace, all most exquisitely done, and to
be raffled A. M.D.G. and the good of the Church
at the big fair beyant St. Mary’s Cathedral.
There has been a dearth of dressy drama lately
until the adyent of “ Sweet Lavender,” which
gives sartorial scope. Terry, of the tiny mouth,
takes full advantage of it; his frock-coat is a
poem, and ho w (space is the latest thing for em-
phasis, according to the Red Page) does he get
his hat so glossy ? The Gilfillians, mother and
daughter, should give a tone to their scenes.
Minnie (Miss Seabrooke) does so in three pretty
frocks, all of local build ; No. 1 is yellow silk with
cloudy blue chiffon on the bodice and big black
hat of the “ Hamlet’s Aunt ” style ; No. 2 is a
blue brocade of girlish evening cut; No. 3 a pink,
daisy-patterned silk with green elaborations.
Bessie Rignold makes Lavvy’s red hair match the
crimson window-curtains, and gets a Jan Van
Beers effect by wearing a pink dress against her
glaring wig. On the second night the intervals
were filled in by the dress-circle, principally in
trying to establish the identity of a pretty stranger
in a stage-box. It was an elderly men’s evening
out, the d.c. positively glistened with bald-heads.
The late German consul Sahl, was at one time
very wealthy and connected with the important
German S. tea-trading firm which is a political
power in Samoa. He did not come forward in
any social way'until after his marriage, and, until
he joined the German Club, he was not well-
known personally even by his fellow-countrymen
in Sydney. He travelled much in Australia and
the South Seas before settling here, where he
lived for the last 27 years. For consular services
Mr. Sahl was given two German decorations ; at
his death he was president of the German Club.
Many gilded personages fancy that they are
made to pay too dearly for their whistle. One
Melb. millionairess, on that account, never buys
her gloves and other small goods herself, but ob-
tains them per her less-known sister, and so saves
a supposed sixpenny overcharge. An Australian
Governor’s wife lately ordered a smart vehicle
built to her order at rather a fancy price. A few
days after it had been sent home a plainly-dressed
young woman called at the builder’s and asked
what he would charge to build her a cart exactly
like the new vice-regal one. He had but one
price and named it. The girl then rejoined her
mistress, who was waiting around the corner, and
proved satisfactorily that the latter hadn’t been
“ got at.”
“In Sydney, 5000 people assemble to listen to a
pianist like Miss Leverrier and strew roses in her
path, while in this wretchedly Puritan-ridden
place (Melb.) the audience generally yawns while
the critics adjourn for a drink, and only return for
a little more sleep preparatory to writing some
commonplace stuff for the next day about a thing
they know nothing of.” Thus H. J. King
harangued the Melb. Liedertafel, of which he- is
conductor. He omitted to state that the 5000
assembled by invitation, and roses are cheap.
Gratuitous indiscriminate applause is the best pos-
sible thing for spoiling a young performer’s future
career. Unless Yvonne has a phenomenally old
head on her young shoulders, her ordinary hats
will become too small for her, and she will have to
wear “coon” headgear. On her first appearance
in Melb., at a Marshall Hall concert, there was an
outburst of disapproval in next day’s criticisms.
Most impartial musicians thought that there must
be something in a girl who could win abuse on such
a lavish scale. And there is much ; Yvonne could
never be insignificant, but her faults are on the
same large scale as her powers.
A Mildura concert was to be enlivened by a
male quartette, but the tenor at the last moment
developed a bad cold and a substitute was hunted
up—-a fat, little pomposity who had only hazy
notions of what he had to do. On the platform he
suddenly developed stage fright for which he had
prepared himself beforehand in vain, and he
quietly and abruptly walked off. The other three
were absorbed in the music, until the second tenor
glanced around with a horrified stare at the blank
space, and he. also went, leaving the two basses
roaring away unconscious of their loss. One of
the basses—a shy, bashful youth—suddenly dis-
covered that the audience was shrieking with
laughter, and then he departed swiftly. The last
man, with a defiant glance, nobly stuck to his post
in a solo to immense applause.
The dressiest woman amongst the delegates’
wives in Adelaide is Mrs. Neil Lewis, the Tas-
manian Opposition leader’s wife. She is a viva-
cious, thin little lady who seems to know every-
body and to wear in turns samples of all sorts and
styles of dress. Many delegates who did not take
their wives with them telegraphed for the ladies
subsequently—probably to keep themselves (or the
wives) out of other temptation.
A country parson has been trying to get his
congregation to mortify the flesh during pre-
Easter days. But they are rebellious, and don’t
mortify anything except the poor unheeded shep-
herd. The godless youths and maidens of his
flock have formed a tennis-club, and have tea-
parties there when games are going on. During
Lent the clerical veto has gone forth against
these guileless teetotal sprees, and players must
swing their rackets with thirst unslaked by the
washy beverage usually spilled about so freely at
a tennis-club meeting.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ The matrimonial affairs of a well-known pair,
who had their miseries crowned (in dental par-
lance) in the divorce-court some time ago, have
taken a most uncommon turn. The divorced wife
duly put her neck in the nuptial noose again; her
first partner had been a comfortable country profes-
sional man ; her present one is a respected man of
letters. Since the lady’s second marriage the
divorc4 has developed a strong platonic affection
for his lost property. After making several ad-
vances of a reconciliatory nature, he forwarded a
substantial gift to the newly-married pair at
Christmas—with his blessing. Amicable relations
exist between husband Past and Present, and the
lonely divorce has asked the happy pair up to his
country mansion to eat the Easter goose with him.
“The announcement, ‘Ladies only, admission
free ,’ drew a big audience of the fair sex to Melb.
Athenaeum, where Dr. Emily Ryder lectured on
‘How not to be ill.’ If any bold, bad man had
succeeded in eluding the girl door-keepers, he
would have found the proceedings disappointingly
mild. The doctor is a brainy-looking, white-
haired woman who lectures in her University cap
and gown, and speaks well, despite her national
twang. She made some trenchant remarks re the
enormous fatality among Australian infants, and
wished there was as much study given to child-
raising as to sheep-ditto. She maintained that
‘ the present custom of marrying a boy and girl,
and shutting them up in a house to breed human
beings haphazard, is simply iniquitous ’! At
which plain statement the young women applauded
vigorously, and the old ones gasped in horror.
According to Ryder, girls now-a-days demand
something more than r mere masculinity ’—anv
girl can get married in a week, if she will take
the specimens that offer themselves. ‘The lack
of marriages is owing to the feminine standard
being too high. ’ In the next breath, she gave men
unstinted praise for their all-round common sense,
and declared that no man ever wore his belt so
tight that he couldn’t eat his meals in comfort.’
Also, ‘bad home-cooking drives men to drink.’
“ The Princess matinee was an entertainment of
the usual scrappy order ; the audience was chiefly
a horde of vast-hatted women. The ladies of the
profession mingled with the newly-joined members
of their ranks and sold programmes, tea, and
sweets, with smiles of inscrutable sweetness, to
d.c. and box patrons. Mrs. Morris, who looked
very charming in black silk with a Paisley silk
blouse and picturesque hat with roses, took her
place in the Pickwick trial scene and chatted with
the expansive and still - spreading Cosgrove.
Miss Sutton, in blue muslin and comic-opera
hat, sold programmes in the front of the house.
By ‘A White Stocking’ the fascinating Ferris
shows that he figures more successfully as a
playwright than as an actor. The scene in the
library of Laughton Rectory is very fetching, with
the firelight shining on the snow-covered boots of

brown curls of Miss Page; and Mr. Knight’s
military dress of scarlet and gold makes a fine
touch of color against a deep brown wainscot.
The little play is merely a set of Randolph Calde-
cott’s pictures dramatised and picturesquely
staged. In the ‘ Trial ’ scene, Charlie Brown, as
crier, spoke with much irreverence to some of the
witnesses. He alluded to Norman Menzies —
cleverly made-up as a red Elphberg—as ‘ a pur-
veyor of intoxicants,’ and called Kyrle Bellew
‘ the Apollo Belvedere ’ with such flippancy that
the admired one refused to appear.
“At the Broughs’ ‘at home’ at the Bijou on
Thursday afternoon, a representative assortment
of people was brought together. The smart set,
with whom the co. has always been immensely
popular, turned up to a woman, and ‘ the pro-
fession ’ trod the boards on which the reception
took place, and shook hands with host and hostess
with a lofty, upward shake, as self-consciously as
though ‘ the front ’ was full of onlookers. The
singing of Katherine Hardy was one of the most
attractive turns during the afternoon. Never has
the silvery-throated mezzo sung so well, and all
present regretted that this uncommonly fine song-
stress does not use her vocal talent more in her pro-
fession. Regarding her acting there has always
been a, division of opinion, but as to her singing
there is absolutely none.
“ The set of choice spirits which rules the world
of ‘ sassiety ’ in this city is gradually becom-
ing so limited that it can hardly people two
smart entertainments at one time. A fashion-
able garden - party suffered much in point of
numbers on Friday last by reason of the Princess
matinee, and, although the house was thronged,
the garden, wherein a capital band discoursed
festive strains all the afternoon, was entirely
deserted. Nearly everyone present was talking
of spending this season in London and making
arrangements to meet each other ‘ln Town.’
The poor residue of the smart set which will be
left behind looks forward to a very dismal and
serious-minded winter. Last winter was flat
enough owing to the scarcity of young and eligible
men, but this year the girls have taken wing, too,
so that the halls of fashion will be left desolate
indeed.
“Mrs. Potter has been much in evidence in
very public places during the past week, and with
her elf-like locks—a tint or so more emphatically
gamboge—weird trailing clothes and marvellous
hats, she has served as a highly-colored advt. for
her forthcoming season at the Princess’. In the
face of columns of wailing against the eternal
theatre hat in the evening papers, Cora sported a
vast black tea-tray, with a hecatomb of wildly-
nodding plumes, at the Royal the other evening.
The patient-looking Kyrle, who sat with her,
was quite obscured by the structure from any
point of view, and looked like a headless body
in too-closely-fitting striped grey pants. .Just here
one might enquire if Kyrle was ever seen in a
well-shaped pair of continuations of fashionable
loose cut ; his nether garments are usually of the
kind which go with high heels and an abbreviated
coat. The attenuated Cora is wearing her hair a-
hanging down her back, gathered in with a black
ribbon at the nape of the neck, a style which
makes her look more than ever like an un-recent
photograph of Mrs. Scot Inglis.
“ A recent Bulletin paragraph, reminding Gov.
Brassey of a certain cheque for ten guineas long
promised to a new Presbyterian kirk in Ascot
Yale (Melb.), evidently caught the Brass-eye.
The cheque was forthcoming last week, and has
been thankfully acknowledged. One or two other
charitable objects are now looking for a decent
opportunity to jog his Excellency’s memory.”
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Despite the cry of “hard times,” the approach-
ing' meeting of the A. J.C. promises to be one of the most
successful ever held at Randwick. With True Blue
Delaware, Newhaven, Coil, The Officer, Cremorne, Charge’
Aurum, The Hypnotist, Hopscotch, Vivian, and Battalion
all in more or less good trim there should be some high-
class racing.
* * * *
Newhaven has arrived at Randwick, and at
exercise moves in his best form. Most of the w.f.a. events
should be easily secured by him, though if he meets
Aurum in the All-Aged Stakes the flying Trenton colt
may stop his winning career. The punting public won't
be sorry to see his downfall, as the Sydney Cup scratching
was a miserable fiasco.
» * * •
The time-honored Hawkesbury Racing Club
holds its annual autumn meeting to-day (Saturday), and
as there is a “sweep” on the big handicap, some good
racing.may be expected. In the Clarendon Stakes, Mun-
diva, Jack Horner, and First Mate seem to have good
winning chances, whilst for the Autumn Handicap, Kal-
latina, Helmsman, Pense h Moi, and Miss Nora look like
supplying the winner. . .
V * * *
Mr. W. Kelso with his lengthy string is pro-
viding much speculation as to his ultimate representa-
tives in Doncaster and Sydney Cup. At present Reka for
the former and Tom for the latter race are both great
public fancies, but when the barrier goes up it will likely
be found that Crfinie d Or will be the elect for Doncaster
and Reka for Cup. The latter, running Resolute at 21b.,
was only three-quarters of a length behind Mr. Wilson’s
colt in Melb. Cup, and now they meet at 91b., which, con-
sidering Reka is very well, gives him, on paper, an un-
doubtedly good show.
* * * •
At Rosehill, N.S.W., last Saturday, the Q.
horse Battalion put up a good performance by carrying
9st. 121 b. into second place in the. Autumn Handicap. As
this wonderfully-consistent performer is very well at pre-
' sent he may be expected to carry Bst. P2lb. into a pro-
minent position in Sydney Cup.
* * * *
The Fleet Admiral division evidently doesn’t
fear the all-victorious Aurum, as it last week booked the
big bet of 800 to 100 about its pet. Should both these
cracks survive the difficulties of training their meeting as
three-years-old will be something well worth seeing.
♦ * * *
The sensational gelding Hopscotch was given a
gallop in Rosehill Handicap, and for over a mile he led
the field a merry dance with 9st. 121 b. up. He now reigns
as second favorite for Doncaster Handicap—but he is well-
in for the more valuable Sydney Cup.
• « * •
The owner of Daimio writes out that jumpers
like his horse lose too much at the English jumps, clear-
ing sft., whilst the Britishers are skimming along a foot
lower. He thinks a really good bold hurdler would win
many English steeplechases.
* » » *
St. Lawrence, late Miss Curtis, won the trot
last Friday at Brighton, Sydney, hut much latitude was
allowed by the judges over her bad breaks. A break is
allowable so long as the horse does not make ground over
it, but when a horse makes several bad breaks galloping
fifty, perhaps a hundred, yards each time, as St. Lawrence
did on Friday, disqualification should ensue. In 1892
Miss Curtis’ record stood at 5.34, which she cut down on
Friday to 5.26.
* * * *
At the Feb. meet of Tattersall’s race-club,
Elphin, Tas., Mr. Quong Lang’s brown mare, K.L., won
the open trot, trained and ridden by the Chow himself.
About half-a-dozen of his countrymen follow the races
regularly, and one or the other is almost invariably seen
at the tote window for div.
* * * •
A Maoriland St. Leger Stakes, 500 so vs., is
billed for 1899, besides three other classic races by the
Wellington R.C. The tote !
* * * *
The Ballarat gelding, Blackwood, who V"!
whispered to have a chance for the Australian Cup (until
they scratched him), won a race at Flemington on Satur-
day in rather handsome style. The son of Vengeance-
named after the owner of that sturdy battler—was seem-
ingly underrated by old Wilson, for he went out at 12 to 1.
* ♦ ♦ *
There can be no question as to the advantage
which a two-year-old enjoys in w.f.a. races towards the
end of the season. Aurum’s recent smothering of Coil
and The Officer doesn’t look at all remarkable in the light
of Key’s performance at Flemington last Saturday. The
unfashionably-bred youngster gave 71b. to Pilatus, 241 b.
to the three-year-old Campaign, and 31b. to sprinter
Bacchus, and beat them from end to end of a six-furlong
go.
* * * *
Of 15 starters in the Hill Handicap, at Y.R.C.
April meeting, five were shut off from all chance of
winning by the bad management of the starting-machine,
for which Godfrey took the blame. Another instance of
the necessity for giving the starter power to call back the
horses that get away in cases where the machine is re-
sponsible for leaving some of them at the post. Or has
the starter a discretionary power, and, if so, why doesn’t
he exercise it ?
* » * *
Turner, the bowler, leaves Sydney in a few days
to make his permanent home in Brisbane.
* * * *
East Sydney paid Paddington back in its own
coin on Saturday. Paddington closed its innings at
two wickets for 269 runs—Alec. Bannerman, not out, 133
evidently thinking it had a good thing on ; but East
Sydney finished out the day with 204 for the loss of one
wicket—C. A. Richardson, not out, 40, W. J. Camphin 95,
and J. Gould, not out, 65.
* * * *
The Forget-me-nots, lady-cricketers, of Warr-
naiubool, played a local team of fat old men the other
day. The male eleven did not contain a man under 50 or
less than 15st. The girls won. Grace before meat.
* * » *
“ Musgrove advises that the prospects of the
Aus. baseball-tour in America are bad.” Yes —not one of
the Australians eats cigars or hits the ball into the next
county or says “Great Snakes!” Australians probably
won’t do as baseballers in Yankeeland.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The conditions of the races
were so dissimilar that Percy Cavill’s beating of English
champion Tyers’ half-mile amateur swimming time goes
for nought. The men swam in salt and fresh water re-
spectively, and no man can say what difference that, and
the kick-off at the end of each lap in Fanner's Baths,
would make. That Cavill should have beaten Tvers’ time
by 34 2-ssee. certainly looks like too much of a good
thing, under equal conditions ; but perhaps the perform-
ance is discounted most by the fact that Repan finished,
within ten yards of Cavill. Had Cavill distanced his
field the performance would have looked better on paper,
for it is scarcely likely we have suddenly dropped across a
whole batch of swimmers able to beat the Britisher by
upwards of half a minute.

a station.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast-Table
David Syme.

frailest man of the Federal Convention. John

BURNS-WALKER, the soulful baritone wnose
singing makes the Sydney girls cry.
Photo., Falk.
Apropos the Sargood portrait affair, it
would appear that any shoddy person can have
his portrait hung in the Vic. National Gallery,
which contains no pictures of the really notable
men who have had a place in Victorian affairs.
Higinbotham is not there, for instance. The idea
of carving Sargood’s effigy in marble was aban-
doned, because the Age intimated that David
Syme would not allow it to be put up in his
Melbourne.
Ricciotti Garibaldi, son of the Liberator,
who offers to go to the assistance of Greece with
100,000 Italians, was formerly a truant-inspector
in Vic. Education Department—which was the
best the authorities of the day were able to do for
him. As private secretary to James Service he
proved a round peg in a square hole. Ricciotti’s
present offer is probably mostly talk. His brother,
Menotti, was the fighting man of the family.
Edmund Barton—M.A. at 21, barrister at
22, Speaker of N.S.W. Assembly at 33. He is
now a trifle over 48.
The British statesman who predicted that
Gladstone would die insane was a seer. At pre-
sent the G.O.M. is riding a bike.
. Canon Kemmis, lately deceased, frequently
had his morning-paper ironed (to take the damp
out of it) before he would read it.
The Adelaide papers are strong in the
history- of interprovincial politics. The Freetrade
journal introduced Barton as Parkes’s Attorney-
General in ’9l.
As the result of an accident, Robert Mac-
quarie, aged 87 years, a nephew of the old-time
N.S.W. Governor of that name, died at Gawler,
S.A., last week.
Melb. city police force contains constables
Felsenthal, Mafferzoli and Mercovitch. And yet
“ St. Patrick’s Day passed off with a marked
absence of disorder.”
Softgoods Sargood : “I have no time to
cultivate those arts commonly called popularity.”
This from a shopman ! Presumably the cultiva-
tion of such a cheek employs the whole of a man’s
time.
Turner has not yet had courage to scrape
the plain “George Turner’’ off the gate of his
St. Kilda home. Displayed humility in a man
who takes a title is ridiculous excess. He is suffi-
ciently humiliated.
Butler’s latest cabled exploits —-attempted
suicide, by trying to open his temporal artery with
his thumb-nail, and an admission that he did kill
Preston, but in self-defence—by shooting him in
the back of the head.
George Dean, of North Sydney poisoning
fame, is taking his incarceration very badly. He
is said to be bordering on lunacy, and reports con-
cerning his condition are now before the officers of
the Prisons Department.
Speaker Coles and Siredwin Smith, of
S.A., both cleared for Tasmania just before
the Federal delegates arrived in Adelaide, the
popular idea being that they desired to escape the
expense of entertaining them.
Westralia was the ninth British dependency
presided over by the musical William Robinson.
This is the record in governing. They quote :
“ The greatest Governor who was ever a musician,
and the greatest musician who was ever a
Governor.”

Of course, the Phillips’ petitions are accompanied

Just think of the parents, whose nerves are unstrung,

The direct cause of Yic. Minister Peacock’s
most violent outburst of demoniac laughter at the
Convention was a hen-story narrated by a Tas.
delegate : This individual’s hens were always root-
ing up the garden, and to stop this he procured
some bantams and brahmas, with the result that a
race of hybrids was produced. If one of those
hybrids stood on the brahma leg, it simply
scratched the air with the other, and if it stood on
the bantam leg and tried to lift up the earth ; at
the first stroke it would be shot over the fence into
the next-door neighbor’s garden. Hence Peacock’s
yells.
James Andrew Massingliam, of Mittagong
(N.S.W.), invented a new wpe d-gear for bicycles.
He was for a while elated with his success, but,
on a friend explaining to him a radical defect in
the gear, the elation gave way to despondency, and
last week he blew his brains out.
N’.S.W. Judge Gibson, who gave a man
three years (which carries nine months' solitary
confinement!) for stealing a ninepenny tin of
baking-powder, was formerly associate to Mount
Rennie Windeyer. Three months’ hard-labor and
one month’s solitary for each penny !
No more rumors of dailv and weekly news-
papers to be started in Melbourne by “ Mr.
Bailey, from India.”, The other day poor Bailey,
from India (he was editor of Calcutta States-
man), died in Melbourne Hospital. He had fallen
from his horse and fractured his skull.
Hori Grey will be- 85 on April 14, and
Way, P.C., will complete his 61st year during the
same week. Grey ceased to be Governor of S.A.
in 1845, and Way did not make his first appearance
there until ’53, the year in which Grey left Maori-
land to assume the governorship of the Cape.
The ornamental Lyttleton H. Bayley, who
was N.S.W. Attorney-General in ’59, is now a
Judge of the High Court, Bombay. Meeting a
Sydney acquaintance not long ago, he attributed
his hale and hearty existence to having quitted
Australian politics before he began to like them.
Vic. Chief Justice Midden, to Barrister
Duffy (who wanted a half-hour adjournment for
the purpose of stowing away food and liquor) : “ I
breakfasted at 7, had no lunch, and don’t expect
to be able to have dinner before 8 o’clock. ” The
fat man again. Surplus is strength. Fat is force.
Mr. “ Hee Hem” Smith, ousted from
M.L. Parliament last election, has applied to New
Plymouth borough council to increase his screw as
town lamplighter. There is no false pride about
Smith.- His son carried on the Ciz. while he was
in the ’Ouse, and now he has resumed his sunset
walks again.
Convention pressmen award the palm for
“wind” to Holder and Glynn, both S.A. dele-
gates. The former, the long, gaunt S. A. Treasurer,
goes along at a terrific pace, though with a clear
voice ; but lawyer and literary man Glynn has a
two-foot brogue which makes the smartest scribe’s
hair stand on end.
The “father” of the Australian Bar is
still alive in England—Alexander Gordon, Q.C.,
equity barrister, who practised for many years in
Sydney, and who was “called” as far back as
1841. The next on the list is retired N.S. Wales
D.C. Judge Dowling, who dates from 1843 ; then
another retired N.S. Wales D.C. Judge, M‘Far-
land, whose date is 1848 ; and then Justice M. H.
Stephen, still in harness, who was “ called ” in
1850.
W. V. Ralston, who succeeds Drury as
general-manager of Q.N. Bank, was the Western
pioneer of that institution from the day it started.
He vegetated for years in a little wooden caravan-
serai in the pastoral village of Tambo, where all
things were as sluggish as the sleepy Barcoo
River. He afterwards passed to the coast, where
he added soldiering to finance. When Ralston
was appointed acting-manager he signified in his
first report, about a year ago, that no dividends
should be paid to shareholders. Now that he is
manager in full he boldly denounces dividend-
paying in the circumstances as robbery.
Major W. T. Reay, editor of Melbourne
Herald, member of the Council of the Anti-
Sweating League, Grand Something or other in
the Oddfellows, candidate for Parliament, A.N. A.
worker, &c., &c., goes ’Ome as one of the officers
in command of the Vic. Mounted Rifle detach-
ment. His special missions are to show Wolselcy
how to command the army, Chamberlain how to
conduct ltis colonial policy, and Andrew Lang
how to write English - together with any other
work that comes handy.
J. W. SWEENEY, well-known Australian Irish
comedian.
Photo., Barroni.
The Mulhalls, father and son, who found
the body of Burgess and helped to carry it 60 miles
into Parkes, have not succeeded in obtaining any
pecuniary reward from N.S.W. Govt. The Govt,
offered £5O for the discovery of the body of Lesah,
believed to have been murdered, and it was while
in search of Lesah that the Mulhalls found the
clay of the assassinated Burgess. But the genius
of red-tape remarks “ Government had offered no
reward for the recovery of Burgess.”
It might be as well to point out to the
great jubilating British public that the 60th year
of Her Majesty’s reign is also the 350th anniversary
of the demise of Henry the Eighth—and, after all,
there is probably more sense in rejoicing over the
death of the latter monarch than over the prolong-
ing of a weariful senile decay in the former poten-
tate.
R. H. Horne (writes “Tom Collins ” to
The Bulletin) was never warden of the Blue
Mountain (Trentham, Vic.) goldfields; he was
mining registrar there, not only for two years,
but during nearly the whole of the sixties. The
contemporary warden’s name was Heron. Horne
was a genial, courteous, forgetful, comprehen-
sively incapable official; a man radically unfit for
any office demanding the smallest exercise of dis-
cretionary power. Nevertheless, he was extremely
popular with the diggers who constituted his
clientele ; and Heron stood to him like a dashing,
unscrupulous young father to a foolish old son.
By the way, do you know anyone who can lay
his hand on his heart and say he has read “Orion”
or “ Prometheus ” ?

The wonder is that a political career was
possible to a man with a laugh like Peacock’s.
The fact that he is intellectually superior to his
laugh does not account for it. The '.writer knows
a cleverer young man who found public career

David Syme’s big donation towards send-
ing a Vic. military show to England has brought
other bloated capitalists to the front. Chirnside,
M.L.A., dumps down £2lOO to pay the expenses
of an Easter military encampment, and Staughton
offers a big cheque to send four more riflemen to
England. Apparently there is any amount of
money available for gas and guns and gaiters, but
if Turner sent round the hat for some plain, useful
purpose —say to make a practicable road through
an inaccessible portion of Gippsland—it would
probably come back not only empty but kicked
mto such a fearsome shape by somebody’s boot
that the owner would shudder when he saw it
again.
S.M. Herald on the unemployed trouble :
“ When Mr. Garrard returns from Maoriland
fresh ground will probably be broken.” Pre-
sumably, Hon. Jake means to bury his dead.
Fed. Convention delegate Dobson (Tas.)
says that if Leg. Councils were to trust the people,
the people would kick them out. Certainly. They
might always trust the people to do that much for
them—with enthusiasm.
Premier Kingston can hardly, like Turner,
stipulate that if he goes to England the Opposi-
tion will agree not to table a no-confidence motion.
John Downer, now of the Opposition, returned
from the Imperial Conference some years ago to
find that his Ministry had been kicked out in his
absence. Kingston was one of the enemy on that
occasion.
Glynn proved the fastest speaker in the
Federation debate on Barton’s resolutions. After
him Holder, Deakin, O’Connor, and Zeal. Simon
Fraser was the most halting; Isaacs’ speech
showed the best arrangement and was the most
epigrammatic; Gordon’s the most flowery and
flippant; Trenwith’s the most serious ; Forrest’s
and Lyne’s the jerkiest.

R. F. Toucher, the new president of
A.N.A. (Vic.), is a clerk in the Income Tax
office. For some years past he has been the
Federation League of the province, with an in-
veterate habit of calling himself together in numer-
ous corners of the country, and then adjourning
after moving himself a hearty vote of thanks.
Toucher’s election was the result of a re-action
against pushing M.P.’s, who seemed inclined to
run the whole show.
Hogan, M.P., announces that, it the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth gets fairly afloat and
Queensland still hangs out, he will move, when
the Constitution Bill is passing through the British
House of Commons, the addition of a clause per-
mitting Central and North Queensland to join the
Federation “on their own.” Hogan’s chances of
success amount to very little, but the attempt
would at least make Brisbane politicians sit up
and curse beautifully.
Who says that women do not take an in-
terest in politics? At Wellington (M.L.) the
other day four lady voters were fined 10s. each for
tearing out each other’s hair in the street as the
result of a debate on the present licensing law.
The two who upheld Prohibition tore out most
hair, though as one of the Prohibition ladies had
the worst-wrecked nose of the four things were
about even. The Maoriland she-voter takes an
interest in politics right enough.
Without Government or Opposition in the
Federal Convention the speeches in the general
debate simply bristled with compliments and
flattery. No speech was referred to, even to be
attacked, unless it were able, or thoughtful, or
skilful, or unless the speaker had laid the Conven-
tion under an obligation or something of that sort.
Barton in replying did venture to hit a little
harder ;so did Trenwith just before him. But
these were the bright’exceptions.
J. P. Abbott’s
nomination of
Kingston for the
Presidency of the
Convention, and
his frank assur-
ance that the post
by precedent and
right belonged to
the local Premier,
hit G. H. Reid,
who had been in-
dustriously bar-
racking for
Joseph, in a pecu-
liarly tender part.
Yet the hit was
quite in the Ab-
bott manner. The
writer recalls a
night in N.S.W.
Parliament, be-
fore the closure
days, when the
Opposition, of
whom Abbott was
one of the stirring
leaders, deter-
mined to obstruct
the Govt, by all
means in its
power. Walker,
O’Sullivan, and
other long-winded
bores were put up
to talk through
the night. Ab-
bott had gone
home at 11 p.m.,
but promised to
be back early in
the morning, and
the effort of the
talkers was to keep a House for Joseph. At
8 a.m. Abbott arrived. Melville was on the floor,
a ghastly picture of an idealess man from whom
words dropped. All the bores crowded in to re-
ceive the congratulations of the spick and span,
well-refreshed leader. “Do you mean to say,”
began Abbott, pointing a finger at the Chair,
“ that you’ve been going on this way all night ?
It’s a disgrace to Parliament and to the Chair.
Why didn’t you shut these bores up ? Why didn’t
you turn them out ? Let the Government have
their vote. I’ll be no party to this senseless, silly,
obstructive lunacy.” Whereat the bores who had
been all night saying “ Wait till Abbott comes,”
melted disconsolately away.

STRANGER (to old hand on the river—at pub.): “Did you know one Ryan on the

“ My definition of Protection is feeding a
dog with his tail.” Dobson at the Convention.
Dobson did not attempt to define Freetrade, but
as it appears in Australia, it is a policy of the dog
lending his tail to feed another fellow.
G. H. Reid waited until all the big
speeches on Barton’s federal resolutions had been
delivered before chipping in. Then he carefully
picked his way, steered a safe middle course,
generally usurped the functions of the leader, and
thus took the wind out of Barton’s sails.
THE X RAYS.
N.S.W. Commissioner Eddy is decidedly
in favor of Federal railway management, and
Mathieson, of Vic., and Pendleton, of S.A., are
reputedly of the same mind. If these three ex-
perts hold that Federal management is not only
feasible but advisable, it knocks most of the stuffing
out of the provincial argument.
Kingston undoubtedly made a bad mistake
in taking the chairmanship of the Convention. A
chairman is really a man who keeps order —a
superior kind of chucker-out, and, in the absence
of anybody to chuck, Kingston is merely a mute
on a cushion, without even a chance to make pos-
terity remember that he was there at all.
Graham
Berry’s experi-

chair has sadly
silenced him.

never inter-

Cockbn rn,
Wise, Gordon,
and O’Connor
are the inter-
jectors in

In the Hawkesbury district a gang of 15
sleeper-getters gave as a reason for not voting at
the Federal election—“ That they could not find
out who was for Protection or Freetrade.” To
the average bushee an election means Protection
or Freetrade, Protection implying more roads,
Freetrade more bridges. Protection subscribed to
the local cricket-club; Freetrade opened the
flower-show; also the tariff is mixed 'up with
births, deaths and marriages, long beers and seed-
potatoes. We want none of this new-fangled
Federation here. Give us the old tariff cries and
we knows where we are.
Reported that a vote of £5OO is to be pro-
posed to cover Premier Reid’s travelling expenses
to England. If so, then the curses which Reid
heaped on Dibbs’s for his ex.’s to
England will come home to roost in real earnest.
By the way, it now appears that the £lOOO drawn
from N.S.W. Treasury to pay Dibbs’s touring ex-
penses was refunded to the State by a faithful
and enthusiastic Ministerial colleague of the Pre-
mier’s, out of his own pocket, on the understand-
ing that certain other persons would share the
expense with him ; but, up to now, the Minister
in question has only been reimbursed about half
the amount.
The Federal Convention is now racing its
business through in red-hot haste, so that it may
get finished in time to let the Premiers go away to
London and crawl. If the committees had leisure
to consider the hard knots of the case at length,
and dine and sleep over them, they might find a
way out of the worst difficulties. But for want of
time they go in for long sittings, and grow tired
and acrimonious, and when they come across a
very hard difficulty, like the railway problem, the
decision is : “ We haven’t time ; let’s throw it up.”
. The biggest question in Australia’s history is
being rushed through as if it were a Windeyer
trial and a Windeyer hanging, merely that the
boss patriots may get away to dab their heads
against the floor m front of a royal fetish. The
irony of it!

Zeal, Syme’s nominee in the Fed. Con-
vention “ Plural voting has always worked
equitably m Victoria. I see no reason why it
should not be adopted by the Convention.”
Last Friday the judicial committee of the
Convention arrived at a notable decision. There
is to be a Federal Court of Appeal, and except on
imitters of State the appeal to the Privy Council
will be abolished. The weird tomfoolery of send-
ing an Australian Judge to England to hear Aus-
tralian cases, and sending the plaintiff and the
defendant and the ca,se there after him, instead of
letting him decide it on the spot, is to be wiped
out, and to all appearance S. A. Chief Justice Way
may as well throw up his new billet.
All the infuriated loyalists of Australia
are rising to protest against the Convention pro-
posal to call this country the Commonwealth of
Australia. ‘Dominion ” is the title that the loyal
crowd specially, hankers after. The Bulletin likes
the term Commonwealth as having a more demo-
cratic sound, but still a name is only a name, after
all. If the road-and-bridge politicians can bring
about an equitable arrangement for consolidating
the railways and the public debts, this paper will
as a fair equivalent, be willing to call Australia a
Dominion ” for the rest of its life. And if they
will get over the trouble about the Federal capital
and stop borrowing, and create a really prosperous
sinking fund, and start a comprehensive irrigation
system, it would almost be willing to let them call
this country Hogsflesh if it would make them feel
any better. No British prince could surely want
to come out here and be Governor-General of
Hogsflesh.
The committees appointed by the Federal
Convention. suggest that the members were
shaken up in a bag and dropped out promiscu-
ously. The committee which draws up the Con-
stitution contains most of the strong men, such as
Barton, Carruthers, O’Connor, Baker, Cockburn,
Gordon, Deakin, Isaacs, and Trenwith. The
other strong men are mostly put on the committee
which is to arrange the judicial system. The
financial question, which is the great stumbling-
block and the biggest mystery of the lot, is handed
over mostly to the dull nonentities. Walker,
of N.S.W., who knows finance and probably
nothing else, is on the judicial committee, while
the mildly incapable Brunker is on the financial
one. Out of the fifteen financial men only four—
M'Millan, Holder, Fysh, and Graham Berry—are
even suspected of knowing anything about the
subject. The judicial question is a matter which
specially demands gravity and seriousness, there-
fore Peacock and his awful guffaw are on it to be
in keeping with the natural unfitness of things.
The naval authorities are completely non-
plussed at t.ic conduct of the alleged defaulter,
Assistant-Paymaster Craddock, who absconded
from Sydney with, as it would appear, <£2400 of
his ship’s cash, as he had a splendid record. He
was a teetotaller, and apparently had no extra-
vagant habits. He had also been a prominent
figure in religious circles, and during his three
years on the Sydney station frequently joined in
religions services at the Y.M.C.A.* and also
lectured at the hall of that body upon temperance.


British Enterprise in
Australia .
The surprising expansion of British trade
during this century, of which we are
reading so much in connection with the
forthcoming celebration of the Queen’s
long and eventful reign, is, of course, to
be attributed to the intrepidity and
enterprise of British colonisation, which
has expanded the roots of one people to
the four quarters of the globe. But this
is not by any means the only circumstance
which has conduced to the present
supremacy of British trade. The Norse-
man of olden time spread his white wings
to the Baltic breeze and both explored
and exploited his neighbors far and near,
but with no lasting success. The heroism
which prompts nations to brave the
unknown is generally regarded as magni-
ficent, but seldom leads to any permanent
commercial success. To this, however,
British colonisation offers a striking
THE SALE ROOM.
exception. Naval heroism and intrepid
exploration have been supported by
trade-organisation of the most solid
character, and British
commerce has been
pushed out continu-
ously in perpetual
accord with the trade
developments at
home always ad-
vancing always
s preading—always
ambitious to touch
heights which are not
even in view in the
immediate present.
A case in point is
afforded by an impor-
tant enterprise now
flourishing in Melb.
Shepherd and Co.
Limited, is a mercan-
tile company estab-
lished in London a
year ago for the pur-

business in Melb.
as a direct distri-
buting agency for
British and Conti-
nental firms. The
founders of the com-
pany are Messrs. T.
A, Shepherd & Co.,
of 79 Mark Lane,
London, who have a
high reputation as
export merchants in
many classes of mer-
chandise, but speci-
ally in wines and
spirits. The com-

eludes some of the

the social fireside, in the settler’s hut,
and in the mining camp tent. It seems
to suit all climes and classes, and to be
universally accepted as a reliable type of
the best whisky produced in Scotland.

SALE OF BUSINESS,

In EAGLE STREET, LONGREACH, QUEENSLAND.

market is now offered by T. Cain. A splendid
chance to a pushing man of small capital. T.C.
is now prepared to sell his well-known Drapery

of General Drapery, Boots and Shoes, to the

The Australasian pole-jump record now stands
at lift., put up by a native, Hori Eruera, at Auckland
A.A. sports, dwarfing the previous best, 10ft. Sin., of
Kingsley.
* * * *
The election of secretary for the N.S.W. League
of Wheelmen has resolved itself into a very bitter fight
between the Sydney morning dailies. The Herald’s can-
didate is Fred Hawley, former secretary, and the D.T.
runs secretary Hunter for all the game is worth. So warm
have things become that libel and slander actions are
freely hinted at. Those who know the prevailing cool-
ness between some of the parties cannot help wondering
that the columns of so-called impartial papers are per-
mitted to be their battleground.
The League secretaryship contest is based to
some extent upon the tropical opposition by secretary
Hunter to various schemes brought forward by other
members. On the other hand candidate Hawley’s solitary
denunciation of another wheelman is famous. “ You
know,” he remarked indignantly, “ I think you’re such a
silly little silly.”
* * * *
Ever since a Melb. paper buttered Berry by
alleging that he rode like Zimmerman, that promising
young man has been letting his hair grow to accentuate
the supposed resemblance, until he now looks like a
skye-terrier, or a chrysanthemum, or something to that
effect.
•'* * *
When plain Bill Martin was donkey-licked by
Eelph in his heat for the recent mile championship at
Melb. Exhibition, Yankee Willie let up at the last moment,
and was greeted with cries of “Cocktail!” by an un-
reasonable populace. Willie could get no pace, the other
competitors resisting all the wiles he employed to induce
them to take precedence, until the last lap, when he was
abolished 50yds. from home, and sat back in wrath. The
boo-hoos of the crowd lashed the gentle William to fury,
and he gibbered at them, and showed his teeth, and
finally performed a gesture of contempt not considered
permissible even in the best society. Billiam, naturally
prone to anger, was probably never so anxious for mischief
before, and there was something almost pathetic in the
soulful eagerness with which he looked round for a victim
as he passed to the dressing-room. However, nobody was
taking any, and the slugger probably refreshed himself by
dancing on his wardrobe.

most unlucky place for Porta. I beat men in Melbourne,
Adelaide, and other places ; I come to Sydney and they
beat me. I break my arm once in my life, I break it in
Sydney ; I tink Porta better get tousand mile from Syd-
ney.” And this all in one breath.
» » * *
“R.” strongly demurs to certain “communi-
cated ” statements made in this column re the bad treat-
ment meted out to the erstwhile champion pedestrian
aboriginal, Samuels, by “ his backers.” “ Samuels,” says
“R.,” “ was handled by one person who decided whether
Samuels should train, run, reside in Sydney, or camp at
Jimbour Station. Whatever share of profits was due on
Samuels’ victories his ‘ manager ’ received. If those who
helped to find stakes or risked their money on Samuels’
speed gave him ‘suits of clothes,’ &c., it was outside all
contracts. When cash was given Samuels it was promptly
taken from him by thugs and thieves. When Samuels
became schooled in the ways of the white pedestrian he
acquired a taste for woman and whisky. Then he and his
‘manager’ parted. Up to this time no more honest a
runner ever laced a shoe, and up to his distance no better
runner ever breasted a tape in Australia. Losing the
direction of the only man that could handle him, Samuels
drifted from bar to brothel, and from mia-mia to mad-
house.”
» * * *
A certain prominent all-round cricketer has
been electrifying his native city by going on a prolonged
burst which will perhaps handicap Australia next season.
* * * *
Re the bombast and bickerings of Melbourne
biking boodlers, a barracker for the L.V.W. writes :
“All the first-class racing men, with only one or two
exceptions, are in the employ of the bike-firms, and the
trade will stick to the league like glue.” This, of course,
is mere talking through the neck. Bicycle dealers, with-
out exception, are chiefly interested in getting bold advt.
for their goods, whilst the racing men they “ employ ”
are hungry for a cut from any fat stake that may be
offered. The fat stake which attracts the “champions”
also attracts the public, whose gate-money provides it,
and any race-promoters who can get the use of the best
track in Melbourne (which is, at present, the St. Kilda
track), and guarantee handsome, attractive prizes, offer
the chance of a more or less valuable advt. to the bikes
that carry the principal winning riders. Where is there
any special inducement to “stick to the league like glue ” ?
The general public, without whose patronage no bike-
racing show can be successful, don’t care a curse about
the patronage of the L.V.W. They know that the league
ter-rembled the other week when Billmartin showed his
teeth.
» * * *
The second half (or rather the third and fourth
quarters) of the L.V.W. meeting at Melb. Exhibition
provided precious little entertainment for the public in
general. A lean programme was dragged out to cover
about 61 hours, afternoon and evening, the total amount
of stake money being £225, inclusive of 30 sovs. dis-
sipated by the spendthrift League in two-and-sixpenny
doles for “pacing” the Fifty-mile Championship A
Third-Class scratch race accounted for £l5 of the outlay,
and this exciting event was cut up into eleven heats
(averaging five entries, and fewer starters, per heat), two
semi-finals, and one grand final—l 4 races in all, for prizes
of £B, £5 and £2.
The two-mile Autumn Handicap, with a first
prize of £3O, failed to tempt Parsons, Edwards, Eelph,
Porta, Martin, or Ken Lewis into the arena, but three
scratch men —Megson, Walne, and Green —showed up for
the 6th heat, and in the final run the Englishman was
out-paced, or out-generaled, by his Australian rivals. This
proved to be the one livel y heat of the afternoon.
A noticeable weakness in the tandem racing
was the limit men’s neglect to make much use of their
handicap. In each heat the leaders appeared to wait for
the rearmost tandem to get right up to them, after which
came a slow processional period, terminating in a short
series of sprints. Relph and Walne, starting at the 50-
yards mark, won the final in good style from Carpenter
and March, who had previously beaten the time (6min.
41sec.) by 3sec. in their trial heat. It was a popular
victory, on account of the winners’ pretty costumes among
other reasons.
Green and Edwards, advertised as “ star ” per-
formers in the tandem race, didn’t start, after all. A
cablegram from ’Ome, it was said, had forbidden the
illustrious pair to ride with other than a Simpson chain,
and the Si mpsonic appurtenance wasn’t on the job. Next
in public interest to the Britishers came Megson and
Payne, whose machine broke down in the first lap of their
heat. Bilmartin and J. Carpenter figured only on the
scratching-board, whilst Porta and Boidi figured nowhere,
save in the programme-book. The scratching of the
Italian tandem was not stated on the board. So much
for the League’s management of cycling sports.
The bright star of the meeting was Megson,
whose performances even beat his record of three years
back. Mile-champion of Australasia and 50-mile cham-
pion of Vic., the horse-faced Joe returns to Sydney
cycling hero of the day.
Second only to Megson comes Tebbutt, the
Hawthorn lad, who was practically unknown before last
Foundation Day. He was nothing more than a
name until the '96 Austral, when he won his
heat so well as to be much fancied for the final; but
therein he finished with the field and fell back into
oblivion. At A.N.A. sports on Foundation Day, Tebbutt
started for the £’3so two-mile handicap from 210 yds., and
won easily. At Beechworth he was brought back to
140 yds. in the same distance, and won. Reduced to
70 yds., he took the Federal Handicap at the first day of
the League’s meeting, and on Saturday last, brought
back to 40 yds., the same mark as Payne, he beat that
representative Australian cyclist in the run home of the
Autumn Handicap by a couple of lengths; but he fell
close to the start of the final of this event. In the 50-
Mile Championship he made a fine half-lap battle with Meg-
son, pacing the champion from the rear in great style, and
only finishing at the axle of his front wheel. Body, the
ex-Maorilander, was responsible for the major part of the
public interest in the big race—he kept the pace a cracker
throughout, and led for 94 laps out of the 250.
The Autumn Handicap final easily fell to young
J. A. M‘lnnes (190yds.), the S. Melb. lad who has always
been going to win something big for the last three years.
Walne and Walker started from scr., but after Tebbutt
(on 40yds.) fell, Walker was left to pace himself up, which
he did in style. Don Walker is riding now better than
he tver did before.
Both days of the meeting, the bookies yelled
the odds as openly as at Flemington. The League tried
to get the betting stopped, but the police pleaded that
they had no power.
The handsome jaw of Bilmartin appeared not
on the track either day or night, its owner being vaguely
understood to be ill somewhere. It is rumored that, like
Samson of old, Bill has had his hair cut.
A touching instance of unselfishness was ob-
served during the progress of the 50-mile race, when
Meg'son handed a famishing rider at his side half the
chewing-gum he had been munching consistently since
the start.
* • * *
Who edits the L.V.W. programmes, anyway
J- A. M‘lnnes, the lightning boy, flashed home first by Tit
lengths for a heat of the Autumn Handicap. Little Mac’s
name was not listed anywhere among the entries, conse-
quently the industrious gentlemen who promote silver
“ sweeps ” among the crowd, charging about 20 per cent,
commission for their trouble in the ordinary way, must
have had a skinner this time. Nobody drew the winning
number.
* * * *
X.Z. : “The Syracuse Cycle Co., of U.S.A.,
has sacked its racing-teams and put on 30 travellers
instead,' each with his own district to work. A good
machine will sell itself by making every rider of it an un-
conscious canvasser. A million “ records ” won’t main-
tain a continuous market for a bad article. Moral :
Record-teams are a bad spec.”
* * * *
“ Emus out back,” writes “fix 8,” “ are greatly
puzzled by bicycles. They will follow a cyclist for miles
across a plain, and if the rider stops come with out-
stretched necks and eyes filled with curiosity right close
up to him.”
* • * *
The civilising influence of the bike : all boxing-
news now comes from the U.S., London, and Johannes-
berg. In this country, boxing-matches are as rare as
clever Postmasters-General, and the heavy-jawed slugger
has given place to the brawny-legged plugger.
* * * *
The law relating to art-unions is more stringent
in M.L. than elsewhere. Suburban brick houses, and
potato-patches, and go-carts and things don’t come under
“ works of art.” Articles offered must be artistic, or no
permit. Yet bikes are “art-unioned” every week in
conjunction with “ daubs.” The difficulty is got over by
painting flowers, flies, frogs—anything, on the wheels,
thus bringing them under the standard. Cute and
curious.
* * * *
The Beeston-Humber bicycle will keep in front
through sheer merit. At the last night’s meeting of the
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League every event on the pro-
gramme was won by a Beeston-Humber, and Megson’s
recent performances show what a champion the popular
Australian is when mounted on a champion machine.
Payne, too, has done remarkably well since he mounted a
Beeston-Humber. *

The will of an eccentric Sydney man,
settled in both criminal and civil jurisdiction of
N.S.W. Supreme Court nearly a quarter of a cen-
tury ago, is again on the tapis. It is now said
that the “ rightful owners ” are on hand, and that
big disclosures are imminent.
Some Convention items. Adye Douglas
(Tas.) is the oldest delegate, 82 years. Then come
Moore (Tas.) 74, and Graham Berry (Vic.) 75.
Twenty of the fifty delegates are Australian-born,
most of the others were born in Great Britain,
three in Ireland. No delegate was born out of
the British dominions and no delegate bears a
foreign name except Piesse, a Westralian repre-
sentative. Twenty-five delegates are lawyers—
N.S.W. six, S.A. six, Victoria five, Tasmania
four, Westralia four. Six N.S.W. delegates were
born in that province, and a seventh, Lyne, in
Tasmania. Victoria has five natives, S.A. four.
Six Tasmanians were born in England, a seventh
in Ireland, and three are natives.
John Norton’s luck sticks to him. Ar-
raigned last week for having, in Sydney Truth,
described William Freeman Kitchen as a “biga-
mist and wife-deserter,” defendant pleaded “not
guilty a plea which, if raised in a civil action
for libel, would mean practically anything, barring
justification; but which, when raised in a criminal
prosecution, signified nothing but a bald denial of
haying done what was imputed. And, as it was
quite clear that the pugnacious Norton had done
what he was charged with, the judge said he
couldn’t see what the jury was disagreeing about.
The fact was, however, that the jury saw that
Norton’s plea technically prevented him from
raising his real defence and offered a bar to the
merits of the case being enquired into ; not being
lawyers they couldn’t see why all the facts and
gossip concerning Mr. Kitchen’s matrimonial
affairs should not be placed before them so that
they could have a good yarn about the matter and
do lay justice between Jacky and his friend Kit-
chen. When the jury was discharged, after hope-
less disagreement, defendant promised, if re-tried,
to plead justification.
The drawing of prizes in the annual
Druids’ Gala is fixed for 24th April. Tickets are
only Is. each, and the first prize is a golden Druid,
valued <£looo. A Druids’ Art Union Concert, at
Sydney Town Hall, is announced for the night of
Easter Tuesday, the proceeds to go to the Indian
Famine Relief Fund. The latter item is only
mentioned casually; the real thrill of this para-
graph lies in the fact that tickets are only Is. and
the first prize is <£looo.

purchase and treat, in large or small quantities, all classes
of Gold, Silver, Lead, Copper, and other Ores.

INVALIDS
AND
The aged.
Vic. J.P.-dom is doing its worst to nullify
the Factories Act by dismissing cases for breach
wherever there is the slightest excuse, and by im-
posing the lightest fines when charges are proved.
New wine in old bottles. The grocer who adul-
terates his coffee is liable, the magistrates who
adulterate the law are not.
Cecil Rhodes, the fallen buccaneer of
South Africa, struck an immortal truth when he
told the British Parliamentary Commission that
the blessed Hempire accepted thankfully and
without question all the bits of territory he had
successfully grabbed by shady processes, and only
assumed a shocked Christian attitude when he
grabbed at the Transvaal, and failed. If Jame-
son’s raiders had overset the Kruger republic,
Cecil Rhodes would probably have been made a
duke. As he was overset himself, Rhodes is
hanging about on the verge of a gaol.
In Boucicault’s dramas the Irishman is
usually a fine fellow who doesn’t pay his rint, and
the Englishman is a hard-hearted savage who
turns him out of the house. Irish contempt for
the inflexible property-owner has become such a
popular article of faith that one is shocked to find
Michael Walsh, at Carlton (Melb.) court, screw-
ing rint from Alfred Pearce by means of a fraud
summons. Pearce failed to comply with an order
of the court made in December, for payment of £6
odd. His wife had been confined in the meantime,
he said, also his earnings had come down to 17s.
per week, therefore he sought temporary exemp-
tion from a settlement of Michael Walsh’s claim.
The result was not in accordance with melodrama
doctrines. Two magistrates (one of them named
Foley, begorra !) “made an order for the im-
mediate payment of the amount, with 335. costs,
in default 14 days imprisonment. The defendant
asked for time to pay, but this was refused. The
decision created astonishment in court.”
Ed. Bulletin, —The Argus (13th March,
’97) on sealed pattern riflemen :
In the regiment there has been the keenest interest
over the selection, and it is an open secret that members
of the Legislature, public men in other walks of life, and
even ladies, have written urging the merits of particular
men. All such influences Colonel Price ignored, and
picked the men on their build and smartness as they stood
on parade, inquiring from the drill instructors only as to
the attendance at parade and length of service.
Mark carefully ! Not for efficiency, good soldier-
ing, or character (have they defaulter-sheets in
Vic. army ?), but for build and smartness on
parade. Just judged like a bullock, by his points
and beef. That’s how “ Fire-low ” chose them.
Even the breeding and pedigree, supplied by
public men and ladies, would not influence “ Lay-
’em out.” “ It’s —aw —style—aw, I want: not
use. Loyalty and royalty only recognise style ! ”
I thought that “all hair” or “moustache” was
the regulation at home now. But in Victoria we
have the hard and fast ex-convict rule —shave as
you’re bid.
Two of the chosen ones were informed that they would
have to grow a moustache before the date of sailing,
while another was instructed to sacrifice a fine fair beard,
which had been trimmed to the characteristic Blue Jacket
point.
Argus again, trimmed :—
There was little difficulty about a sergeant, however,
for Sergeant Strong, who is a son of Councillor William
Strong, the Mayor of Melbourne, has just the cut of a
smart, well-built rifleman. None of his rivals had a
chance with him.
The writer remembers being asked to select two
volunteers for the Royal Horse Artillery. The
adjutant, old “ Jock ” Everett, came up from the
Dep6t at Canterbury to pass them. The chosen
men were both disqualified, one for his long
defaulter sheet and the other for his small ohest-
measurement. But the others who went were not
picked solely for smartness on one particular
parade nor for their length of service. I suppose
the two unfortunates who have to grow their
moustaches will be dipped. I can recommend
“ phenyle.” Perhaps the whole squad had better
be run through the trough and then they’ll be
physically clean, which is what seems to be wanted.
—Scotty the Wrinkler.
P.S.—Hold on !
Before making a selection Colonel Price explained that
eaoh man would receive £5 for new equipment, £lO each
for pocket money, that the non-commissioned officers and
men would travel second-class and the officers first.
Tommy Atkins with a whole tenner ! “ Sart
M’jah ! what’s that infernal row in the canteen ?
P’oo Sart ! send a file of your men there.”—
S. the W.

FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for tht-se health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Imtnediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branehes are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re- j
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

An Old Crimean Hero’s Story .
SERVED IN 17 SHIPS IN HER
MAJESTY’S NAVY.
INTERESTING NARRATION OF ’53.
FOUGHT AND BLED IN THE QUEEN’S
SERVICE.
From the Australian Military Gazette.
Although it is 44 years since the Crimean War
was fought and nobly won by England, we have
in our midst a veteran who can point with pride
to the fact that he was one of the heroes who
helped to thrash the Russians at that memorable
campaign. The gentleman in question is Mr.
Chas. Terry, of 485 Kent-street, Sydney, who has
been a temperance lecturer in Australia for some
time, having left the navy a few years ago. A
representative of our paper, wishing to find out
more about Mr. Terry, called on Mr. Terry, who
proved to be a chatty old gentleman of 66, and
who readily expressed his willingness to talk about
himself. In response to a few questions, Mr.
Terry stated :—“I joined the Royal Navy about
1853, and served during the Crimea in H.M.S.
Retribution, Captain Thomas Fisher, under
Admiral Dundas, bearing the flag of Rear-
Admiral Baines, and I bear the Crimean decora-
tion. I arrived in Australia at the latter end of
1877 in H.M.S. Sapphire, the Hon. Captain
Merry. I then joined the Diamond, under Cap-
tain Dale, on this station, and on leaving the ship
quitted the service for good, having in all served
in 17 ships in her Majesty’s Royal Navy, during
which time I was employed as Admiral’s, Commo-
dore’s, and Captain’s cook. In August of last
year I found myself at Emu Plains, being in the
employ of Messrs. M'Grath, fellmongers, employed
as cook. It was whilst there that I was taken ill,
towards the latter end of August. I attributed
my illness to exposure in periodically leaving a
hot kitchen to attend to some outside work. My
first symptoms were coughing, a spitting of blood,
and a bloody discharge from the kidneys of the
consistency of jelly. One man there was particu-
larly kind to me, and constantly stayed up with
me all night, giving me eucalyptus to inhale, and
continuously rubbing it into my chest, throat, and
stomach. This man kept constantly telling me to
clear out from Emu Plains and go to hospital,
otherwise, to use his own words, he said, ‘ You
will peg out.’ Feeling that he was likely to prove
a true prophet, I followed his advice, came to
Sydney, and took up my abode at 485 Kent-street,
where I still am living. I should, however,
mention that both Mr. and Mrs. M'Grath gave
me the highest of references, and regretted that
my bad health would not allow me to remain.
(These recommendations, which were of a most
satisfactory nature, he showed our representative.)
On arrival in Sydney I suffered from great pains
in the back and sides, and also under the ribs.
I could take no solid food, and entirely subsisted
on eggs and new milk, whieh my landlady pro-
cured for me. I went to see the Government
medical officer at Circular Quay, and was so
horribly weak that it took me an hour and ten
minutes to get there. He told me to strip, which
I did, and he then sounded me and asked me how
long I had been bad. I told him that from the
first it was about eight weeks, upon which he said,
‘ Get away man ; it’s chronic.’ He could not send
me to Little Bay, but suggested that I should go
to Liverpool Asylum at once, but I refused to go
there, and returned home. I then went to see the
Grand Chief Templar. I also saw ’his wife and
the Grand Lodge Secretary, and told them of my
pitiable state. With the assistance of Brother
J. C. Jones, late District Deputy of the Good
Templars, and the Grand Lodge Secretary, I had
medicine prescribed for me of various kinds, which
proved quite ineffectual, and afforded me no relief
whatever. I took no less than seven bottles of
eucalyptus, and even chlorodyne was prescribed
for me. I fell away lamentably, perspired pro-
fusely, and could not obtain any sleep. I was in
such pain that I could not shift in bed. My right
side was much the worst, and my breathing was
most labored and painful. I cannot be too grate-
ful to my friend, Brother J. C. Jones. He would
stay with me for three or four hours at a time, and
it was he that saved me from going to asylum.
One day a friend of mine read to me the account
of Boilermaker Jarvis’ wonderful recovery by
taking Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I at once
procured a box. I had taken all but two, when
the blood-spitting and emission ceased. After
two boxes I was able to get up and move about,
and now, at the end of six boxes, feel almost well,
with the exception of feeling very weak. The
cure was MAGICAL, and I tell all my friends
and the general public that nothing but Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pills brought me once more before them to
agitate the temperance question, and on each
occasion I have met with a great reception. (Mr.
Terry here called our reporter’s attention to the
amount of useless physic he had consumed, and,
indeed, the amount of bottles reminded one of the
interior of a chemist’s shop.) lean speak in the
highest possible terms of the marvellous curative
properties of the Pink Pills. On my visits to and
lectures in the country I shall consider it my duty
to suffering humanity to recount my astounding
cure, and I trust I may be the means of saving the
the life of many a despairing mortal.—CHAS.
TERRY.”
(Signed on oath and in the presence of Charles
Lovely, J.P.)
This great remedy enriches and purifies the
blood, strengthens the nerves, and in this way
goes to the root of disease, driving it from the
system, and curing when other remedies failed.
Every box of the genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills has the trade mark on the wrapper around
the box, and the purchaser can protect himself
from imposition by refusing all others. Obtain-
able from all dealers, or the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Company, Sydney, will forward, post paid,
on receipt of stamps or post order, one box for
2s 9d or half dozen for 14s 9d.

chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken

and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney.’’

According to Guerin, the celebrated expert
in caligraphy, the anonymous letter-writer who
avails himself of-the services of a typewriting
machine as a disguise, is an ass. No two machines
print exactly alike, and a magnifying-glass clearly
reveals the idiosyncrasies of each machine.
