A theatrical incident of the recent Con-
vention campaign, in Melbourne, was supplied
by those tricky old legal showmen, Gaunson and
Purves, Q.C, Gaunson, addressing the electors
at a suburban town-hall, remarked the presence
on the platform of a gentleman with whom he
had for long not been on speaking terms —“a
gentleman whose splendid efforts in the Federal
cause should not be forgotten,’’&c. Whereupon
Purves, the gent, in question, shook Gaunson by
the hand, and the tableau (entitled “Reconcilia-
tion ”) was greeted with immense cheering. Yet
neither got in.
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in-
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection —
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
The beastly uncertainty of marriage again !
In Sydney Divorce-court the other day a lady
told this story: In her guileless youth she
married a man whose name did not transpire, but
who may be called Blank to economise pronouns,
and who described himself as a widower with two
children. After a while she discovered, or thought
she did, that his first wife was still alive, where-
upon she didn’t prosecute him for bigamy, or get
her marriage formally annulled, but simply left him.
Later she married Ryan, on which occasion she
called herself a widow. “ I wasn’t a spinster, and
I wasn’t a married woman and I wasn’t a widow,”
she said ; “ there was no word in the English lan-
guage to describe my condition. So I called my-
self a widow.” The next trouble was that the lady
wanted a divorce from Ryan on several grounds.
Whereupon Judge Simpson became interested,
and ordered that an inquiry should be made
as to whether the first husband really was
a bigamist, and whether the second hus-
band had incited the lady to falsely de-
scribe herself as a widow. And if it happens
to transpire in the course of the investigation that
the. first husband's first wife had a previous hus-
band living, so that the first husband was in
reality not married to her, but was legally attached
to his second wife, and consequently that the
latter wasn’t legally married to her second hus-
band and so doesn’t require a divorce ; or, if it
chances that the second husband of the first hus-
band’s second wife had also a first wife alive, or
some other interesting complication of the sort
arises, it will be interesting to know what Judge
Simpson proposes to do about it. Anyhow, it is a
rash thing for any Judge who wants peace and
quiet to dig up the relationships of such a badly-
complicated family. Almost anything might
turn up.
“ Timi Piripi ” obituarises a Maori chief
for The Bulletin
A Maoriland cable announces the death of Wiremu
Hoterene Taipari, a well-known chief of the Ngatitamatera
tribe, Thames River district. Taipari was chief owner of
the eastern littoral of Hauraki Gulf, and was a potent
factor in the throwing open of the Thames goldfield; The
dead chief was an excellent specimen of the semi-civilised
Maori. He dressed in the height of the prevailing Euro-
pean fashion, and his wooden mansion stood proudly
amidst the raupo whares of his fellow-tribesmen ; but he
was in many things a Maori at heart. Some twenty years
ago, finding that the art of carving had been lost by his
tribe, he imported an entire hapu of natives from
YVhakatane on the East Coast, and caused them to
erect a highly ornate meeting-house on his private
grounds, so that the Ngatitamatera might have before
them an object-lesson in the art of their fathers. Taipari
was instrumental in raising a corps of Maori volunteers,
and very smart they looked in their serge uniforms,
French-peaked caps, with Captain Taipari in command.
The deceased chieftain’s wife, jyferi Taipari, introduced the
first thoroughbred stallion into the Thames district, a
speedy animal named Potentate. At one race-meeting at
which Potentate competed, the fair owner arrived on the
course accompanied by two tohungas, or wizards, who
caused immense amusement by performing an incantation
from the grandstand which, it was confidently believed,
would render Potentate still fleeter of foot and stouter of
heart. Every Maori on the course had wagered on Meri’s
horse, and, when it was beaten by a short head, they
raised the Ngatitamatera war-cry, and, headed by the
indignant Meri, pursued the unfortunate jockey and
would have mauled him severely but for the intervention
of a strong force of police. But those roaring days are
past, and poor old Taipari has gone to his fathers, and
his place well be taken by descendants who have gradu-
ated at pakeha universities, and whose claims to gentility
are based upon considerations quite apart from deftness
in the use of the taiaha or a capacity for dying with an
arched back-bone and a face that blanched not to meet
the shades beyond the Cape of Spirits.

Some criminologists consider the. practice of tat-
tooing characteristic of the criminal type. But
it is also a characteristic of seamen—whose
criminal average is only ordinary. The truth
seems to be that anybody will start tattooing as a
pastime if there is nothing better to do. Tattooed
people are idle people, and idleness leads to crime.
Travelling along the Flinders, a little
while ago, writer stayed for one night at an
ancient-looking pub. On seeking his horse in the
morning, he stumbled across a whole cemetery
belonging to the place, conveniently situated be-
hind a bit of rising ground which hid it from view
of the road. A grim tale of the quality of the
fire-water dispensed of yore at that hostelry in r
turn for lambed-down shearers’ cheques.
There are said to he no old maids in
Russia. Public sentiment is so decidedly against
celibacy that when a woman reaches the age of
and still pines in single blessedness, she finds it
best to go off on a journey in search of a husband.
After some length of time she returns and an-
nounces herself a widow. Russian etiquette for-
bids any allusion to a deceased husband m the
presence of the widow, and is very convenient for
these ladies.


A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb.', a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

Some Sporting Topics.
One “Harold Benson ” has gone into the sweep
business in Tasmania as a rival of “Tatt.,” and issues a
circular which is full of good solid reason why no one
should trust him with a copper. Benson promises all
prizes and no blanks. He wants 50,000 subscribers to
send along 10s. each, and in return he promises three
first prizes of £BOOO, £3OOO, and £1000; £2OOO to be
divided among starters and non-starters ; 20 prizes of £lOO
each ; 40 of £5O each ; 100 of £lO, and 200 of £5, and a
“ good dividend” to all the other 49,400, or so, subscribers.
The “ good dividend ” is to be made up thus : the prizes
above-mentioned represent £20,000 out of the £25,000
subscribed, and with the other £5OOO Mr. Benson pro-
poses to back horses successfully on a large scale and win
enough to give a round sum to everybody. If the “ good
dividend ” amounts to even 55., whieh would be pretty
miserable, Mr. Benson has to win over £12,000 to pay it
with, therefore he has to win more than twice the capital
he starts with. If he can win £12,000 in this off-hand
manner it is difficult to see why he should struggle with
the sweep at all; it only yields £2500, and expenses
have to come out of that, so he probably
won’t have quite £lOOO for himself, and why a
man with such a valuable knack of winning big
sums fpr other people doesn’t throw up this very un-
profitable business and win the big sums for himself in-
stead, passes comprehension. Benson claims to have
special knowledge which will enable him to scoop large
sums for his clients with certainty, but as The Bulletin
never heard of him before this rests mainly on his own
authority. Considering the present impoverished state
of the Bing, however, it is dead certain that if Benson
wins a very good dividend for his clients he won’t be able
to collect it on settling day. The Ring hasn’t enough
money to stand having good dividends taken out of it by
any plunging philanthropist for the benefit of 49,400
clients. The writer knows nothing against or in favor of
Mr. Benson personally, but his circular has the misfor-
tune to look like an impudent attempt to take in the
great Australian juggins and loot him.
The “ private composition ” maggot has eaten
• nto every branch of Smellbourne society. The L.V.W.
’was recently requested to take over a debt of £2l con-
tracted by the Vie. Racing Cyclists’ Association, prior to
incorporation with the League. Seemingly it was a fair
request, and the League felt anxious to keep the financial
honor of professional biking management spotless before
the world. “ Mr. Salter moved, and Mr. Comber seconded,
that the matter should be referred to the executive com-
mittee, with power to compromise the debt if they thought
fit. Carried.”
* » * »
The chief day of the year at Hobart is regatta-
day, and to make the affair a success the committee goes
round town collecting subscriptions from citizens. One
of the committee of last regatta entered an antiquated
little boot-shop and addressed a venerable old man behind
the counter. “Can you give me a subscription for the
regatta?” asked the committee-man. “Eh ! what 1” was
the acrimonious answer. The visitor repeated his ques-
tion. “ Regatta,” said the man of leather—” did Christ
ever go to a regatta?” Somewhat flabbergasted, the
com.-man answered, “Perhaps not.” “Well, out you
so.” said the bootmaker. How very like Hobart!
• • * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Be Torrens Creek (N. Q.) horses
living on boiled meat from the local meat-works, Edward
Carpenter, in his delightful book, “ Civilisation: Its
Cause and Cure,” has a chapter on Custom, in which he
argues that a wolf of an enquiring mind would find peas
and strawberries nourishing enough. He instances cases
where dogs have made themselves nuisances in the garden
once they have acquired the habit of picking gooseherries,
&c., for home consumption. “Cats can be bred up to
live on oatmeal and milk with next to no meat; and a
sheep has been known to get on very comfortably on a
diet of port wine and mutton chops.” The present writer
knows of a Hawthorn (Vic.) pony which, against the en-
treaties and pulling of its child-rider, burglariously
entered a butcher’s premises and stole and ate a string of
sausages.—Huffy.
* * * *
The proprietor of a well-known English weekly
thief-paper, who has just got a divorce from his wife,
ascribes her downfall from the paths of virtue to her
having taken to cycling and met a man-cyclist who told
her her legs were “ a dream.” As the thief-paper man
had evidently never told her this she went off with the
other fellow.
• • • •
Yol. A. Ballinger, the M.L. shooting-champion
this year, is a slight and wiry man, aged 36 ; he is a tee-
totaller and non-smoker. Shooting has been his lifelong
hobby. In ’7B, he finished within 5 points of his brother,
W. H. Ballinger, for the championship ; was fifth in ’BB,
seventh in ’9O, fifth in ’92, and eventually won in ’93.
Next year he tied his brother for the coveted honor, and
this year put up a record in points. The Ballinger family
think of little else than bullets and bulls-eyes, and their
steadiness and proficiency furnish an object-lesson to
aspiring shootists.
The ladies’ matches, won by the Misses Whit-
ney and K. Gittos, were a feature of the late M.L. rifle
championship meeting. Of the 17 entrants, some shot
excellently, but others couldn’t fire for sour apples—one
actually hit the wrong target. One lady shot from her
back.

Bicycle horror from England. At Folkestone
the main road runs, in one place, down a long, steep hill,
at the bottom of which is an old-fashioned toll-bar. A
gate extends right across the road, and the driver or rider
stops and hollers till the toll-keeper comes out, and, hav-
ing been appeased by the monetary consideration pro-
vided in the bye-laws, opens the gate and lets them
through. A cyclist was coming down this hill recently
when his brake gave way, and he negotiated the
declivity at a fearful pace, yelling as he came to the toll-
keeper to open the barrier. The keeper didn’t hear him;
there was a crash ; and the cyclist was projected right
through the toll-house window, where he landed, minus
both his ears, on the dinner-table. The broken glass had
sawed the ears off in his hurried progress through the
window. But even this isn’t a much worse horror than
the N.S.W. affair in which a cyclist, whose bicycle had
bolted with him down-hill on to a barred bridge, was
thrown over the barrier and landed on his face. When
he got up he thought he had a big splinter in his nose,
and proceeded to pull it out and throw it away. Then he
found he had thrown away his nose.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, — Nicol’s patent starting ap-
paratus, mentioned in a recent issue, was tried in two
heats and the final of a race at Brunswick (Melb.)
bicycle sport, the other day. This apparatus has no con-
nection with the starting barrier known to horsey men ;
it is electrical and simple—strikingly simple—and, if the
conservative instincts of bike handicappers can be over-
come, should presently be found on every racing-track in
Australia. Young Nieol was ill-advised in selecting
Brunswick for an exhibition performance of his “ clock.”
There the democracy has the freedom of the path, and
the judge’s stand, and the place generally, and its
anxiety to boss the job militated somewhat against a satis-
factory exhibition. Sufficient was done, however, to
show the utility of the concern with proper sports
management and disqualification for the rider who lets
go before the machine gives him his signal. The ap-
paratus can’t lie, and that accurate handicapping can be
assured under its regime was fully demonstrated on
Saturday. Its chief advantage (and it has several) over
the present distance-handicapping system lies in the
fact that with its assistance an absolutely equitable handi-
cap becomes only a question of the ability or honesty of
the handicappers. At present the fairness of handicap-
ping depends mainly on wind and weather.—S.S.
As a bike-praiser, Dr. Philip Muskett, of
. Sydney, “ knocks ” even the most enterprising Yankee
agent. “ I am a passionate believer in the wheel . . . the
unrivalled exercise of cycling . . . makes thin men fatter
and fat men thinner . . . men who don’t begin until
they are 70 derive much benefit from it . . . cycling
cures indigestion . . . cures stomach catarrh, stomach-
dilation, stomach-neuralgia . . . rejuvenates men who
are physiologically older than their years . . . often cures
insomnia after every known soporifio in the whole phar-
macoposia has utterly failed ... it keeps down uric acid
in the system . . cures anaemia in women and 90 per
cent, of the functional ailments arising from ennui . . .
the cycle is a revolving panacea.” What pill-advertiser
could be more comprehensive ? Dr. Muskett quotes Dr.
Turner, the well-known British athlete-doctor, on the
connection between varicose veins and cycling. Turner’s
experience is that “ he has never seen the slightest in-
crease in any varicose conditions which could be ascribed
to riding.” Varicose veins, all the same, are more common
among cyclists than they are among other athletes ;
Zimmerman, the Carbine of cyclists, suffered greatly from
the trouble. Writer has heard from the lips of three dr
four top-notch cyclists that they were free from varicose
veins until they went in for track-racing. Of course,
racing isn’t ordinary cycling.

A Maori trotting yarn. He bought a neddy,
changed its name from English to the Maori equivalent,
won a race in an adjoining district and got passed out.
“Why you qualify me?” asked the indignant brown
brother. “Myhosshe have same name all the time.”
Ingenious Maori logic, but not racing law.
• * * *
At Nanango (Q.) Land Court, the other day, a
witness deposed that he lodged applications (under the
moral ballot-system in a country where gambling is pro-
hibited) for 42 persons, the money for each being paid in
cheques drawn by his father; that he had an equal in-
terest in each of 22 of those applications, the successful
one of which was either to be “used mutually” or
“ transferred to one of his friends, who was likely to be
one of his own family.” He had made all arrangements
to buy-out the successful applicant. There were 60 ap-
plications altogether for this one ohance, a grazing-farm
of 3000 a.
* * * *
Judge Gudgeon in Canterbury (M.L.) Press
in an article on “The Maori” “It has lately been my
good fortune to come across a very funny notice, which
was posted up on the door of a country hotel. It is so
thoroughly Maori that I did not dare to translate it
myself, lest it should be said that I had drawn on my
imagination ; hence I handed it over to one of the best
of Maori scholars, and his translation is as follows : ‘ Let
all men know that Christmas will be celebrated, and a
race meeting held, at Te Teko on the 25th Dec. All those
who patronise sports should assemble at that place, where
there will be amusement for us in honor of the new year,
and also to celebrate the advent of our Saviour from the
unknown, in order that He may know that we hold in
reverence and love His birthday, and may in consequence
remember and love us on the Day of Judgment.’ ”

Smith was the crafty possessor of two ponies,
A. and 8., either of them good enough to capture the
14-hand handicap at a meeting which shall be nameless.
Pony A. had the better public form, therefore Smith
decided to run both and win with outsider B. A long
turf experience had taught him to trust nobody save
himself with a stiffening job. On the cold, grey early
morning of the race Smith'sent A. for a ten-mile gallop
over the sandhills, after which he rewarded the animal
with a heavy meal of bran-mash, sufficient to “ stop ” an
elephant. The eventful hour found a crack jockey
mounted on red-hot favorite A., whilst a smart stable-boy
bestrode 8., who was backed by the owner for a fair
stake just before the flag fell. It was Smith’s race from
the distance—all the others out of it. A. and B. came
along together, the boy on the latter riding so shockingly
“ green ” that A. won by a nose. The public threw up
their hats, and Smith ruminated on the cussedness of the
wrong pony in carrying a 141 b. bran-mash penalty first
past the post. Then B’s rider sneaked up to him with the
news that he (the accursed urchin) had carefully allowed
A. to win for his employer’s sake. “I knew by the
betting that your stuff was on the other one,” said the
mannikin. And 8., like the steadfast dissembler that he
is, said “ Good boy 1 ” instead of “ Gawdam !”
The Resourceful Sidney.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The brothers Slaney were shifty as rats.
Mick, after rummaging the earth to its corners
and striking no money, made a sudden and inex-
plicable rise. He was gracious enough to hasten
death by indiscreet drinking, and Peter secured
the remnant —a few hundreds.
“ Lucky for us he pegged out so prompt,” re-
marked the remaining Slaney with the corners of
a grin, “ ’r else ther’d ’ve bin nothing left for us.”
“ Neither ther’ won’t,” said Mrs. Slaney.
Peter had forgotten his creditors, and was rue-
ful. Many of them clamored like the Damned,
whilst the others had given up, swindled and for-
lorn.
“Hell! ”he grunted, “ I never thought o’ ”
But Slaney was shifty, and an expert. He
paused for the development of thought, and then:
“ Course,” he added, “ ther’s a way out. A man
can make ’is ol’ woman rich, if he can’t hisself.’’
Mrs. Slaney, a natty little woman, observed
him with admiration, and wondered aloud why
she couldn’t scheme things out as Slaney did. She
supposed it was her ignorance of legal processes.
“You don’t know th’ game from two-up,” com-
mented the sententious Slaney.
* * * *
A week later Slaney skulked up to me and
tried to borrow sixpence wherewith to buy a drink.
From a hilarious night had proceeded a tremend-
ous morning thirst. Slaney said he had been
temporarily cleaned out —the established lie for
the occasion.
“Get out!” I said. “What’s come of that
legacy?” ,
“Gorne.”
“ I thought the missus had it safe ? ”
“Ow, she’s gorne, too.”
After gulping his beer Slaney detailed that his
most rapacious creditor had misappropriated Mrs.
Slaney, and rendered his financial claim pre-
ferential.
“Th” Missis knows th’ game orlright,” said
' Slaney.
I fancied there was a twinge of grief in his
voice, and invited him to drink again.
“Ow, it ain’t so much th’Missis,” he said, “as
th’ money, ’nd it ain’t so much th’ money as every
blarsted fool thinkern’ I can stuff up when I
carn’t.”
Sydney.

A Nasty Fall.
“ About six years ago, when residing in Hobart, Tasmania,”
said Mrs. Nellie Denver, of Countess Cottage, Double Bay,
Sydney, to a reporter, “I had the misfortune to meet with a
serious accident, which happened in the simplest manuer possible,
as P' will endeavor to relate. On leaving my house by the back
gate the bolt slipped, whilst I had hold of it, which caused me to
fall very heavily, which I subsequently discovered had seriously
injured my spine, although, strange to say, I felt no ill-effects for
about two years after. I then commenced to suffer from great
i It
* ~
weakness and dizziness, and an in-
tolerable feeling of exhaustion ; in
fact, I felt so nervous and uneasy
that I feared to cross the street,
alarmed that I might meet with
some accident from a passing
vehicle. Not at first recognising
the really serious condition to which
I was reduced, I did not seek medical
attendance, but when I at last be-
came aware of my serious condition,
I was persauded by my friends to
go to the Sydney Hospital, where I
remained two months without re-
ceiving any permanent benefit. On
leaving the hospital I again rather
neglected my state of health, and on
applying for re-admission was in-
formed that my case was perfectly
hopeless. Being unfortunately not
overblessed with this world's goods,
I obtained admission to the
.Newington Asylum, where I remained seven months : yet
still I got no better, being unable to swallow any nourishing
food. I then took two courses of electric baths, for which I paid
six guineas, and after them, with the exception of some slight
alleviation of my suffering, I cannot say that they did me much
good : in fact, I could not even walk, so I returned home feeling
very despondent, and imagining that there was no cure in store
for me. One day, on reading the daily paper, I noticed an account
of a marvellous recovery effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and considering that this case (the one of Boiler-
maker Jarvis, suffering fromTloeoinotor ataxia) closely resembled
mine, I determined to make a trial of them, and sent a boy from
the house to buy some for me. I experienced relief after the first
box, and have steadily improved ever since ; my appetite returned
and I ceased to suffer from that terrible feeling of nervousness. I
am now in my seventh box. and can confidently assert that I am
most decidedly improved in health. My friends also notice a
marked improvement in my condition, a&d aau I entirely attribute
to the health-giving properties of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills; in
fact, it must be solely due to them, for I have tried no other
remedy. The proprietors of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are at per-
fect liberty to make whatever use they may think fit of this state-
ment, for I feel deeply grateful for the benefit I have derived from
them.”
Low Spirited and Weak.
Ax Unfortunate Condition fab too Common among the
Women of Canada.
Low spirited and weak is a condition that aptly describes the
condition of far too many women in this country. They are old
in appearance when they should be in the prime of womanhood.
They are martyrs to headaches, easily tired and indisposed to
exertion, and are pale and sallow in complexion. To those who
are thus unfortunately situated the following letter from Mrs.
Daniel Gavey, Gaspe Basin, Que., will point the road to renewed
health. Mrs. (jravey says : — <s For a number of years I have been
more or less of an invalid. I was low spirited, weak, and not able
do very much of the household work. My appetite was bad
and I suffered from headaches, and slightly from asthma. I used
several medicines with no beneficial results, and at last was
advised to try Dr. Williams' Pink Pills. I used them for some
time, and am again enjoying the best of health. My whole system
is toned up, and with the other troubles the asthma has disap-
peared, I consider Pink Pills an invaluable medicine, and recoin-
mend them to the thousands of women who are suffering as* I was/*

A Vain Sacrifice .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He went by the name of Brown, but she
shrewdly guessed that he had adopted that un-
assuming appellation for reasons of his own. He
rented a small cottage in an outlying suburb, and
“ batched ” for himself.
One evening there had been a street row, and,
at the expense of a cut arm and a few scratches,
he had rescued her from the attack of a drunken
bully. Her gratitude led her to bandage his arm
and dress it every day. Then, seeing that he
could not very well manage, she came daily and
performed the household tasks. Gratitude on her
part soon deepened into a passion doglike in its
fidelity. He, on his part, was grateful, and finally
it came about that, in the neighbors’ delicate
phraseology, “she kept house for him.”
bhe kept it well. She was an excellent cook,
and always had the house spotlessly clean. Of his
private affairs she knew nothing, but in her own
mind he was a “toff.” In his bedroom was a
small brass-bound box which she never touched
even in her dusting. No wife could have loved or
served a man more faithfully than this half-taught
daughter of the people.
She did not know his occupation and he never
told her. He was absent all day, but always
spent his evenings at home. Often he would sit
in a silent reverie from which she never attempted
to rouse him. Once, sitting thus, he looked up
and caught her gaze. He passed his hand lightly
over her hair. She seized it, and, kissing it
passionately, rushed out of the room, her eyes full
of tears.
Poor soul! ” he said, lqoking after her. Then,
with a sigh, “What a curse love is!”
» * • •
Then he fell ill and developed typhoid.
It was a serious illness, and under any but her
nursing he probably' would have died. As it was,
it was a long and tedious road that he travelled to
recovery.
His first conscious glance lit on her face as she
knelt at his bedside. “ Dear 1” he said, and feebly
tried to lift her toil-worn hand to his lips. To his
surprise she snatched it away 7 and, rising hastily,
left him. She stood outside in the little kitchen
and wrung her hands. “ God ! ” she cried voice-
lessly, “ what will he say when he knows ?”
• * * *
At last he was well enough to be lifted into
an arm-chair and be propped up among pillows
and blankets. As she busied herself about the
room he watched her. She had seemed to avoid
him since his recovery, and something vague in
her manner troubled him. Often when she had
thought he was asleep she had crouched beside his
bed, and he knew* she had been crying silently.
“ Bessie,” he said, “ bring me that box and the
key off my chain.” She handed it to him. “ Hold
it, please, he said, as she would have turned away 7.
She did so, and watched him as with trembling
fingers that moved her woman’s pity he unlocked
it. There were some papers, and on the top of
them a small canvas bag. He slowly opened the
bag and then gave an exclamation.
“ Yr hat money have you been using ? ” he asked.
Her face faded to an ashy whiteness.
“ Don’t ask, ” she said, piteously, twisting and
untwisting her hands.
“ I do ask,” he answered, and waited patiently.
I borrowed it,” she said at last defiantly, but
wl “L a hopeless ring in her voice that touched him.
“That is not true,” he replied quietly, “you
know no one to borrow from.”
Suddenly she fell at his feet. “Can’t you
guess?” she cried wildly. “I sold everything I
could—then the man wouldn’t wait for his rent
and the doctor said you couldn’t pull through
unless you had all sorts of things. How was Ito
get them ? I never thought you had money in
your box. She waited a little, then with lowered
voice : “ I couldn’t see you die, so I did it.” She
knelt there and the tears trickled slowly through
her hands and fell on the floor.
\ ou don’t mean ?’ ’ he said slowly, and the
disgust in his voice cut her like a whip.
“ Yes,” she answered dully, “ there was nothing
else to do.”
She waited in silence, expecting a torrent of
abuse and half-cowering as from a blow, for she
did not know the ways of “ Mr. Brown’s 1 ’ class.
lhe silence at last became unendurable. “ Won’t
you speak to me ? ” she asked at last in tones whose
misery should have moved him.
“ There is nothing to say,” he replied coldly,
looking past her and turning his head aside
wearily. She rose as if to speak, but after one
glance at his face she stumbled blindly from the
room.
“ Faugh !” he said bitterly, for what she had
done seemed to taint him too.
He mended slowly, but still needed careful
nursing, and, though he hated himself for it, still
he was dependent upon her. He shrank from all
contact, but if he could have guessed the bitter
pain he caused her he would have shown his dis-
taste less.
* * * *
Night after night she spent sleepless, hud-
dling herself on the mean little sofa and striving
in vain for warmth, for all unnecessary things had
been parted with, and she did not consider blan-
kets for herself a necessary.
“He will leave me,” she said to herself, “and I
can’t bear that, God pity me ! ” and she clenched
her hands against her breast as if to still the cruel
pain.
* « * *
Through the evening days she was very
quiet, but she did not linger about him as for-
merly. Then came a morning when he was well
enough to return to his work. He dressed him-
self, putting aside her offer of help, but as he
was going she came to him, with eyes whose
hungry yearning was to haunt him in after years.
“Won’t you say good-bye?” she asked him, and
he was almost softened by the hopeless humility
of the tone.
“ Good-bye,” he said, coldly, but did not take
the proffered hand.
She stood quite still for a little while after he
had gone, then mechanically set the cottage in
order. Over his clothes she lingered with a loving
touch, then folded them away quietly. When all
was finished she knelt by' his bed and, laying her
head on his pillow, wept pitifully like a child.
STARLIGHT.
A record that licks Yankeeland. Joseph
Bond, of near the Glenelg river, has
grown potatoes running 8 to 10 feet long in the
stalk, (variety, “ White Elephant ”). Under his
10ft. stalk were found 55 large potatoes. His
brother, Robert, living about a mile off, grew on a
patch 11 yards each way, 22 wheatsackfuls of good
marketable spuds. That means 73 tons to the
acre, with only sheep manure and mighty little of
that. Robert Bond has also produced parsnips
running 81b. a piece, and 14Jlb. cabbages.
A Cup-day Courtship.
[FOR THE BULELETIN.]
Scene : Brighton Beach ; innumerable couples in
various stages of wooing. Aunt Sallies, swinging-
boats, etc., surrounded by hot and happy crowds.
He and she seated on the sand wearing each other’s
hats, and oblivious of onlookers.
She : “Oh, you, go on away, or I’ll give you sich
a smack in the faaaee.” (The sentence ending in
a prolonged drawl, a shrug of the shoulders, toss
of the head, and droop of the eyelids.)
He (slipping his arm round a waist that
wriggles under his grasp): “ Bit shy ; it’s a fishy
sort that sits a mile away from a good-lookin’
donah, eh ? ”
She : “ You move away, Bill ’Olt.” (Protest
in the form of a stiffening of herself, and shake of
the shoulders.)
He : “ Move away ! Not me. Come on closer,
we’re just made fer one another, eh ? ”
She: “Made fer yow ! Likely! Ellen Jane
Brown’s more your sort, little an’ stumpy, red
’air, an’ ”
He : “ Chuck that, Em’ly. ’Ere, come closer.”
She: “Keep yerself to yerself, Mister ’Olt, I
’ate you.”
He : “ Not you, you’re too ldnd-’earted ; you
wouldn’t ’urt a fly, an’ so why ’d you ’ate a poor
beggar who wants to get a kiss off them there red
lips?”
She : “ You try it on.”
He : “ I’m just goin’ to.”
She : “ You just better.’
He : “I know that.”
She : “ I’ll give you such a clip on the ears if
you touch me.” (Drawl more prolonged than
ever, injured dignity more intense.)
He : “ Good enough, here goes.” (Short struggle
provoking mild interest among neighboring
wooers.)
She (emerging flushed and breathless): “ You
do that agen, Bill ’Olt.”
He : Jes goin’ to—good, ain’t it ? ”
Gradual softening of coy beauty, more than
content at length with Bill’s ardent embrace. Sits
on his knee all the way back to town, in the
crowded, noisy, second-class carriage, and ex-
changes flashes of repartee with other damsels
similarly seated.
M.D.
“The Free Surgery.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ After all,” he remarked, apropos of nothing, as
we sped along in the Melbourne express, “ after
all, it is not a difficult matter to make a fortune.”
I looked up in surprise. The speaker was a
well-dressed, clean-shaven young man, and very
good-looking. His hand was extended in a
friendly way, and he smiled confidently. His
manner was certainly engaging.
“I wish I thought so,” I said in reply to his
observation.
“ Ah, that’s because you let your mind run in a
groove and don’t give ideas a chance. There are
hundreds of ways of making money, but the aver-
age man sees none of them. Fact is, he won’t
look.”
“ Is not capital an essential requisite?” I ven-
tured.
“ Not altogether, sir. To be without capital is
certainly a disadvantage, but that’s how I began,
and I liked it, sir. I would not have been
deprived of the pleasure of making my fortune
no sir, not for the wealth of nations.”
I had come to the conclusion that he was a
very amusing book-canvasser.
“ 1 was always a keen observer,” he proceeded
“ and, to be brief, the first thing of importance
that I noticed in this country was an unmistake
able demand for horrors of every description
The supply was deplorably limited and altogether
inadequate ; and, worst of all, no one was pushing
the business. Why, sir, when a man took a fit in
the street the people would crowd round in hun-
dreds. They were eager for that sort of thing, sir
and I knew there was money in it.”
His voice, which was like the purring of a cat
overcame me to an extent, and, with eyes half’-
elosed, I listened to his beautiful lies, while a
pleasant thrill played up and down my spine.
“I soon formed a scheme,” he said, “which I
laid before an impecunious young doctor of my
acquaintance. He laughed outright at first, but
was a sensible fellow and gave me a hearing’ and
when I get a hearing, sir, I generally carry my
point. It was the etiquette question that baulked
him ; but eventually he accepted my proposal and
we entered into partnership. Three weeks from
that day our Free Surgery opened its doors. Was
that quick work ? ”
He had begun to talk very rapidly.
“ We treated patients free,” heoontinued, “and
charged the public for admission. I managed
everything myself. I had teeth-extractions going
on all day on the left side, epileptic fits on the
right, and a specialty on the stage every hour.
They swarmed to us like bees—the public, I mean
—and we were coining money. Why, sir, I hadVa
gall-stone fished out of a man before 180 people
at half-crown a head. We had to get professional
assistance and larger premises and a man to shovel
the cash into bags and take it to the bank in a
barrow. I managed everything, sir. Well, the
surgery flourished for a year, and then the (trash
came. One of my dentists, a clumsy fellow, had
the misfortune to break a man’s jaw. Fact was
the man wouldn’t scream, and we had to have
screaming. The public wanted it. The authori-
ties came down on us with the whole medical pro-
fession at their backs. They closed us up, but it
was too late, sir, we had made our pile and were
rich men.”
The train stopped with a jolt, and when I had
pulled myself together my companion had alighted
at Goulburn station, and, hat in hand, was bow-
ing to me. I fancied 1 could hear his soft voice
saving, “ Good day, sir. I managed everything.”
CURRA CURRA.
After the Port Darwin cyclone, Chinese
carpenters were paid 12s. to 14s. a day for their
services, and careful they were to insist on the full
allosyance. The story is told that one contractor
was in the habit of paying 6s. a day wages and Bs.
a day commission. On protest being made by
other contractors this genius raised the wages to
10s. a day and pocketed the other 4s.
